UNDER THE GUMTREE
iVT OW that there.is no risk
I of embroiling Australia
1 and 'endangering the
peace of Europe, I
naturally grasp the first oppor-
tunity of explaining the matter.
The Coronation lias become
ancient history ; and, however
King Edward may be vexed, I
h ive myself to consider. I scru-
p dously refrained from promis-
m x secrecy. And it lias become
im lossible to endure longer the
eternal chant : “Did you see
th ’ Coronation?” “What did
yo ' think of the Coronation ?”
“ low was it you did not write
ab ut the Coronation ? ” Quid-
nu or crony, they have no
oth r greeting.
O course, when I knew that
the e was going to be a Corona-
tio i. and that I was travelling
from Australia to write about it, 1 realised thai
there and Wen was the chance of my journalistic
career. “The'Coronation,” I told myself, “will
never occur again ; and if it were to occur, you
will not be there to see. What is your life
but a newspaper that endureth a little time and
then vanisbeth away ? Out of all possible Corona-
tions in iill possible existences in all possible
worlds, there is given to you just this one Corona-
tion to write about. Now, if ever, you are to
compose a jewel of an article five paragraphs long,
that shall on the stretched forefinger” etc., ill
fact.
Yet i did not regard myself as a parasite upon
King Edward. In a temporal way, to be sure, he
is a very great flea indeed T -afid I a very small
one; but then, he could only be crowned, and I
could write about his coronation. Austin Dobson
declares that
All passes. Art alone
Enduring stays to us ;
The
(I forget what; but he meant my coronation
article),
—outlasts tire throne, —
The Coin, Tiberius.
Therefore, considering King Edward sub specie
ceternitatis, as a peg to hang my fame upon, I con-
ceived that he might easily become my debtor.
A.nd —— well, I was satisfied with my oppor-
tunity. I was satisfied with King Edward. He
would not cut- so romantic a figure as the young
King of* Spain, hut I thought he would do. I
thought it would be my own fault if I did not
write my article about him. In a way, indeed, I
was grateful to him. 1 felt that if he knew me,
and if ho knew Australia, and if he knew what
Daley hjp,d taught me about “green fire,” he
would not grudge being crowned.
So I set sail; I arrived ; I found a boarding-
house with a bathroom ; and at five o’clock on my
first Loudon morning, even in the very thrill anc
moment of waking, 1 was admonished of an Omen
and lay expectant.
The next moment I saw My Coronation Article.
It defiled before me like the procession it com-
memorated ; and I understood at once that it was
written symbolically, so that the varying rhythm
and colour of its sentences typified perfectly the
character and movement of the processional items.
That is the peculiar virtue of the Symbol Sibyl:
she decants Eternity into a single instant, and
from one grain of sand decomposes Infinity. So
my Article, as it marched magnificently past,
ceased to be a mere assemblage of words and
images, and became the Coronation Procession
itself. Through the grey streets, between the
hedges of the hustling populace, past the surging
stands it moved, with a noise of men and horses
trampling, and with glimpses of sudden glory that
flooded my brain with scarlet. So complete was
the reproduction that jn the Strand I saw a seat-
contractor standing gloomily beside a row of
benches priced at ten guineas and filled at ten
shillings, and heard what he was going to say to
his wife when he went home. (Unpublishable.)
I remember quite well the style in which my
Article was written. Sometimes it had scin-
tillating facets that suggested G. W. Steevens,
and sometimes there were abrupt dashes of colour
that reminded me of Stephen Crane ; but chiefly
it came sonorous from the soul of that-mighty-
mouthed inventor of harmonies, Thomas De
Quincey. Nothing, so gentle as its fluting in the
piano passages ; and when it rose crescendo I
thought of the voioe of the great Lord Romney
thundering at the close of BeaucJiamp’s ( areer.
It was gay, delicate, audacious a? Max Beerbohm.
It was stern, acrid, undissuadable as Walt Whit-
man. Now it held the singing sweetness of Keats,
and now the reverberant majesty of Marlowe. In
brief,'it was a masterpiece ; and I wondered if it
would do for The Bulletin.
After thart (and this is the kernel of my explana-
tion) —after that—well, after that I lost it.
lor, just as the procession reached the Abbey—
and, of course, in virtue of its symlwlic quality,
it passed in much less time than I have taken to
tell about it—l grieve to say that I lost control of
myself. I knew the importance of holding the
vision like a brimming vase until I reached the
table and my pen; and I never moved,-scarcely
breathed, until we came to the Abbey. I lmd no
ticket for the Abbey ; and my idea was to let the
procession pass inside, and then get gently out of
bed and write while everybody was engaged with
the Archbishop of Canterbury. Then I would
have been ready to take up the vision again when
they ali came out.
Unluckily, just as f was raising myself cau-
tiously on an elbow, I saw Barton, and I cheered,
cheered lustily—" Hooray ! ” and " Hooray ! ”
again. _ I did not cheer Barton; I cheered
Australia. The warren of London and all the
pallid, driven, rabbit-faces disappeared ; and I
was back among brown-necked men in a land of
infinite purple vistas, where the npllion-varied
eucalypts lean one to another to croon seorets
old before Britain was born. And I cheered with
all my heart and soul and strength.
Then I heard alarmed noises in the next bed-
room, and knew that my coronation article was
gone. In a flash, it had vanished utterly. How
I hunted for it ! how L tried to recapture its first,
fine, careful rapture ! I sat; I walked ; I tried
stimulants and the Turkish hath. All vainly.
At some instants, 1 felt very close, very warm ; I
seemed- to know that if 1 could remember the
first- sentence the remainder would follow. That
first sentence ! —it was as unattainable as the song
the Sirens sang.
I have little more to add. When the messenger
came from Buckingham Palace for the copy, I
told him exactly how things stood, and asked for
time. They made some excuse, and postponed
the Coronation. In the end, I had to say that I
had lost hope of recovering my article, and could
not condescend to makeshifts* It was a great
blow, and the Coronation was a very inferior
affair in comparison with what it might have
been.
Of course, I am glad that King Edward is better
now. For myself, lam inconsolable.
Iln Rio Grande 1 s Last . Race, and Other Verses,
A. B. Paterson has collected another series of
rhymes after the Australian’s own heart. These
newly-gathered pieces (many of them already
familiar from newspaper publication, and already
prized) make no higher literary pretensions
than their elder brothers ; but they are equally
li tted to earn the honest, hearty popularity won
by The Man from Snowy River. In hand 'and
spirit “The Banjo” is still the same, and he has
gained greater command of the rhymer’s trade
while preserving his vigor hardly impaired.
As before, we have humour and sentiment alter-
nating, and Paterson develops a strain of piety
which chimes curiously, but not unnaturally, with
the rhetoric of “the Man from Ironbark.”
What will it profit that with tears or laughter
Our watch we keep ?
Beyond it all there lies the Great Hereafter !
Thank God for sleep 1
For, at the last, beseeching Ohrist to save us,
We turn with deep
Heart-felt thanksgiving unto God, who gave us
The Gift of Sleep.
But it is in the old alliance of humour and horse
that Paterson is most successful. No other local
writer can get such twinkling, genial fun from the
play of fancy over four hoofs and a hide—the
I Austral microcosm. And the verse runs with such
a swing, too—as in “ Father Riley’s Horse ”
So they buried Andy Began, and they buried him to
rights,
In the graveyard at the back of Kiley’s Hill;
There were five-and-twenty mourners, who had five-and-
twenty fights,
Till the very boldest fighters had their fill.
There were fifty horses racing from the graveyard to the
pub,
And their riders flogged each other all the while.
And the lashios of the liquor! And the lavins of the
grub ■!
Oh, poor Andy went to rest in proper style !
One eould not be.more graphic.
And indeed Paterson’s talent would be ex-
ceptional anywhere. “A Disqualified Jockey’s
Story,” for example, in one of the metres that
Leland popularised, is a jewel of observation and
description. “The Banjo” has the virtue that
many a historian vainly covets—the virtue of
seeing life and incident with his country men’s
own eyes, and of writing as they would write
if they had his gift of expression. And the
melody is often true and catching: in “Song of
the Artesian Water,” for example—
But it’s hark ! the whistle’s blowing with a wild, exultant
blast,
And the boys are niadly cheering, for they’ve struck the
flow at last,
And it’s rushing up the tubing from four thousand feet
below,
Till it spouts above the casing in a million-gallon flow.
And it's down, deeper down
Oh, it comes from deeper down ;
It is flowing, ever flowing, in a free, unstinted measure
From the silent, hidden places where the old earth hides
her treasure—
Where the old earth hides her treasure deeper down..
The new volume is cordially recommended
to the Australians whom it typically repre-
sents in phrase arid sentiment. The publishers
are Messrs. Angus and Robertson, Sydney arid
Melbourne.
Every student of Australian history and geo-
graphy, and of the story of Australian exploration
(writes F.J.8.), will feel grateful toErnest Favene,
for the work (modestly called a “compilation”)
published in “ Brooks’s Australian School 1 Series.”
“ The Geographical Development of Australia” is
much more than a compilation. It possesses
features of marked originality.
Australia is shaped like a saucer, with—at vary-
ing distances from the coast—a rim which has been
called the Great Divide. Had the continent been
shaped like a dish-cover, with a mountain range,
by way of handle, in the centre, the problem of
droughts and deserts would never have confronted
us. The Great Divide, beginning in South Aus-
tralia, practically annulates the entire continent,
with the exception of the desert bounded by the
Great Australian Bight. This creates the coastal
river system. On the western side of the Great
Divide is the Murray River system. Between
this and the Macdonnell Ranges lies the salt lake
system ; and, stretching westward for hundreds
of miles to the head waters of the coastal rivers of
Western Australia, is the vast riderless area of
desert. These physical facts are admirably
brought out by a map designed by Favene on
a new plan.
The feature of the book is, however, the de-
velopment of geographical knowledge, hand-in-
hand with the progress of settlement consequent
on exploration. This side of our history is
illustrated by a series of maps, dated from 1808 to
1888 at 10-year intervals. In these maps the un-
known area is coloured black ; the known area is
picked out in white, which, in the earlier maps, is
a very insignificant quantity indeed ; that of
1808 showing only that part of N.S.W. extending
from the Hunter to the Cataract and taking in the
Hawkesbury, while Port Phillip appears as a
mere white pin-head in the surrounding black ;
and these, with Western Port, St. Vincent’s Gulf,
and King George’s Sound summarise the geo-
graphical knowledge of the period. From this
stage the white portion steadily encroaches on
the black, till, in the 1888 map, we find the latter
represented only by sundry patches in the rainless
and riverless area of Western Australia. The
intermediate maps showsuccessivelyand distinctly
the work of the various explorers, from Cunning-
ham and Hume to Forrest, Warburton and Giles.
The work of geographical development is now
practically finished, since, despite the remaining
black patches aforesaid, everything of value to do
has been done.
One cannot say precisely what is the clwtrm of
Henslowe's Annual, which (for the fourth time)
rears its head proudly above Australia. IV
literary matter is as undistinguished as ever ; the
editor’s own drawings are as wooden as ever ; and
the other illustrators have certainly not excelled
themselves, however they have surpassed their
patron. Yet there i s a charm—vague, indefinable,
oozing, like a damp smell from a dark church-
yard. Possibly it exudes from the verse of
Edward Hyacinth Tottenham, M.A.—verse like
this:
THE FOREST.
Life is a forest dark,
Wild, wild is the breeze,
Fitfully skimpering adown
. . . Adown thro’ the trees.
Life is a forest dark,
Wild, wild is the breeze.
But there's a shimmering beyond !. . .
. . . Beyond . . . thro’ the trees.
Edward Hyacinth Tottenham, M.A.
Does the charm come when Hyacinth is skimpering
adown ? or when he is shimmering beyond ? This
is irritating, for one cannot say precisely.
Since Zola’s death it has been disclosed that he
had two natural children by a Mme. Rosereau—
Mine. Zola bore him none. But, as happens in
liberal France, the wives legal and illegal met in
amity over their husband's body.
S.O.C. desires to know why, “of all the news-
papers, magazines; etc., who at different times
criticise Marie Corelli’s works, few, if any, have
ever a word of real unprejudiced praise to say in
her favour.” It is because most of the newspapers
and magazines are conducted by men, which are
jealous that a woman should possess the most
wonderful literary talent of the age. Thus the
editor of Eng. Gentleivoman, the other day,
meanly printed a letter that Miss Corelli wrote
to him, complaining because her name was left
out of a list of persons admitted to an enclosure
where King Edward was. He knew that be could
never learn to write like Miss Corelli, so lie per-
mitted it to appear that this marvellous, romancer
has the instincts of a snob.
Arthur a’Beckett, assistant editor of London
Punch , tells interviewers that lie sleeps only three
and a-half hours per day. Punch , of course, sleeps
the other twenty and a-half hours.
Melville and Mullen publish Australian Echoes,
a book of verses by John Mathew (taken to be the
Rev. John Mathew who wrote that excellent
study of the aborigines, Eaijlehawk and Crow).
The echoes, alas ! are uninspired and uninspiring
—prosaic, virtuous sentiments sheathed in
rhythms of antique buckram.
The Bookfellow has pleasure in recommend-
ing to book-buyers the long-established house of
Geo. Robertson and Co. Prdp. Ltd., Sydney.
The firm’s advertisement appears on this page,
and that in itself is a recommendation. Orders
for any book or magazine, new or old, may be
forwarded ; and the staff takes pains and pleasure
in answering queries. Especial attention is given
to teachers and librarians. And this is an honest
puff.
Cutcliffe Hyne’s Honour of Thieves, dropped
into publicity in 1895, made no particular splash—
though it contained the first sketch of admirable
Captain Kettle. In 1902, re-issued under the
new title of The Little Red Captain: An Early
Adventure of Capt. Kettle, the book finds sale and
praise. This conversion of backwater into flood-
tide shows how the modern publisher leads on to
fortune. 4
In Roman Jesuitism’s Pollution of Ethics (a 24
pp. pamphlet issued by W. K. Thomas and Co.,
Adelaide) W. A. Magarey tells us interesting
things. For example: about “the Capture of
the Press” by Jesuits :
. Not an open capture, for the influence of a paper
known to be Roman is, in Protestant countries, at once
discounted. It is a subtle manning of the reporting and
setting-up staffs of important newspapers with lay-
Jesuits, who,, in obedience to their secret or implied
instructions, will, wherever possible, omit, misreport,
or ridicule anything and anybody opposed to the aims
of the Order or of the Roman Church ; and, on the other
hand, introduce, report fully, or glorify anything and
anybody favouring those aims. It is all so intangible that
even the editors and proprietors may not perceive ; but
the influence upon the public mind is there always. That
great puhlic mind whose daily food is newspaper.
This evil, says Magarey, is rampant in Australia.
One desires to know where? when? precisely how ?
An ounce of clear proof is worth a ton of vague
assertion. W. A. Magarey’s method is, generally
speaking, a method of vague assertion throughout
his pamphlet. And, like the other religious con-
troversialists, he abstains from giving references.
There is no sense in quoting doctrines of horror at
third or tenth-hand, and attributing them to
Escobar, Lfguori and company. We want chap-
ter and the original editions ; and
with so much zeal, religious pamphleteers might
spare a little piins. It is doubtful whether even
Cardinal Moran will think Mr. Magarey worth
replying to. (And this par., anyway, is written
by a healthy pagan.)
Under the title The Earthen Floor, E. J. Brady
issues from Grafton Grin offioe some seventy
pages of verse gathered since publication of The
Ways of Many Waters. The new book cannot be
said to rank as highly as the old ; but' it contains
some excellent pieces and stanza?. Brady’s best
work is still done in the nautical vein which has
taught us to prize him. The blind sailor’s regrets
are effectively voiced, fqr example—
If I moon out here on the cliffs and think, my face to the
open sea,
’T is only because of the things I love that throng in
the dark to me :
’T is only because of a world I knew, a world that was
wholly mine ;
It had three parts water and one part land, and plenty of
teak and pine ... V;™. ,
They are cracking it on up coast, I ’ll bet. ’T is a rattling
tops’l breeze;
I can see the gloss of their wetted sides awash with the
clinging seas ;
I can catch the gleam of the Baltic pine with the caulking
lines between ; ,
And I know the rig of the yards of Tyne from the build
of Aberdeen.
How the smell of it sets my heart to beat! There’s never
a garden rose
With a scent like that of the salt sou’-east when a ten-
knot monsoon blows ...
Till I hear the churn of the bow-cut waves ; the kiss of
her cuddling dip;
Till I’m live and whole and a king again on the deck of
my bonny ship. ...
And “ Otahai, ” too, comes with the fragrance of
frangipanni on a tropical beach, the murmur of
tropical tides lap-lapping under the moon.
Then the: leaves like lace hung down
From the sleeping palm-tree’s crown ;
Then we heard tha sea-birds call,
Heard the night-tide rise and fall—
Loving, dreaming, you and I,
On the beach at Otahai. . .
The price of the booklet is Is. 6d.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Our Own Live Poets.
Shall we dream, and for ever he mourning
Dead bards, and lie down in their dust?
Shall we write, and for ever be scorning
New lyrics that come as-they must ?
We’ve men who are hustling and shoving,
And women still luring and loving
Down South of the line, and disproving
Old dogmas and rules red with rust.
In the boundary-rider’s old whare,
In the night, velvet-soft, silver-starred,
There’s a fellow who’s saying he’s sorry
In verse strong as wine, sweet as nard.
Though his Helicon’s only a billy,
His Pegasus just a bay filly,
He sings (as he grafts) willy-nilly :
A typical Bulletin bard.
If his harp’s not the harp of old singera
Who sang of the Queens of old time,
In the life of to-day there yet lingers
A girl or a kiss worth a rhyme.
If old rules in his verses are broken,
The words, in a younger strain spoken,
Are a dawning new day, and a token
Of a new young Parnassus to olimb.
By the light of a mutton-fat candle,
When the fire in his shanty is low,
A “ ringer ” is chewing the handle
Of a pen in the long night Smoke-oh.
With the rest of the board swapping stories,
“ Contes Drdlatiques ” garnished with “gory’s,
He’s busy transcribing the glories
Of things in the old Overflow.
In the camps and the bush and the gullies
And plains, though your mind is not set
To capture their speech, or too dull is
To fathom their joy and regret,
Some fellows are piling the treasure :
And their message —the pain and the pleasure
They weave into metre at leisure —
Wide songs that are echoing yet.
The swagman finds songs without seeking,
Caught up on the wallaby track ;
They blaze in the musterer’s beacon
On hills in the country out-back.
And the lute of the bush dilettante
Is strung in the blazing grog-shanty—
The drought-land has food for the Dante :
The stockwhip great odes in each crack.
The word wings its way from the choking
Blind spray on the collier a-roll,
Swinging up in the teeth of the smoking
Harsh seas racing down to the Pole.
From eastward and south’ard and nor’ward,
Heard inland or echoing shoreward,
The songs of the live men, flung forward,
Torn up by the roots from the soul.
In the bush and plain, mountain and meadow,
They are building, with keen ringing chords.
Their city, undimmed by the shadow
Of alien lyrical lords.
Their songs of a younger new fashion
Are fresh love, hot labor and passion :
They are with us—their lips are not ashen —
We have found a new creed in their words !
ML.
M. CORMAC.
Our Song-Birds.
Our mimic birds their cries have caught
From Nature’s speechful strife,
From rev el and corroboree,
Wild laugh of savage life;
One minstrel bird alone we boast
Who learnt his song from heaven’s host.
Out on the fringes of the bush
The gaudy parrots cry,
And cockatoos with crests erecb
Scream hate, and grace defy ;
The whip-bird’s whistle cuts the air,
The jackass laughs while men despair.
And though the notes of gentler birds
Attend our lonely way,
Till comes the magpie, carolling,
W e hear no perfect lay ;
He hath no heart-outpouring trill,
But how his note is musical!
A chorister, in surplice clad,
He seemeth always near,
And pipes his joyous canticle
Through all the changeful year—
He ushers in the dawn of day,
And makes the hour of twilight gay.
Our bards, rough-fashioned like our birds,
Their first impulse have caught
From Nature in her sterner moods,
When man is held as naught;
The rage of flood, and drought, and fire,
The lonely life, sad themes inspire.
With brighter days, and larger hopes,
Our bards with open eyes
Will see and love the beautiful —
O land of wrathful cries !
“ Weird melancholy ” dost thou know,
But thou shalt have thy VAllegro.
V.
ANDREW SARD.

MODEL 1900 SINGLE-SHOT RIFLE
IS rifle sells at a price within reach of almost every-
body’s pocketbook, but it is made with the same care and
exactness as Winchester rifles that cost five times as
much. The name “Winchester” on this rifle is a
guarantee in itself of its quality. The Model 1900 is a
hammerless take-down, and can be taken apart easily
and quickly. It is made with an 18-inch round barrel,
fitted with open front and rear sights. It shoots .22 Short and .22
Long Rim-Fire cartridges, and Bullet Breech caps. These cartridges cost
so little that one can afford to shoot as many times as he likes as often
as he likes. Some makes of .22 Caliber rifles are a few pence cheaper,
but they are a great deal cheaper in quality than they are in price.
We don’t try to see how cheap we can make this rifle, but how good.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 27, 1902.
The Murray-Darling Irrigation Problem.
The first Australian Government has a hard
row to hoe. It took up the work of adminis-
tration when the provincial hatreds and
jealousies of the six States were fresh and
raw, when each one was still full of the idea
that the union was made for its special and
exclusive benefit, and when the only way to
really and wholly satisfy any one was to con-
fiscate the goods and chattels of at least two
others. It had to drive six horses abreast—
six horses that had never been in harness
before, and whose fixed idea was that five
should always jib at the same moment. In
future years there will probably be Govern-
ments of far less ability that will drive the
team with far less friction, for the simple
reason that the innate cussedness of the
six will, by that time, be diminished
by custom and the effluxion of time. At
present, as regards a large number of
matters, if the Australian Government
strikes a fair average it doesn’t really please
any State ; if it really pleases one State it
fills at least three with indignation. This
was about the task with which the Barton
Ministry commenced its career :
To construct a purely-revenue tariff which would be
wholly Protectionist.
To build a transcontinental railway which Would go
from Adelaide to Perth, also from Adelaide to Port
Uarwin, likewise from Sydney to Port Darwin—the same
railway.
Not to tax the people heavily ; also to construct out of
revenue works which the States used to build out of
loans, and to return the States as much customs revenue
without border duties as they used to get with them (this
after paying the cost of the Federal administration, and
constructing the aforementioned works, and cutting the
tariff down to zero). b
To enforce the customs law without fear or favor vet
not to prosecute fraudulent importers.
niggers freely 1 ** " Australia policy while admitting
To enforce a law against the importation of contract
labor, jet to let contract labor (especially six hatters) in
without question.
To prevent Federation being accompanied by any
increased burden on the people while the States insist on
spending as much on their State Governments as they
look after ttloSe had tlle whoie business of the country to
To buUd a Federal' capital inside 18 months, on a site
would be wholly satisfactorj 7 to everybody, and to
build it without monej". J
Also to bring down rain, and do many other things
* • . ,
The report of the Murray River Water
Conservation Commission opens up another
pleasing prospect for the Barton Govern-
ment by way of addition to the above list.
The recent drought—if it is “ recent ” —has
convinced Australia that something must be
done to prevent its one big river system
running to waste. It is a big system with
very little water in it, but the need for con-
serving what water there is is all the more
urgent. So the commission was appointed,
and sat down to consider this complicated
situation :
The Murray-Darling' system extends mainly over New
South Wales, yictoria, and South Australia. In wet
seasons it also includes a good part of Queensland, but
of late years Queensland’s share of it has been mostly dry.
N.S.W. and Victoria supply water to the system in
about equal proportions. Victoria supplies most of the
Murray water. N.S.W. supplies part of the Murray
water and a good part of the Darling supply. South
Australia supplies no water—only an outlet. In wet
years an outlet is essential. In very dry years it is merely
a leakage, and so much superfluous frill.
N.S.W. has hitherto claimed sole ownership of the
Murray, and objects (theoretically) to Victoria irrigating
with the Murray waters, or even with the waters of
purely Victorian rivers which flow into the Murray.
N.S.W. has also claimed what is practically the sole
ownership of the Darling, regardless of the fact that
Queensland is at least a casual—sometimes a large—con-
tributor to its waters.
N.S.W. has claimed what is practically the sole right
to use the Murray and Darling waters for irrigation pur-
poses, regardless of what may happen to Victoria and
S.A. . But as there has, so far, been no irrigation on a
sufficiently large scale to make serious trouble, these claims
have been merely matters for paper argument.
_ Victoria has hitherto claimed the right to use for irriga-
tion purposes not only the waters of its Murray tribu-
taries, but also of the Murray itself, regardless of what
may happen to S.A. It has done more irrigation than
any of its neighbors ; still it hasn’t done enough to
greatly reduce the water supply and bring the question
to a head.
S.A. demands a share of the water supply for irrigation
purposes, and it is assuredly entitled to a share. But it
also demands that enough water shall be left in the river
to make it navigable, because the river trade is mostly
S.A. trade, and it demands this even if the irrigable lands
of N.S.W. and Victoria are left dry.
Queensland, so far, doesn’t seem to say anything.
• * • •
The Australian Government—which it is
the habit of the parochial party in each State
to speak of as if it were a foreign Govern-
ment, also a public enemy, and also a
miracle worker which can do, in 18 months,
out of no visible cash resources, all the things
that the State Parliaments have failed to do
with 40 years of time and £200,000,000 of
borrowed money and all the proceeds of their
land sales, but which wilfully refuses, by
reason of its innate malevolence, to do these
things—set itself seriously to work to recon-
cile these impossible pretensions. It
appointed a Happy Family consisting of
men from each of the three States which
are most interested and most indignant, and
asked them to draw up a workable scheme.
The delegates from N.S.W. and Victoria
appear to have done so, the one from S.A.
violently dissenting on many important
points. The commission went on the simplest
and the only possible plan. It disregarded all
the old paper claims and quarrels, and started
on thelogical basis that Australia is adry coun-
try, that there isn’tenough water togo round,
and that the only thing to do is to share out
the waters of the Darling and Murray among
the people settled in their vicinity as fairly
as possible. It had to consider the popula-
tion, the amount of land in each State favor-
ably situated for irrigation, and a host of
other things, and it appears to have done
about as well as possible.
• * * *
Its main recommendations are that, until
l ocks and weirs are constructed over the
whole river system, N.S.W. shall, during
the months when the rivers are supposed to
be high, be allowed to take from them
292,000 cubic feet per minute ; Victoria,
146,000 cubic feet; and 170,000 cubic feet
are to be left for S.A. In the dry months
N.S.W. is to have the right to use
240,000 cubic feet; Victoria, 127,000;
S. A., 70,000. These amounts are to be pro-
portionately increased or decreased as the
supply igr larger or smaller than the average.
Certain alleged tributaries which only send
water into the main river at times of high
flood, when their supply is not wanted, are
not regarded as part of the Murray-Darling
system at all, and, as regards whatever water
the respective States can find in their upper
channels, these States may do as they please.
As soon as the Australian Government can
find about £3,000,000, it is recommended
that large water-conservation works be
undertaken, some of them to be charged to
all three States, and others to Victoria and
N.S.W. only. These works, it is to be
hoped, may make the river permanently
navigable, but the N.S.W. and Victorian
section of the commission rightly holds
that this is not the first consideration by a
long way. Irrigation comes first all the time.
If the water is used for irrigation the country
will grow a vast amount of produce, and
supposing navigation is rendered impossible
the produce can be shifted by rail. If
irrigation is abandoned in order to save
enough water for navigation, then steamers
will be able to plod through a wilderness
where, in bad years, there is next to nothing
for them to carry. Railways can replace
navigation; but iron won’t make crops
grow, and neither man nor beast can drink
signal-boxes and permanent way; and rail-
way lines, as a substitute for irrigation, are
a dead failure. Till the water conservation
works are constructed, it is "seemingly in-
tended that each State shall have the right
to annex its share of the water the best way
it can, Federal officers being appointed to
see that no State takes more than its share.
If it takes less that will be “ its own funeral."
It is difficult to see what more can be done.
State extravagance has so wrecked the credit
and diminished the cash* of Australia, that it
is impossible to find £3,000,000 at a mo-
ment’s notice.
» ♦ • *
The proposed shares seem fair enough.
The general idea appears to be that the
Murray waters are divided equally between
N.S.W. and Victoria ; that Victoria has the
use of the Goulburn, and N.S.W. of the
Darling and Murrurabidgee, but all this
is subject to the proviso that S.A.,
the small, dry brother further down,
must be left its fair proportion. But
the S.A. delegate demanded that the
first consideration should be to leave
enough water in the river for navigation
purposes, so that the river boats can carry
the trade down to his own State. Certainly
the cheapest outlet for produce from the
Darling is down the river to S.A., but it is
much more important to have produce to
send out than a cheap outlet and no produce.
To make quite sure of keeping the river
navigable would apparently, in bad years,
mean abandoning irrigation altogether, or
nearly so. It would mean, in bad years,
a big river flowing, at great expense in
public works, through a desert, which is
much the same as offering a jug of water to
a corpse.
• • • •
Jenkins’ Attorney-General is especially
aggrieved in this matter, and says, in bitter-
ness of spirit, that the whole preposterous
scheme will leave S.A. worse off than it was
before. In a sense, and till the huge
water conservation works are built, it
will—in fact, it is difficult to see
what else Jenkins’ Attorney-General
expected. Irrigation is a scheme for
taking water out of a river—not for
putting it in. Hitherto there has been
really very little irrigation in N.S.W. and
Victoria. Almost all the water of the
Murray-Darling, except what evaporated,
has flowed into S.A., and S.A. has let almost
all of it flow into the sea. If S.A. expects
that after large irrigation works are estab-
lished in the two eastern States it will get
as much water as hitherto, it is a very
sanguine community indeed. Still the com-
mission’s scheme provides two things for
which S.A. should be profoundly thank-
ful. It proposes to give that State a
fixed right to a certain proportion, and a
very fair proportion, of the available water ;
and if no such right is arranged, and things
are left on the old go-as-you-please basis,
N.S.W. and Victoria will probably, in time,
mop up the whole two rivers for their own
irrigation works, and leave S.A., except in
very wet seasons, with only a furrow in the
sand. Also the scheme provides, ultimately,
for large conservation works in S.A., and
one-fourth of the water, if held back by
weirs and locks, will probably provide the
Crow-eater State with as good a river as it
had when practically all the water flowed
through it into the ocean.
• • * •
But, in any case, this much seems reason-
ably certain : If the Australian Government
leaves the whole business alone it will be
warmly objurgated by all three States. And
if it adopts the commission’s very reasonable
scheme, it seems in a fair way to make many
reliable enemies in S.A., even if the other
two States don’t want to rend it asunder for
giving S.A. too much, or for giving each
other too much, or for giving each other
and S.A. too much, or for unfair division of
the cost, or simply on general principles. It
is a hard contract to be the first Australian
Government, when all the old parochial
grudges are fresh and in good working order.
Victorian and other Railway Deficits.
Victoria has now an accumulated railway deficit
of £10,836,140, and N.S. W. a similar accumulated
deficit of £8,223,257. All the other States, except
Westralia, have the same kind of spectre on hand,
but these two are the largest ones. They are both
serious items, but JST.S.W. takes its perpetual rail-
way shortage so philosophically, and says so little
about it, that not one person in ten knows of its
existence. Victoria, on the other hand, takes its
shortage in a loud and frantic spirit, and hires
wild, fat individuals like Bent to put it right.
And Bent’s idea is to curse everybody and
everything ; to smite the table hard with his fist
and issue forty impossible and contradictory
orders at once ; to personally bullyrag station-
masters, porters, and men who wheel barrow-
loads of cinders ; to pay five days’ wages for six
days’ work ; and to pay high wages to probably
unqualified constituents of his own, and
send them round to decide, out of the vastness
of their experience in running a public-house
or a drapery store, whether the employees are
doing their duty or not, and whether the manage-
ment is good, bad or indifferent. Incidentally,
also, he has decided to help the railways and to be
good to some of his own constituents by estab-
lishing a gigantic new deadhead system. Leaping
frantically in the air and roaring in a powerful
bull-voice, Thomas has decreed that whoever
builds a house worth between £3OO and £4OO in
any one of a large number of favored localities
shall get a free pass between that place and Mel-
bourne for two years, and the duration of the pass
is to vary with the value of the house, until the
person who puts up a house worth over £lOOO is
to help to reduce the Victorian railway deficit by
being a deadhead on the lines for nine years.
• • • •
All this row, to say nothing of smaller but
equally persistent rows on the same subject in
other States, is based on the preliminary theory
that the Australian State railways ought to pay
under existing conditions, and that if they don’t
pay there is something wrong. And, as a matter
of fact, no sane individual can expect them to pay
what is demanded of them. In other countries
where the population was comparatively thin,
railways were built almost wholly by alleged
private enterprise, which meant, in most cases,
that the State found at least half the money, and
the syndicate owned all the railway. In Canada
the State paid in land and cash, and other aids,
between £5OOO and £6OOO for every mile of private
line now in existence, which left the companies to
pay about a similar amount. If N.S.W. and
Victoria had handed over to their Railway Do-
partments a similar endowment of £17,000,000 o
£18,000,000 worth of land apiece, or paid that amount
of the construction cost out of revenue, neither of
them would have any deficits whatever; in fact
they would be able by this time, on present rates.’
to pay interest and working expenses, and show
about £500,000 a-year profit apiece. The United
States and the South American republics dumped
down State aid to private railway enterprise with
an equally lavish hand. To put the Australian
State railways on a level with companv-owned
railways in other countries which are somewhat
like ours in scarcity of population, the accumu-
lated deficit should be wiped off, and the lines
should only be expected to pay interest on one-
half their original cost. In no country with a
population anything like as scanty as that of Aus-
tralia do the railways do anything like what the
Australians expect seldom anything like half
what the Australians expect—from their State
systems.
• • • •
In the early years of a private railway, it seldom
pays dividends, or, at least, it pays very small
ones. The older States of Australia expected their
lines to pay about per cent, from the beginning.
The early railways were built with money bor-
rowed at as high as 6 or 7 per cent., and as the
lines couldn’t earn this, the Great Deficits started.
The State railways got no land-grants, as private
lines did in almost every country. They- weren’t
credited with a penny of the extra value they
added to private lands or to State lands ; they
were loaded with an absurd and ridiculous debt
(as well as a debt at ridiculously high interest)
through the purchase of the land required for
railway purposes from influential private thieves
at from twice to eight times its value ; and they
were expected to carry all manner of social and
political deadheads at their own loss. In Vic-
toria, especially, the lines were starved
from the commencement in the struggle
to make them pay about twice as much
interest as any other lines on earth with a similar
country to depend on. No allowance was made
out of earnings for depreciation in the early days.
Repairs were always put off till the last moment,
in the effort to show the good balance-sheet that
the public demanded, and when they accumulated
to a huge extent they were paid for out of loans.
Old roliing-stock wore out, and new rolling-stock
was charged to loans. New bridges were built
out of loans to replace old ones, also built out of
loans. In the struggle to furnish railway facilities
to every two-men-and-a-dog township, to carry
goods and passengers at rates only possible in
much more thickly-peopled lands, to carry free all
the political and social deadheads of the country,
and to make the lines pay full interest on a cost
of £12,000 or £13,000 per mile, while the Canadian
private lines pay a much lower rate of interest on
about £6OOO a mile (the interest on the other
.£5OOO or .£6OOO a mile being paid by the general
Canadian taxpayer), the existence of the Aus-
tralian State railways became one long “ fake.”
• • « •
Taking the Victorian railways as an awful
example, they are expected to defray the interest
on over £2,200,000 of debt incurred for rolling-
stock which has disappeared, and which should
have been paid for out of earnings—it wasn’t paid
out of earnings as the stock was used up, simply be-
cause of the constant struggle to show impossibly
good results under impossibly adverse condi-
tions. They are expected to pay interest
also on about £6,000,000 spent out of loans
on alterations and repairs; this was also
charged to loans in the struggle to make the
revenue show, for the moment, an absurdly good
result. They have to pay interest on £217,000
spent on abandoned lines, and about £300,000
spent on surveys which never came to anything,
also on an immense amount spent on land re-
sumptions, over and above the present value of
the land. Taking them all round, the Victorian
railways have been a splendidly profitable pro-
perty. They have honestly returned, on an
average, fully 3-£ per cent, all the days of their
existence, ranging from over 4J per cent, in the
long ago down to a, little more than 3 per cent,
now—this in addition to doing immense good to
the country. But when they 7 were able to earn
per cent, the Department wanted them to earn
6 per cent., because a fool-Governmerit, instead
of constructing them out of land revenue, had
borrowed the money at 6 per cent, to build them.
And to make them look as if they earned as near
to 6 per cent, as possible, all manner of current
expenses were charged to loans instead of being
paid out of revenue, till a huge dead debt grew
upon them, the interest bill attached to which
made solvency impossible. So now they earn 3
per cent, or so, and still charge repairs and so
on to loans, and accumulate more dead debt, in
the effort to make them look as if they earned 3£
per cent. And every Australian railway system
(possibly bar Westralia’s) is in the same situation.
• * • •
The way to abolish the Victorian railway deficit
does not lie in the wild howlings of Thomas Bent,
nor does it lie in a general distribution of free
passes to Thomas Bent’s or anyone else’s consti-
tuents, nor in a general cutting down of wages,
or sacking of employees, or engagement of
Thomas Bent’s unemployed, and possibly quite
respectable, constituents as spies. There is, doubt-
less, great room for improved management, but
even that isn’t the first essential; even now the
Victorian railways are well up in the list of the most
cheaply-managed railways in the world. What is
wanted first is honesty—just common honesty. Let
the Railway Department be relieved of the debt
unjustly heaped upon it in the past—the debt
for abandoned lines, abandoned surveys, patches,
repairs, and for new rolling-stock to replace old.
All this should have been paid for out of the Tail-
way revenue which the people received and spent
in former days; and it was not so paid because
the people demanded that the railways should
show absurdly large results on absurdly small
rates. Let all this bygone swindle be made good
and the amount—probably over £10,000,000
be taken out of the railway debt, and added to the
deficit, and paid off by instalments. Let the rail-
ways be relieved of the necessity of carrying
deadheads gratis, and starving stock free, and
fodder for starving stock at reduced rates. These
are charities, and if the people want to be good to
the poor they should do it openly ; it is absurd
to load the railways with a great part of the
country’s charitable expenditure and then
start a general persecution of railway
servants because the lines don’t pay. With
these things done, the Victorian railways would
defray the interest on every penny of their cost,
after providing fully for maintenance and repairs
—they would even pay a little towards a sinking
fund. They would certainly, on present results,
pay better without land-grants or other outside
aids than the Canadian lines do with land-grants
and other assistance to the extent of £5OOO or
so a mile. And the same theory applies to the
railway systems of other States. All they ask
to make them splendid properties is that the
Parliaments and the people should go straight
That is what they ask—what they mostly get n
Bent.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Navigation Question.
Australia was spoken to rather severely last
week by one of the potentates of the Orient s.s.
Co. He said the big steamship companies didn’t
seek to do a coastal trade—to take passengers and
cargo, that is, from one Australian port to another;
therefore, if the Australian Parliament, under the
impression that they sought to do such a trade,
enacted, as has been proposed under the Naviga-
tion law now in preparation, that they must
pay Australian rates of wages while on this coast
it would be a serious injustice. One possible
result of the said injustice would be, when the
Western transcontinental railway was built, that
the big European steamships would call at Fre-
mantle only—dropping Adelaide, Melbourne and
Sydney altogether. Supposing that event did come
to pass, it would, of course, mean a great rush of
business for the transcontinental railway, and
Australia, which would naturally want that line
to pay, might not be wholly displeased. But,
apart from that, it seems as if some fair arrange-
ment might be made. „ Australia doesn’t really
care a straw what wages outside vessels pay on its
coasts so long as they are not trading on its
coasts and unfairly competing with locally-owned
vessels, that have to pay higher wages. Let
it therefore be enacted, on the American plan,
that no vessel that is not owned in the Common-
wealth shall take in goods or passengers at any
Commonwealth port for delivery at any other
Commonwealth port, and the thing is done. As
we are informed, on the best authority, that the
British steamship companies don’t want a
coastal trade, they naturally wouldn’t object to
not being allowed to have it. Then, as British
and foreign low-wage vessels wouldn’t compete
with the locally-owned coasters, there would be no
need (until the Commonwealth begins to run steam-
ships of its own to Europe) to interfere with the
wages paid on British and foreign vessels at all.
They might carry goods from foreign ports to any
part of Australia, and back again; they might
stay about our coasts as long as they pleased, and
they might pay their sailors 2s. 6d. a century if
they liked, and the Australian sailor and ship-
owner would be none the worse. This arrange-
ment should satisfy all parties—for some years at
least, and till new questions and circumstances*
arise. It is a blessed and Christian thing to
satisfy all parties.

A Disastrous Effect in the Minds of
Capitalists.
Robert Harper, M.H.R., has returned from a
visit to China, Japan and other foreign
parts, with the heart-breaking news that “ our
labor legislation is producing a most disastrous
effect in the minds of capitalists ” thereabouts.
And Robert Harper, M. H.R., seems to advance
this as a really terrifying fact. The capitalist has,
as a rule, a quite inordinate opinion of the im-
portance of his own mind and the value of his
good opinion, and when he returns from anywhere
with the news that our course of action is a Shock
to other people like himself he feels that he has
struck this country with a very large and spiky
club indeed. Yet Australia has had the minds of
Harper and Sargood and Daniel Cooper and
others of the same sort under its close
observation for years, and hasn’t seen anything
in them or similar minds to make their dis-
approval so very serious. It has done things
that had a disastrous effect on their minds and
the heavens didn’t fall; it has done things that
pleased their minds and the earth didn’t rise. It
lias no reason to suppose that these foreign capi-
talists would give Australia any of their money if
this country’s labor legislation didn’t have a
disastrous effect on their minds, or that Australia
will lose any of their money by reason of the
disastrous effect aforesaid. It doesn’t know who
they are, and it doesn’t know for certain that they
have minds. Uso, it doesn’t care. Whatever
minds they have they probably require for their
own use. Further, Australia recognises the fact
that similar terrible things have been hap-
pening all the world over for as long as history
relates. George Washington’s conduct had a
disastrous effect in the mind of George 111.,
who was a considerable capitalist; and French
capitalists strongly disappro\ ed of the Revolution;
and Egyptian capitalists had a powerful objection
to the departure of the Israelites ; and the opinions
of Roman capitalists regarding Alaric were
doubtless strongly hostile. Capitalists have been
disapproving of something or other ever since the
glebe began, and the world has got along somehow
—in fact, Robert Harper would to-day be a serf
and a chattel if his ancestors hadn’t done things
that produced a most disastrous effect in the
minds of capitalists, and looking at Robert
Harper to-day The Bulletin is half-inclined, for
the first time, to wish that these insurrectionary
ancestors had left things alone. The habit, to
which ordinary Fat Men are addicted, of
coming jack from foreign lands with the
intimation —which rests on their bare word —
that capitalists or caterpillars or cockroaches are
greatly shocked at our labor legislation is pitiably
small and futile, 'in many cases the said capital-
ists never heard of our labor legislation till the
wandering Australian capitalist gave them his
own shockingly garbled version of it. In
other cases the Australian capitalist probably
just looked at them, and decided by their
appearance that they were the sort of men
who probably would be shocked by our
labor legislation if they heard of it. And mean-
while the seasons come and go, and the rain
comes or else stays away, and the wool grows as
best it can, and the wheat comes up if circum-
stances allow, and the gold and the coal are dug
out as usual, and the fact that some person who
has an office in Shanghai or Yokohama or New
York or London lifts his head out of the inkpot
for a moment and says he is shocked at our labor
legislation doesn’t matter as much as one cow’s
bone bleaching in the wilderness.

Private Communications to the Bench.
A Melbourne lawyer to The Bulletin :
The position is perilously close to a scandal when the
Chief Justice of Vic. Supreme Court has continually
to he coached in his law by the Crown Prosecutor. Two
instances have occurred during the present Supreme
C jurt sittings, in its Criminal Jurisdiction. In the first,
the C. J. sentenced a prisoner to seven years, and, when
it was" pointed out that he had exceeded his jurisdiction,
inasmuch as the maximum penalty for the offence was
only five years, prisoner had to undergo the anguish
of listening to another lecture, and the sentence was altered
to accord with the law. It would be interesting to
learn whether this can be done legally—some day, per-
haps the question will be properly tested. But the
principle underlying the second case is one not merely
affecting the interests of an individual, but also involving
the satisfactory (and possibly pure) administration of
justice. In the Wartman case a most unseemly wrangle
took place owing to the Chief Justice having recalled the
iurv and directed them on a certain point in the absence of
accused’s counsel. Having told them there were no taici-
cating circumstances he, on recalling, said they could bring
in a verdict of guilty under mitigating circumstances and
refused to allow prisoner’s counsel to address him on the
subject in presence of the jury. Whether he or Purves,
K. 0., is right is a matter that, in all likelihood, will he
settled by the Full Court. But it is interesting to note
how the jury came to be recalled at all. After the
Judge’s charge and the jury’s retirement, the Crown-
Prosecutor communicated to the jury the fact that His
Honor had overlooked the provisions of the Amending
Crimes Act. This oversight may have been by
inadvertence or it may not, but it most cer-
tainly should never have happened. The associate
carried the conversation to His Honor, who re-
called the jury and directed them on the point, and
who subsequently stated in answer to Mr. Purves that
“ the Crown Prosecutor had reminded him of the section
which he had forgotten when he charged the jury ” {vide
Age). His Honor seemed to overlook, or to treat in very
cavalier fashion, the fact that he does not occupy his
exalted position for the purpose of forgetting Acts of
Parliament. But the principle involved is whether it is
to be tolerated for a moment that, during the hearing of
a trial, the presiding Judge is to have any private con-
versation with or to receive any private communication
about the case from the Crown Prosecutor? No counsel
for a prisoner would for one moment think of daring
to approach any Judge in that way. In all such cases
the matter must be mentioned in open court, and very
properly so, for if private communications are permissible,
what a vista of possibilities is open to the view ! Counsel
may not during the hearing of a case, either civil or
criminal, speak to a friend on the most harmless matter
if that friend happens to be a juryman in the case ; but
if the Chief’s ruling is correct, that same counsel may,
as soon as he is elevated to the Bench, have private
communications conveyed to him in reference to the
case in issue. As an example, take a very extreme
suggestion. Suppose, for the sake of illustration only,
that Mr. Panton, P.M., during the hearing of a case, so
far forgot himself as to allow himself to be interviewed by
a detective in regard to the matter pending, and that the
occurrence subsequently came under the judicial notice
of the Chief. This supposition is, of course, clearly im-
possible, but good enough for the purpose of illustration.
Would the English language be sutliciently ample to
express His Honor’s indignant horror at such a proceed-
ing ? The fact is that there is a great deal too much of
this judge-made so-called Law being thrust down the
throats of a long-suffering community, and the sooner
private communications of however harmless a character
are put a stop to, the greater will be the feeling of security
that justice is being properly administered. The Chief is
notorious for being possessed of a phenomenal memory. It
is to be hoped that he will not again, in three short days,
forget the provisions of Acts of Parliament, and require
to be set right by the Crown Prosecutor. If his memory
is failing him, surely he can, at least, remember that a
copy of the Statutes is always with him on the Bench.
And, as he himself would say, “ Private or semi-private
communications of a controversial character are to be
regarded with extreme and unqualified reprobation.”

The N.S.W. Press Liar.
N.S. Wales journalism never reached a lofty
plane. It was clever in the days when convicts
and emancipists had m'osb to say, able in Empire
days, and so all through, so far as one office was
concerned, in the time when Hugh George bossed
S.M. Herald. But it was never, in any previous
period of decadence, quite so contemptible as at
present —so utterly lost to all sense of truth and
proper responsibility. A comparison of the
Federal Parliamentary reports in Argus or Age
with those of the Sydney dailies will make this
plain enough ; but if any man wants a searchlight
on it, let him face the facts of the Friedman or
the hatters’ case, and compare them with local
reports and comments. The Sydney press Fried-
man is put away in formalin, to be exhibited on
any likely occasion ; the hatters’ case, ended by
the admission of the men, is still as locally dis-
torted, falsified and magnified, going like a jack-
o’-lantern about the world. Not once has
one of the local dailies dared to set before
its readers the exact reasons for the main-
tenance of the clause under which the customs
authorities acted, nor yet the results which
might follow on its repeal or falling into
disuetude. Manual laborers under contract to
employers cannot be admitted at any Common-
wealth ports, unless the authorities are satisfied,
etc. And why not ? If any man wants to know,
let him look back to the time when Brogden and
Co. had on hand most of the railway-construction
of Maoriland, and introduced under contract a
thousand or so English navvies. The contract did
not stand, but what of that ? The men were on
the island, and had to earn a living. The
labor supply having been made to exceed the
demand, the contractor got the whip-hand and
used it. Suppose, in this instance, instead of six
English, 600 German hatters had been brought
over, all capable of passing the education test and
well-trained in the duties of German citizenship ?
Would local interests be saved by turning them
loose in the community, contracts quashed, but
bellies empty, and the necessity of working for
any wage becoming more exigent with every
week ? The Act is framed to bar both a black
and a white coolie system, and only those who are
hankering after one or the other are blatant in its
denunciation.

These Hatters.
A deputation, representing three crusted Tory
organisations—the Taxpayers’ Union of N.S. W.,
the Victorian Employers’ Federation and the
“National” League of S.A.—waited the other
day on Premier Barton to ask for the repeal of
the clause under which the six infuriated hatters
found their landing somewhat delayed. And
even this crowd had to admit that Premier
Barton’s action was strictly according to the law,
and that he couldn’t, without breaking the law,
have acted otherwise than he did. The following
is from Sydney Telegraph’s report:—
Mr. Fraser admitted that the Prime Minister could
NOT HAVE ACTED OTHERWISE THAN HE DID IN HAVING
REQUIRED PROOF THAT THE HATTERS WERE MEN OF SPECIAL
skill. Still it was un-English to have to take that
action. Such a provision in the law would most certainly
bring the Commonwealth into trouble with the other
British possessions throughout the world. He agreed
that Australia should not be made the dumping ground
for sweated workmen, but in this case such a suggestion
was not applicable, because the men were trade unionists,
and were to be paid union wages.
Mr. M'Lennan, of the N.S.W. Taxpayers’ Union, agreed
that a bar should be placed on the admission of
criminals, paupers and so on. It was repugnant
to Australians, however, that able-bodied, sound-
minded Englishmen should be kept oat. (“ Hear, hear.”)
The deputation looked upon the hatters as Englishmen,
who inherited with us the countries that our fathers
fought for, conquered, and peopled. (“ Hear, hear.”) T.ie
mere fact that we came to Australia first did not give us
exclusive right to this portion of the British Empire.
(Cheers.) These British were not undesirables, hut the
very people we wanted. (“Hear, hear.”) The deputa-
tion did not wish to criticise the Prime Minister’s admin-
istration of the Act. If anything, they supported the
whole-hearted and impartial way he had administered
the law as he had found it. They protested against the
law which, when they understood it, made them blush ;
and they thought that the law was on the statute-book
on account of the undue influence exercised by the Labor
party in Australian politics.
Thereby the Tax-dodgers and the rest give away
the whole case. The law exists; it was openly
created by the votes of a huge majority, so there
is no possible doubt about its existence; and
Premier Barton, by the admission of even his
opponents, simply enforced it in a perfectly legal
manner. In their agitation against him the
Reids and Joe Cooks and Pulsfords and
other strange political cattle didn’t dare
to demand the repeal of the law. So
they (like the insincere and shifty and un-
democratic Premier See) pasted their snuffling
little personal advt. on the case by denouncing
Barton for not breaking tiie law, or by hinting
that he was doing something not covered by the
law. And now the Taxdodgers and the Federated
Sweaters and the National Ass. go and break up
the Kjsid-Pulsford-G’ook show in this fashiou.

.The Pay of the Politician.
The State Parliaments of Australia have followed
the Australian Legislature into recess, marching
in most cases through the blood of slaughtered
Bills amid a storm of objurgation and rowdi-
ness. In no single State can the legislative
results of the session just ended be con-
sidered as at all satisfactory. Everywhere
there is the same condition of financial muddle,
and of legislative ineptitude—accounts in confu-
sion, necessary laws neglected, unnecessary laws
passed, “jobs” or scandalous follies as bad as
“jobs” approved—astale fumeof recrimination and
disorder hanging over all. This condition of tilings
is not extraordinary, and typical of one session only.
It is as usual as a dry season, so that the news-
papers have come to look upon it as a necessary
condition of Parliamentary government, provid-
ing bright paragraphs for them if doing no other
good. Yet it is safe to say that if, in any
public company, the board of directors con-
ducted business in the same way, the share-
holders would at once make a change. The
Parliamentary trouble, however, continues to
exist because the shareholders in the State (i.e.,
the voters) are singularly fatuous in their ideas.
They demand a high degree of brain power, a
serious sense of responsibility and an irreproach-
able honesty for a net average return (after deduct-
ing election expenses and so on) of about 50s.
a week, with the rather remote chance thrown in
of occasionally enjoying a net average salary of
about £lO a week for a short time as a Minister.
Naturally they don’t get it. The law of supply
and demand is to some extent interfered with in
this regard by considerations of patriotism and
public spirit; also by the willingness of the average
politician to take some of his pay in the tickling
of his vanity. But the great rule that a man or a
community will generally get what it pays for—no
more, though often less—is not wholly or even
largely abrogated by those considerations. The
people, on the whole, get either (1) the sort of
brain-power that has a market value of 50s. a
week; or (2) that portion of a clever man’s
brain-power which he is willing to give at
50s. a week and the chance of occasionally
getting £lO a week for a short while.
And that is the secret of Parliamentary stupidity
and Parliamentary laziness; of congested business,
neglected legislation and confused finances. When
the community has the sense to pay for its political
work being done properly it will have it done
properly ; it would be a cheap bargain if that end
could be secured by the payment, in a State of the
size of N.S.W., of £250,000 a year in Parlia-
mentary salaries.

Providence and the Kanaka.
The goody-goody proprietors of S.M. Herald are
hereby urgently requested to consider the follow-
ing statements (extracted from an article which
they allowed Senator Jawbone Neild to write
for their pious paper last Saturday), and ask
themselves if they can afford to lend their spotless
character to the dissemination of glaring and
obvious untruths in this fashion :
It was the proud boast of Englishmen that oil British
soil all men had equal rights. It has been reserved for
the Commonwealth to create legal distinctions between
man and man, such as Australia never knew before, and
to deny a British subject his daily bread because of a
shade in his face !
I have yet to learn that you can reverse the decisions
of Providence by Act of Parliament. The going of the
Kanaka will be followed by the extinction of the great
sugar industry. This happened before when Samuel
Griffith prevented the employment of Kanakas, and it
will happen again.
In that brief statement there are condensed two
flat and absolute untruths and one unreliable in-
sinuation. All men do not have equal rights on
British soil; if they had, the people of India
would have the same Parliamentary rights as
those of England, instead of being bossed by
white officials ; and a shade in a man’s face
wouldn’t prevent him rising beyond a cer-
tain very humble rank in the British-Indian
army ; and his color wouldn’t exclude him
from the blessed self - governing privileges
which the Englishman enjoys; and there would
be no special law dealing with Indian immi-
grants in Natal, and we wouldn’t see, the re-
markable spectacle of Parliamentary privileges
being practically restricted to the white popu-
lation of the empire. The great sugar industry
didn't become extinct when Samuel (Griffith re-
restricted Kanaka immigration, or anything
like it; any one who can read the flgures
dealing with sugar output in the Queensland
Government statistics can see that. As to the
fact that Senator Neild isn’t aware that the
decisions of Providence can be reversed by Act of
Parliament, Providence didn’t put the Kanaka or
the Asiatic in Queensland, and shutting them out
isn’t reversing any visible decision of Providence.
Also Providence didn’t put the white man in
Australia, so letting Jawbone Neild remain hero
is, in a sense, reversing its decisions, or trying to.
Surely the Herald, which is respectable and con-
scientious though painfully dull, must be at heart
ashamed of a cause which has to be bolstered up
by such glaring perversions of fact.

Justice Fringes Logic.
The dictum of Justice Piung, that when a Govt,
has decided on Way, everybody should either shut
up or shout “Hooray!” until the war is over,
ought not to stop at war. It should apply all
round. For instance:—
When the Crown has arrested a person and declared
(through a constable) that he is guilty, everybody should
shut up. The constable may be wrong; but that doesn’t
matter ; better that an innocent man should suffer than
that the Crown should be embarrassed.
After a man has been sentenced to be hanged for
murder, it is some sort of sedition to show that he wasn’t
there when the murder was committed. He may not
have been ; but we can’t afford to destroy confidence in
our Institutions—we must See the Tiling Through.
When the Government appoints a poet laureate, every-
body must either shut up or say that he is a great and
noble poet. He may, it is true, be a Nahum Tate or an
Alfred Austin. But that doesn’t matter ; nothing must
be done to suggest that the Government could possibly be
a poor judge of poets.
If the Government “ app’ints Maloney” it is the
solemn duty of Dill-Macky and everybody else to say
that Maloney is the best man for the job. Even if we
don't approve of Maloney we must pretend to—anything
rather than embarrass the Government.
Also, when Constituted Authority, as represented bv
the eight aldermen of Dingbat Swamp, declares that it
d >esn’t see why it shouldn’t empty the town filth down
by the town dam, it is the patriotic duty of Dingbat
Swamp to shut up. Better to all die of typhoid than to
reflect upon the wisdom of Constituted Authority After
everybody s dead it is permissible to suggest that the
eight aldermen probably made a mistake.

the future government of tliis hitherto benighted

Six Hatters and a Nation.
Thk Oront-es’ hatters have landed, to give up the
stage to the Oruba hatters, who were also detained
for a while by Federallawas being laborers imported
under contract and required, therefore, to provide
proof that they are men of special skill before they
can be exempted from the provisions of the Immi-
gration Restriction Act. Like the Orontes hatters,
the Oruba hatters are Englishmen, and their
detention, even for a day, makes the blood boil
in the veins of many a stout gentleman of
an English and plethoric habit of thought.
But no definite protest has been attempted
by England, and no real indignation has
been aroused among Australians, in spite of
the frenzied efforts of the newspapers representing
the “import ing in terest, ” which is always violently
English by nature even if it is foreign-Jew by
birth. The right of Australia, in fact, has been
once for all established definitely to keep out of
this continent English-born citizens if, in her own
interests, she so chooses. That is a big step
towards Australian independence, a definite
advance towards the National ideal. It is not
here urged that the right, so established, should
be exercised arbitrarily or offensively ; it would
not be advisable to at once set about the task of
excluding the objectionable Englishman who comes
out here with £lOO, his father’s curse, and a rooted
objection to labor, contract or otherwise. But
what is important is the fact that Australia
has proved her power to keep Australia for the
Australians and such immigrants as Australians
choose to welcome, and has shown that an Eng-
lishman is not necessarily welcome because he is
an Englishman. The six hatters have made
history. The next great step in the development
of Australian Nationalism to which The Bulletin
looks forward is the stopping at one of the ports
of the Commonwealth of a six-million British
loan, on the ground that it is an undesirable
immigrant of the worst kind. When that comes
Australia will really begin to own herself.

Those Bushveldt Scapegoats.
Now that the official censorship is relaxed, we are
enabled to learn more of the real facts of the
case against the Bushveldt Carbineers. Mrs.
Handcock is in receipt of a sea'ecl letter, through
the proper military channel, of which the follow-
ing is a true copy. It bears out The Bulletin’s
assumption that Morant and Handcock were
made the scapegoats of the British Army in
S’Africa :
Dear Madam, — I was military chaplain at Petersburg,
in the N. Transvaal, during all the time that the Bush-
veldt Carbineers had their headquarters there, and I
knew your late husband and all those officers and men
who were concerned, for and against, in his trial, and I
attended most of the sittings of the courts. And,
knowing what I know, I want to say to you that, great
as may be your grief for the loss of him, you need
feel no shame, but rather pride, on his account.
He was a good-hearted man and a brave soldier, simple
and fearless, and he did what he was told. If he did
wrong—l do not say that he did — it was the fault of his
superiors, and of those that appointed his superiors, and
gaoe them their orders. In the matter of the shooting of
Boer prisoners, of which he and others were found guilty,
he acted 'under the orders of Lieut. Morant, a man of
strong feelings and eager to avenge the savage murder of
his friend Capt. Hunt. In the matter of the shooting of
the missionary, the only one of the crimes charged which
really excited any moral indignation, the Court, without
hesitation, found him not guilty, and never, I should
think, has a feebler charge been brought before a Court.
I was not a friend of these officers of the Bushveldt
Carbineers, but my sympathy was aroused by the harsh
treatment they received—in being kept under close arrest
(I myself, the chaplain, was requested not to visit them)
for some months before they were tried, and by the way
the case was, as it were, prejudged from the statements
of bad men ; and by the utterly false accounts which were
inserted in English and, I believe, Australian papers.
I did not see your husband after he was taken down to
Pretoria, but I understand that he died simply and fear-
lessly, as he had lived.
I have heard it said that the execution convinced the
Boers of British fairness and made them ready to come to
terms. If this be so, then Lieuts. Morant arid Handcock
died for their country in a very special sense, and this is
one of the many instances of suffering, even if undeserved,
bringing salvation.
With much sympathy and good wishes, I am, yours
very truly,
Joshua Brough.
Newcastle, Staffs, 25/9/’O2.

Premier Irvine, of Victoria, says that his
Government won’t give way if the House of 48
Rich Landlords rejects or mangles his Reform
Bill. Which is very good in a general way—but,
to come down to details, what will Irvine do ? He
can’t dissolve the Council, lie can’t swamp it.
If he dissolves the Assembly, and gets a fresh
mandate from the country that would make no
difference. There are only three at present
visible ways of overcoming the House of Land-
lords : To get it to consent to its own abolition by
straight-out bribery—say by pensioning off the
present 48; to terrorise it by suspending the
whole machine of State till it gives way—by the
Assembly going on strike and leaving the public
service to go to pieces, the public liabilities to go
unpaid, and generally by letting Chaos come
again; and, thirdly, to shift it by Revolution.
Irvine must adopt one of these devices, or find a
fourth equally serviceable —or else cave in, as he
did over the Factories’ Bill.

As the Victorian Constitution Reform Bill passed
the Assembly on Wednesday last, it was a strange
mixture of the most mulish Toryism and the most
enlightened Liberalism. In this respect it was a
true reflex of the Irvine Govt, that carried it
unough. The Bill, on its Liberal side, reduces
the qualifications. of voters for the House of
Landlords to the ratepayers’ roll, provides
woman’s suffrage, embodies provisions for settling
deadlocks between the cwo Houses, and makes all
Council electors eligible to sit in the Council. On
its Tory side it restrict" the issue of voters’ certi-
ficates to ap that renders them almost
useless, Jnd does many other things.
There is a grave danger to democracy in
the peculiar mixture of this Reform Bill.
The Irvine Govt, and its majority is a good deal
more Tory than Libe V, and there is little doub
that if the Council throws out all the Liberal pro-
visions of the Biel and returns it to the Assembly
with its Tory framework intact, Irvine can, if he
wishes it, force the Assembly to accept the
Council s amendments. Ihe Arctic person stoutly
protests that he is deeply sincere in his attempt to
pass all the “essential features of the Bill” into
law whether the Council likes it or not. Up to
now, however, he lias been careful not to tell the
country what he regards as the il essential features
of the Bill.”

Vic. Premier Irvine’s tender regard for the Mel-
bourne Tramway monopolists is truly touching
Tnat grasping company having lately reduced its
fares on the lines, or portions of lines, where the
Otate railways are competitors, Hirsch, M.L A
asked Irvine if he would make use of the power
given to Parliament, by sec; 26 of the ’B3 Tram-
ways Act, to revise the company’s fares all round.
Irvine replied that he had waded through manv
byg’oue Hansards to find out Parliament’s infcen-
turn as to that clause—as if the section wasn’t

its dividends have averaged 17 per cent, per
annum, and it has almost doubled its capital and
placed to reserves more than its capital—all out
of profits! And Irvine doesn’t consider these
“ very large ” profits!
