George Musgrove resumed business at
Sydney Royal on Saturday, and put on the old
“Chinese Honeymoon” for a scrappy season of
four nights to fill in the pre-Christmas interval.
That wild and misty revel looked just a
trifle less unsatisfactory and a trifle brighter
than of old. It was nicely dressed and
brilliantly staged, and Miss May Beatty’s
get-up was better than formerly, and the
mother-in-law carried her own monkey to the
kitchen, instead of making the Emperor shift our
hairy mutual ancestor. The “Honeymoon” has
some shreds of coherence at the beginning, but
the latter half is an amiable derangement of
Chinese lunatics, and the meaning of it no
man is ever likely to find out. The cast
was the same, or much the same, as be-
fore. Miss Josephine Stanton was bright and
saucy as Princess Soo-Soo, and Miss May Beatty
was bright and perky as the lady-of-all-work and
general rusher-around-with-refreshments at an ill-
conducted Chinese hotel. Henry Hallam towered
like a tall, friendly full moon in the gauds and
petticoats of the fat Emperor, and the saddened
Admiral, with a countenance of the length of a
serpent, was as before. The “ Thirty Thieves” is
to illumine the Royal on Friday night. Hopes
are entertained that there is no mother-in-law in
the new piece. There has been a long course of
mother-in-law at the Criterion, and the recent
outbreak of the same female at the Royal was a
superfluous infliction.
* * * 4*
Bland Holt’s “ White Heather" gives way
on Boxing night to a drought-drama, as before
mentioned. The play is a new production, and
ought to shed an original light on stage rural life.
In most pastoral plays a large harvest is being
brought in, and a fat ox is driven across the
stage', and everything in the way of crops is
understood to be satisfactory the family
trouble generally arising because Susan has
hearkened to the squire’s son and erred. A
drama wherein Susan is tired and haggard, and
probably virtuous, and nobody falls except the
cattle from want of grass, should be refreshingly
realistic, and if Bland’s drama is that sort of
drama, it may be commended beforehand. A
heroine starving in Woolloomooloo instead of
London, or seeking her lost character on the
George-st. tram-line, will probably be another
touch of originality, according to advertisement.
** * *
Sydney Criterion has closed its doors till
Boxing Night, when it re-opens with a “good,
old-fashioned children’s panto.,” which will pro-
bably succeed in pleasing the average grand-
parent, as the spectacled modern child has few
associations with the columbine or the string of
sausages that gets round the policeman’s neck. If
Sheridan takes the part of Cinderella’s elder
sister and a few other people, and if some incon-
gruous coon songs are introduced, this production
will no doubt be forgiven in consideration of the
season of peace and goodwill.
*# * *
Sydney Palace opens on Boxing Night
with the well-favored “Dorothy,” sung by a
cast which suggests a performance of merit.
Besides Miss Florence Young, George Lauri, and
other favorites of the Royal Comic Opera co.,
whom it would be libellous to call “old,” but
whose merits are familiar, R. Roberts and H.
ThOrley, both new to Sydney, are announced for
singing parts. -
** * *
Last Friday night saw a crowded house at
Sydney Royal to watch the Players’ representa-
tion of Pinero’s “The Profligate,” preceded by a
curtain-raiser, “ The Captain.” Judging by the
attendances which Philip Lytton’s productions
draw, Pinero’s plays are popular in Sydney, also
the actor-manager knows enough to prevent his
amateur co. making too rude a slaughter of the
author’s work. “The Profligate” is far from
being one of Pinero’s best plays, but there is a
certain smoothness about all his work that covers
ft multitude of deficiencies. It is like a wicked
old man with lots of tact. The moral of the show
is that if you do profligate, you should profligate
with all your might, and designing mothers will
“ lay for ” you, and sweet young girls will fall into
your arms, and say they hate you. Dunstan
Renshaw had had a little affair down in the
country under an assumed name, and then came
back to town and married pure-minded, delightful
Leslie Brudenell. The religious Scotch solicitor
who loved the girl in secret, but was afraid he
wasn t _ pure and holy enough to marry her,
maintained that attitude till the reckless Renshaw
came along and took Leslie to his sinful heart.
The country lass, having had all the profit and plea-
sure of a sinful time, comes to London for revenge,
and manages to let Mrs. Renshaw know in the
presence of others that Mr. Renshaw was not an
unstained saint in the near past. Mrs. Renshaw’s
virtue revolts; but, like all the rest, she
gets over it, and fortunately gets over it soon
enough to reconcile with Renshaw before any
other girl gets hold of him.
As the Scotch attorney Lincoln Jones was
Scotchbyterian enough to have been named Ton aid
Macpherson M'Dougall; and Owen Bancroft
played the debased Renshaw in a good human way.
George Beecher made a good, disreputable but
high-class Lord Dangars. Miss Lonsdale realised
the author’s ideal of Mrs. Stonehay. Astheabsurdly
happy wife of the debased Renshaw, Mrs. Lytton
was distinctly convincing; but Miss Alice Holroyd
made the deceived Janet Preece a cat. Which
she was, but Miss Holroyd made her a raspy,
jarring cat, and Renshaw, as a man with a culti-
vated taste in sin, would surely be more careful in
selecting his cat.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli the main features of the
holiday programme will be the Florenz Co , Spry
and Monti, the Harvey Boys, and the exhibition
of sculling by champions Towns and Pearce. This
latter item serves to show very well the move-
ments necessary for skilful rowing (to get on in a
row-boat is almost purely a matter of skill,
strength being quite a secondary consideration),
and for that reason is a great attraction to the
young man with _.he burnt-sienna hands, the
roasted looking nose, and the marine-flavored
moustache.
** * *
M‘Adoo’s Jubilee Singers inaugurate a
Sydney season at the Centenary Hall on Christ-
mas night. The combination needs no elaborate
recommendation. It is a rich, mellow, melodious
party, with a streak of piety in its business that
isn’t wholly unpleasing, and it ha 3 gathered
up the ruins of many a butchered hymn
from beneath the feet of a discordant Methodist
congregation, and put it together again as good as
new. There is a wondrous toughness about these
old hymns. One can stand outside almost any
church, any Sunday, and hear the congregation
perpetrating their mangled remains, and still they
survive.
«* * *
Melbourne shows, at time of writing, are
mostly passing hence in obedience to a law of
ancient custom which requires managers to change
their bills at Xmas. Nobody can state why that
custom originated in the dim wayback, consider-
ing that a holiday and hayseed-visitor season is
obviously the most suitable of all seasons for
squeezing the last ounce of profit from an old
show. But the custom stands, and theatrical
people bow to its musty dictate.

Her Majesty’s (where “Paul Jones” ceased
to loot the Spanish Main on Saturday last) is
closed until Boxing Night. Williamson and
Musgrove have conspired to produce a panto,
of great bulk and abnormal splendor on that
annua.! occasion, and the name of their joint enter-
prise is “Richard Whittington,” commonly called
“Dick.” The company engaged for “Whitting-
ton” includes numerous people of a special de-
scription, and the production promises to be all
that the advt. claims for it. If the Grand Com-
bination panto, can possibly excel the ad. it will
do so.

At the Princess’s the last night of “A
Royal Rival ” will be Christmas Eve, and the new
Srogramme will presumably happen on Boxing
fight, although the advt., at time of writing, has

** * *
At the Royal the grand superlative
Andersonian bill for Xmas is “ The Power and
the Glory,” which four-act reminder of the Lord’s
Prayer starts its revival to-night (Wednesday).
Melbourne accepted this drama in a kindly spirit
some two or three years ago. There are points of
peculiarity about “The Power and the Glory.”
It introduces a sweet little cherub, who sits up
aloft and pops out through the London fog when
least expected. And it concludes with a trans-
formation scene in a theatre, instead of bringing
the curtain down upon a picture of the dear old
home, with a handcuffed villain in the back-
ground.
** * *
At Fitzgeralds’ Circus the entertainment
has taken a very horsey turn, at the instigation of
General Desire. The ancient equestrian drama of
“Dick Turpin’s Ride to York ” rages within the
arena and its vicinity for 45 minutes, or there-
abouts, and strikes a sympathetic audience as
being a particularly well-built narrative of its
kind. Also Barton, the buekjump rider, is putting
his “Skyscraper” through some violent exercises
whilst he (Barton) remains on top of the restless
anitnal. Ajax, the contortionist, is back at the
circus after a long absence, and several other
people are taking the places of some who went to
Adelaide last week.
The Montrose acrobats, the Harbecks, and
the admirable ballad-singing of Brownlow are
principal items at Melb. Opera House this week,
but a considerable change of bill is likely to occur
on Saturday. The Bijou is still advertising for a
tenant to follow Hawtrey. The uncle of Little
Lord Fauntleroy was to close his highly respect-
able and unlucky season on Friday of this week,
and seek for greener pastures in Tasmania.
** * *
Mdlle. Dolores, who has become identified
with the oratorio performances of Melbourne
Philharmonic Society, will sing the soprano solos
in “The Messiah” on Xmas night. “The
Messiah,” needless to say, is a Town Hall fixture.
It hasn’t been performed in the same Town Hall
for fifty consecutive Xmas nights, but it is about
to be performed for the fiftieth time by the Phil.
Society, and Dolores the Delightful will appear
as guardian angel of the jubilee. An interesting
souvenir programme, prepared for the occasion,
will differ from most souvenir programmes in
being given away gratis.

Melbourne Princess’s. All the theatrical com-

the cause, furthermore Melba has offered her
services, which amounts to a guarantee of a
crowded house at improved benefit prices. D.C.
and front stall seats, 10s.; reserved seats in the
amphitheatre, ss.

Charles Arnold undoubtedly sold his
famous Luck as well as his comedies to the
Willoughby-Geaoh management. “ Wrong Mr.
Wright” has repeated the financial success of
“Jones” every wherein Maoriland, and Willoughby
and Geach are returning to Sydney anon richer
and fatter men. So much for the “ One-play-one-
Co.” policy. With the exception of Miss Ethel
Appleton, who took Miss Hope Mayne’s place
when that tall charmer left for England some
months ago, the “Wright” co. is the same as it
was in the beginning. Consequently, “Wright”
and the other pieces are played more and more
smartly as time goes on. For farcical comedy
work there is no co. like the old 00.

At Adelaide Royal Maggie Moore has
ceased to Strike Oil. At the Tivoli Cinquevalli
has made his first appearance in Adelaide, and
the reopening of the house for a Xmas season is
very popular. Nelstone and Forrest and the
Lentons are also in the bill. Fitzgerald’s Circus
is at the Exhibition grounds, where strong-man
Page! manipulates one lion and a horse-and-a-half,

Adelaide is a much more lively place in the

There was no trouble over the second
batch of hatters who arrived under contract at
Sydney per Oruba. This time boss Anderson
tore himself away from his old reticent attitude
and applied for exemption as the law provides,
and got it promptly. He would doubtless have
got it just as promptly in the previous case had
he applied, and then all the row about the “free-
dom of British subjects” and the “Constitoosh’n”
and the “ flag ” would have been saved.
A correspondent:—
Sydney Harbor Trust has brought back the huge Ger-
man mail-boats to the west side of Circular Quay. It is
now two years ago that the bawling “ Teddy ” Clarke,
M.L.A., going home to North Shore to lunch one day,
was kept waiting half-an-hour while a 10,000-tonner
slowly drew out and allowed his ferry boat to pass. Clarke
made a row, and then Premier Lyne ordered the German
people to berth at Woolloomooloo Bay. The North Shore
folk didn’t gain much, as*the vacant wharf was promptly
allotted to the White Star co. for the Medic, Persic, etc.,
bigger, if anything, than the displaced boats. Now the
Persic, Runic, etc., go to Woolloomooloo Bay, and the
Barbarossa, Grosser Kurfurst and co. are back at the Quay.
After all, it does not matter very much whether your
ferry-boat is smashed-up by a White Star 12,000 tonner or
by a 10,000 tonner flying the German flag.
One of The Bulletin’s old friends, Rev.
E. Treinayne Dunstan, has come back to Sydney
with an “explanation” as to why he left so
suddenly. He traces his first financial entangle-
ments to W.A. After he came to Sydney—
Claims were constantly being made fcn me. I had to
meet them by borrowing, sometimes at an appalling rate
of interest. As a matter of fact, I shall have no difficulty
whatever in showing that I have paid in interest alone
more than the sum of my present indebtedness.
It would be interesting to know if E.T.D. was
plucked by Jew or Christian usurers—one of the
most greedy at the calling is an ex-Wesleyan
Sunday-school teacher, and a bond of piety might
have misled this clerical fly to his parlor. Mr.
Dunstan says that he intends to stay in Sydney
now “ until he can clear himself,” and if the Con-
gregational Union prevents him from “exercising
his ministry,” he will, in the vernacular, “go on
his own.”
Sydney D.T. wound up its humorous
report of the proceedings of the Carriage of
Newspapers Committee of Inquiry with a 2\
column “notice” last Saturday. The findings
of the committee were: That the arrange-
ments for the free carriage by train of bulk
parcels of Sydney morning newspapers were
“ highly irregular and improper ” ; that it would
have been “ a most improper thing ” for
the State Govt, to have continued them after
Federation ; that the Federal Govt, could not
have continued them without actual misappropria-
tion of public money ; that “contents sheets” of
the Sydney morning papers were illegally sent
free through the post for a number of years ;
that the newspaper managers, in trying to induce
the authorities to continue “a highly irregular
and improper” course, erred through ignorance
of its nature, and not from a contempt of the law.
This very sweeping condemnation of the free
carriage by rail of the Sydney D.T.’s parcels
occupies a half column ; then follow two columns
of a minority report by Mr. Hogue (it is a humor-
ous fact that Mr. Hogue is a newspaper man him-
self) stating that the newspaper managers were free
from all blame, and that the free railway carriage
system was a splendid one, and should not have
been disturbed. This protest by the man (the
newspaper man) out of step with the regiment is
dignified by D.T. with the heading “An Historical
Statement,” and it is also given a “leader” in
Monday’s paper—a leader which absolutely
ignores the very weighty counts of the verdict
against the newspapers by the majority of the
committee. What a valuable, thing a sense of
humor would be in the D.T. office!

The increase in the expense rate is a misfortune: still
even now the National Mutual is more cheaply managed
than the majority of offices. The interest earned is
well up to the average, and is calculated on the
total funds—depreciation reserve of £81,915 in-
eluded. It would be even better if it were
not that the funds are absurdly inflated by the
inclusion of that dubious and papery asset, “ Deferred in-
stalments of annual premiums, £57,152.” Wiiat good
that item does to any sober or respectable society it is
hard to understand, yet a good many institutions suffer
from it.
• • * •
Apart from that one point, however, the
National Mutual may be regarded as an irreproachable
institution. It is the second largest concern of its kind
in Australasia. It has a steadily growing business ; its
assets look very sound ; it maintains a substantial de-
preciation reserve (£81,915) ; the expenses are not exor-
bitant ; and the interest earned is a trifle higher than
might be expected these bad times. Altogether, the
management has every reason to feel satisfied with itself.

In addition to the Bill authorising the bor-
rowing of £4,000,000 on temporary Treasury bills at
4 per cent, the N.S.W. Government has passed through
Parliament a measure authorising the borrowing of
£3,000,000 by the issue of long-dated stock in the usual
way. As it assures the Legislature it won’t use the power
thus asked for of borrowing £7,000,000 in a heap it prob-
ably means to issue stock if possible, and Treasury bills
if stock is unsaleable. It probably means this, but still it
asks for power to borrow the whole £7,000,000, and
Boohgal is paved with good intentions.

1900.. 2403 8 „ „ 2400 8 23,891

1902.. 2407 8 „ „ 2400 7 23,904
The above list covers a period of 12 years. During
that period, without making any visible allowance
for possible depreciation of securities, the reserves
have been reduced by £22o—solely by paying divi-
dends which were not earned. In five years out
of the 12 the dividends exceeded earnings. Once the
reserve was encroached upon in order to pay an exor-
bitant 15 per cent, dividend, and twice to pay an almost
equally exorbitant 10 per cent. Theseare old stories, and
it wouldn’t be worth while referring to them again if the
management showed serious signs of reformation, but a
visible addition of £lB to the reserves in four years is not
serious reformation. Moreover, the intense accuracy
with which the National Trustees earns its divi-
dend, and from £2 to £7 over, leaves a possibility
of two different kinds of suspicion; One is that a special
effort has to be made each year to show sufficient profit
to pay the dividend—in which case the dividend should
be reduced. Another is that only enough profit is shown
to cover the dividend, while a remainder (amount un-
known) is being eet aside to write down depreciation


(amount also unknown) of assets. In that case the share-
holders should be taken into the confidence of the
management and told the exact position of affairs.
There may be no ground whatever for either injurious
suspicion, but if there isn’t, the coincidence by which the
earnings vary no more than £5 in four years is simply
marvellous.

The Tasmanian Wool-Growers’ Agency Co.
(Launceston, Tas.) presents a splendidly prosperous
balance-sheet for the year which closed on 80th September,
19 >2. It is in a position to pay a dividend of 25 per cent.,
with dividend tax and bonus’to staff ; adds considerably
to its reserves; and closes the year with total reserves of
£12,098, as against a paid-up capital of only £B5OO.
• « • •
The South Australian Ostrich Co. (paid-up
capital £12,269) was registered 16j years ago, and the
swift-footed bird has kept so far ahead of the share-
holders that not one dividend has been paid, nor have the
dir -tors received any fees. Now it is stated that the
la . lialf-year “ has not been at all satisfactory." When
last heard of, the co. had 490 birds on its Port Augusta
farm. The tail of the fowl has been fetching unprece-
dently low prices of late.
* • • •
Statistician Coghlan estimates the N.S.W.
wheat crop for 1902 at 1,411,219 bushels (36 bushels to
every 10 acres). This is about one-tenth of the yield for
1901, and the smallest return since 1871. Still it is only
an estimate, and as at least one State (S.A.) has a surplus
to sell, things may not be so bad as they look. But if
they are as bad as they look, the remedy is for the State
Government to import sufficient to fill the gap (when it
imports, and pays the duty to the Australian Government,
and gets the money back again at the monthly division of
the surplus, the result is the same as if the duty was
abolished). Then it can sell at a reasonable figure—not
at cost, for to depress the price of the farmer’s little crop
to the lowest possible figure would just about complete
his ruin—and let the profits be applied towards public
works. This will eliminate the middleman. There is a
great row about the duty, but the middleman'* stealings
amount to much more than the duty.
* « • •
“The Fisheries, Limited,” nominal capital
£5OOO, is being formed. The office is at Gladstone Cham-
bers, 90 Pitt-street, Sydney, and there is a wholesale
depot at 104 Hunter-street. The co. proposes to open
shops for the sale of fresh wholesome fish in great variety
—not only N.S.W. fish, but many other varieties —
throughout Sydney and suburbs, and the speculation
ought to yield great dividends. There is no bigger want
to be filled in Sydney than an abundant supply of all
kinds of fish of a quality that is above suspicion.

The reserves consist of reserve fund, £33,150 ; reserve for

£1500; and undivided profit, £2073. It is a pity the co.
doesn’t consolidate these and start with a reserve fund of

ment on some past records, only represent £2 9s. 3d. per
cent, on the shareholders’ funds as at the commence-
ment of the year. When a co. is doing no better than
this in a general way, it should pass its dividend if the
earnings in any one year are not equal to paying the
usual 3 per cent.—not draw on a dividend equalisation
reserve or on any other reserve. A dividend equalisation
reserve is a superfluity under such conditions.
Still, as already mentioned, the earnings show a
g*-eat improvement, and a further good point is that the
liabilities at the same time have neen greatly reduced.

The prospectus of a Melbourne Civil Service
Co-operative Store has been issued, and if civil servants
are not too tired and impecunious to seize the opportunity
there should be no trouble in disposing of the 20,000
shares. Proposed that all transactions shall be for cash
only. It was on a purely cash basis that the original
Civil Service Co-op. Store made its gorgeous success.

The Castle Salt Co. (S.A.), which works the
dry lakes on Yorke’s Peninsula, has declared a shilling
dividend, the first of its career. Co. claims to have
circulated locally well over £lOO,OOO in its 4J years of
existence.
• * • •
The Broken Hill share market has fluttered in
harmony with the alternating hopes and fears concerning
the new zinc process. And at time of writing nothing
more definite can be said about that process than that it
is a complete laboratory success. In practice success
will depend to a great extent on whether the salt-
cake solution can be effectively used over and
over again. The most powerful agent in salt-cake is
sulphuric acid ; and that costs from £8 to £lO per ton.
Schnabel knew the sulphuric acid process well,
but the cost blocked him. Even without the improve-
ment in this process made by Delprat and his scien-
tific assistant, Carmichael, the situation as to sul-
phuric acid is a good deal changed. The Moonta
and Wallaroo Copper Co. is now producing over
100 tons a week ; and it is probable that the B.H.
Prop, could make its own acid. A little while back much
was said about the Oarmichael-Bradford desulphurising
process. Well, one of the things that process does is to
drive off the sulphur in the form of acid fumes,
and it was proved that these could be condensed.
The co., however, adopted the Huntington-Heberlein
desulphurising process, and experiments are now being
made to see whether the acid can be saved in this
process, too. If it can, the new zinc treatment
will get a big lift. Meantime, the co’s ; that have
the magnetic zinc-separation processes in hand are
not alarmed Their strength lies in the fact
that they don’t waste any chemicals. After three
years’ continuous experimenting on a big scale they claim
a practical success ; and there is the best of evidence that
they really believe so in the fact that a complete
new plant has been built in Germany, and is
now on the water. With these undoubtedly impor-
tant developments, with the large savings effected by
the desulphurising and sintering processes lately adopted,
with a moderate rainfall ending the fears of a water
famine; and with a lead market which, though still
weak, is a trifle more healthy-looking than it was, the
B H. Prop, closes a dismal year in comparative cheerful-
ness. The most offensive fly in its ointment is the
shockingly low price of silver and the apparent hopeless-
ness of any substantial recovery.
* • * *
Sulphide Corp. (8.H.) shares would have im-
proved more but for a rumor that there is another
reconstruction in the air. Possibly; a co. which buys
ore to smelt can very soon make or drop a pot of money.
But it is at least as likely in this case that, if more capital
is called, it will be to save bankers’ charges. The co. draws
against shipments of bullion, and it has long growled at
the price charged for the accommodation ; so it wants
to do its own three-ball business. If money is raised it
will probably be £lOO,OOO in debentures, part to be used
to provide zinc-plant at the mine.

• • m *
Lake George Mines (N.S.W.) chairman con-
soled shareholders, in London, the other day, by telling
them that his investigation satisfied him that as far back
as 1896 “ the mine was worked out from a paying point of
view.” This was the year in which the present co. was
formed. It paid £lOO,OOO for the mine, spent £95,000 in
developing it, and realised £151,524 from products, all of
which went back into the mine without any return being
made to shareholders. The chairman says that the mine
was so honeycombed with prospecting drives that there
ought to have been no difficulty at the outset
in determining that it could not be made a
payable proposition. Some of the experts who reported
favorably on the mine may challenge the assertion ; they
can suggest all sorts of excuses. But what explanation
can be offered by the financiers who ran the show, who
cajoled the speculating public into the belief that it
would be payable, and boomed the valueless shares to
over £3 ?
* * • •
The Shuttleton copper mine near Nymagee
(N.S.W.) has been formed into a co. called the Crowl
Greek. The prospectus would indicate that there has
been no exaggeration in the information respecting the
show which has appeared in this column. The figures
read like a fairy tale. The prospectors, as the result of a
few months' work, sold 845 tons of ore averaging 26.6 per
cent, and from March l to Oct. 31 the Melbourne syndicate
consigned 2813 tons, averaging 33.5 per cent. Assuming
that during Nov. and Dec. it disposed of the same
average quantity and quality the record for 10 months
development work will be the sale of over 3500 tons
of ore, containing nearly 1200 tons of copper,
of a gross value of about £60,0001 On 31st Oct.
there was at grass 5678 tons of “seconds,” averaging
9.8 per cent., and in the mine there was ready for stoping,
above the 170 ft. level, 22,000 tons of 8£ per cent. ore.
Since then the lode has been intersected at 240 ft., where
it is solid sulphide, assaying 20.8 per cent, for a width of
18ft. This is the show which the lucky syndicate got
hold of, on a working option, for £30,000 (the preliminary
deposit being only £1), the agreement practically pro-
viding that the money was to be earned out of the mine.
Including the neb value of ore at grass, the mine has
more than accomplished this.
• * • •
The Crovrl Creek Co. consists of 105,000 £1
shares, which is a modest capitalisation for a mine that
appears to have prospects equalling, if not rivalling, those
of the Great Cobar of the early days. Out of the 105,000
shares, 55,000 paid-up, and 25,000 contrib. paid to 25., are
to be issued to the syndicate ; 25,000 contrib. shares are
offered at a premium of 2s. 6d. on application and 2s.
(part of the capital) on allotment. This is merely a
nominal public offer, as syndicate shareholders
had the preferential right to take up the shares,
which, quite naturally, they exercised, except as
to 2500 shares, which were made available to out-
siders—soo each in Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide,
Brisbane and Launceston. To the uninitiated it may
seem an extraordinary thing that men having control of
such a remarkably good show should allow even 2500
shares to pass out of their hands, on such terms. But
the initiated will readily discern what it may mean.
There may be nothing uncommon about it. It may
signify preparation for a market move to enable the
syndicate to get rid of their contrib. shares at a big
figure. There may be the usual “pool” arrange-
ment, and, if so, it is a pool which appears
to have something unusual at the back of it—
a solid mine. To “ make ” a market for shares
there must be . a certain number not locked
up, and it is always advisable, in the interests of the
pool, that these shares should be in outsiders’ hands
for the preliminary “playing.” So far, all seems most
satisfactory for the syndicate. There is a great mine ;
account sales-which never by any mischance exaggerate
values, but may include extortionate charges—testify to
that; impartial, qualified experts unhesitatingly pro-
claim it; there is no problematical “ if,’’ no difficulty that
can be suggested ; the syndicate “stands on velvet,” and
it has made all arrangements for a grand coup.
But one thing has been overlooked : The mine is
in N.S.W. And no successful N.S.W. mine ever
got into smooth water without first of all being
buffetted hither and thither in a typhoon of law.
Growl Creek is not likely to be any exception to the rule,
unless wise counsels and a spirit of compromise prevail.
As indicated in last week’s Bulletin the law courts may
be invoked to declare who are members of the syndicate ;
that, however, would not affect the co., and need not,
necessarily, interfere with the accomplishments of the
coup. But a serious dispute has arisen between the
prospectors and the syndicate as to the interpretation of
the agreement under which the mine was sold, and if this
has to be settled by reference to the law courts it may be
many a day before any market scheme can be
worked. Terms of sale were £30,000 cash, one-fifth
paid up interest in a co. having a working capital
of not less than £50,000 £5OOO of which had to
be in the bank on the registration of the co.—this
being so much surplusage in the agreement as the law
provides for it. The prospectus, after stating that £5OOO
will be placed to the credit of the co., goes on:—“The
balance of working capital—namely £45,000 —will be
payable in calls when required, and the products of the
mine may be applicable to its development." This
italicised provision, the prospectors state, is not con-
tained in the agreement, and they contend that it is a
distinct violation of the spirit, if not the letter, of the
contract; as they interpret it, it makes their so-called
paid-up shares no more valuable than the ordinary edn-
trib. shares. So far, each side is obstinate in its assertion
of right. There are other points on which there is
difference of opinion, and the present outlook is that
Shuttleton will be a shuttlecock with which lawyers will
have much pleasurable and profitable diversion.

The “cementation,” or sea-water leaching, of
the old tailings and slimes, which was to level up the
Moonta and Wallaroo Copper Co.’s profits for the next six
years, has not been doing quite so well lately ; the stuff
has settled down too solid.
• * • •
Berry Consols Extended (Vic.), which was a
“ stringer ” for many years, and was so much of a hope-
deferred show that it sickened most of the original share-
holders, looks like soon balancing the ledger. The total
expenditure on the mine has been £318,470, wages
amounting to £156,309 ; gold has been produced to the
value of £214,314, and £75,000 was called up. To date
£41,000 has been paid in divs., last half-year accounting
for £9OOO, and it is expected that the call account will be
wiped out within the next 18 months.
The chairman of the Stannary Hills Tin Co.
evidently approves of the financial philosophy of Mi-
cawber ; the co. negotiates a p.n. to meet a liability, and
its head exultantly exclaims, “ Thank God, that is
settled.” He told the recent meeting that the “ antici-
pation of the board that they would not have to call
further on the shareholders had been realised, temporary
advances having been obtained on reasonable terms."
Stannary Hills may be a good mine, but it will have to be
equal to fancy’s painting of it to make sufficient profit
to wipe out the co.’s indebtedness.

Tom Doyle, ex-Mayor of Kanowna (W. A.), has
just treated another lot of 18 tons of stone from his rich
leader for 2720 z. This makes the total for the year
20640 z., worth £7430, from 72 tons. The tributors, who
are working the main reef on the property, into which
the rich leader dips, got 98oz. from their first parcel of
19 tons, and expect to get 130 to 140 oz. from the second
lot of about 60 tons.
• • • *
At the meeting in London of East Fingall
(W.A.), which holds 36 acres on the east of the Great
Fingall, nothing consolatory was laid before subscribers.
The capital consists of £BO,OOO, in 320,000 shares of 6s.
each. Out of the total, 80,000 shares were set aside to
provide £20,000 working capital, hut only 26,000, pro-
viding £6500, have been subscribed for. Chairman
assured shareholders that the shaft was to he sunk to
1000 ft., and that a new battery and cyanide plant were
to be installed out of profits. Vendors were alleged to
have treated 1000 tons for £6400 from above the 70ft. level,
where they were drowned out. At this depth the mine was
said to be quite as rich as its undoubtedly rich
neighbor, the Great Fingall. It is safe to predict that
this co. will be on the reconstruction list before many
months expire.
• m * *
The latest return from the Great Oversight,
Bulong, W.A., brings up total gold won from this line
during the year to 13,2410 z. from 1587 tons. The gold
realised about £52,000, all of which, except treatment
charges,-went into the pockets of working miners. At
time of the find, a year ago, as the gold was near the
surface, the Minister of Mines refused to grant leases, and
the ground was cut up into small holdings. The original
lease from which this gold has been won was pegged out
in the early days by jingle-writer “ Dryblower ” of
Kalooorme: Sun, who made nothing out of his venture
owing to the ore being covered over with alluvial soil.
Captain Truscott, the manager of the Adelaide co. which
acquired the show, wanted to treat the whole lot in a
battery, but was vetoed by the directors. Had his advice
been followed, the rich ore would probably have been
found seven years ago.
* * * *
Queen Margaret, Bulong (W.A.), is still plod-
ding along on the original leases, without encountering
anything specially encouraging at the 700 ft. level. On
the new property, the S. Gippsland, at Vosperton, a drive
is being put in along the wall of the “sugar” reef, the
stone being left standing meantime. A pump is being
fixed in the shaft to drain it, and as soon as the water is
got under control, driving on the ree;' at the 370 ft. level
will be started. The future of the co. hinges entirely on
how this property turns out, and the management appears
pretty confident as to the result.
• * • •
North Kalgurli, which was reconstructed in
July last year with a nominal capital of 350,000 shares
with a 2s. 6d. assessment, is again getting to the end of
its tether. Of this assessment but 6d. remains to be
called, and, if fully met, this means only £8750, which
will not go far in development, not to mention the
new equipment suggested by the management. Recently
most of the exploration work has been confined to the
100 ft. and 600 ft. levels, and some fair ore has been met
with in both places. All the same, judging from the
enormous amount of money sunk on the adjoining shows,
such as the South, without tangible results, he would
indeed be an optimist who would predict success with the
small amount now available. The heavy decline in the
value of shares indicates that the market fully recognises
the situation.
• • • *
South Kalgurli is being subjected to an inspec-
tion by Boulder Hamilton, in order to try and set at rest
the doubts of shareholders. 00. is reported to have debts
amounting to £7OOO, but, as an offset, is said to have
stores valued at £9OOO and gold on plates and “ else-
where ” valued at £lO,OOO. The stores item, though very
high, may possibly be correct, but it is hardly likely that
any large quantity of gold is on the plates. These plates
are removable, and extra ones are kept to immediately
replace those withdrawn from the vats. They are taken
out at frequent intervals, and leisurely cleaned up with-
out any stoppage of the Reicken process. When manager
Isles’ locum tenens was driven to his wits’ end to main-
tain the returns, it is safe to say the plates were scrubbed
as bare as possible. Reserves of pay ore are given at two
years' supply on basis of present output, or about 90,000
tons. If this is true the difficulty in maintaining the
output is inexplicable. Hamilton’s report will be
anxiously looked for. # *
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) has at last got fairly
going, and it is quite on the cards that co. may join the
div. -payers early in the new year. Manager Loreing has
had to use the pruning-knife freely, as, for example,
the staff of accountants has been reduced from ten to
four, and storekeepers from six to two. All other depart-
ments were officered with equal lavishness. The lode
to the north in the lower levels continues unsatisfactory,
though going south it carries a lot of ore which should
prove payable on the very much reduced scale of costs
introduced by Loreing.

There is a strong agitation in London mining
circles in favor of modifications of the W.A. mining laws
in the way of lessened labor-conditions, reduced dividend
tax, and the removal of various “ impositions,” which are
alleged to be a serious handicap on the industry. Also,
at the bottom of this agitation is an intended combine of
forces, with a view to an all-round reduction of wages.
It does not seem to strike the agitators that the greatest
handicap on the industry is their own abnormal greed—
as is shown in a statement of “ office expenses ” of various
co’s for the year: Horseshoe, £ll,OOO ; Boulder, £9200 ;
I van hoe, £6700 ; Lake View, £6500 ; Associated, £6OOO ;
Associated Northern, £5900; Perseverance, £4BOO ;
Fingall, £3200. The bulk of these amounts represent
directors’ fees—and what a cheeky, ignorant, useless lot
is the average London mine-director !

• « • •
The Jewhannesburg mme-labor trouble, of which
the cables have given us a tired hint or two, is a mixed
affair. Formerly one white miner bossed two drills and
10 Kaffirs. Now, the war having delivered crowds of
unskilled white laborers into their hands, the co.’s want
the miner to boss three machines, and, for each machine,
a white “ helper ” and gang of four. Some of the miners
took it on, but most of them soon found that the “ game ”
was to use the “ helper ” to cut down the skilled man’s
pay, and a strike followed—as much of a strike, that is,
as there can be with the men unorganised. The skilled
machine-man’s pay has been big, but in the advanced
leadings especially the work has been deadly—bad venti-
lation and drill-dust knock out the strongest in four or
five years.
• • • *
Apropos the deadliness of the Rand mines,
through bad ventilation and machine-drill dust. After
the '92 strike at Broken Hill eight strapping young
Cornish-Australian miners moved out to S’Africa. Word
came through a month or so back that the last of them
was dead. And of the miners who returned to Cornwall
when the war started 600 have since died there from
“ miner’s phthisis.” The Jewhannesburg Chamber of
Mines is now offering a reward of £5OO for the suppression
of the drill-dust nuisance.
• * * •
Copper continues low in the face of conditions
which, under ordinary circumstances, would favor high
values, such as increased consumption, reduced produc-
tion, and the falling off of stocks ; in fact, the position is
remarkable, and no newspaper seems able to account for it
(writes a London correspondent). The shares of the
Amalgamated Co. have fallen considerably. One yarn is
that the big copper men of America are the buyers of big
things in London, for which big money is to be paid
shortly, and it takes them all their time to raise it with-
out creating a slump in New York. Another yarn is that
the Amalgamated Co. continues to break down the price
of copper to bring about another and much larger com-
bine. The copper men who kept out of the last combine
and made such a lot of money by doing so, are slow in
coming in, but the general opinion is that they will be
forced in sooner or later.

The Monet Market Review, London, writes

« • » *
The North Gippsland Proprietary Company,
Grant, have been engaged for many months past driving
a long tunnel in very hard country. The tunnel is now
some 2500 ft. in length. The last 1500 feet has been
driven with the aid of the Gardner Electric Rock Drill
which has given the manager every satisfaction ; only
one drill has been in use and it is still working in as good
condition as when first started. About 35 feet of drilling
is done by it every shift, so the installation of the Electric
Drill must be regarded as in every way successful. The
drill requires less than two horse power to drive it as
against at least 10 horse power required for an air driven
drill doing the same work, besides dispensing with com-
pressor, receivers and air pipes. The fact that this
Electric Drill has been working so satisfactorily in one
of the most inaccessible parts of Victoria is sufficient
proof that the plant is not liable to derangement. The
sole Agents for the Gardner Electric Drill are the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, South Melbourne, who will
be pleased to answer all inquiries.*

In 10 years the number of mining co’s. floated
in Great Britain was 2979, having a nominal capital of
£453,400,000 ; 244 “ made some return to the share-
holders,” and 2375 gave no return. In the four years
preceding 1900 no less than 1148 co’s. (capital £123,000,000)
went into liquidation. It is stated that “ the chief cause
of these failures has been the payment of excessive com-
mission to promoters, the effect being to make England
a bad market for good ventures.”
“Burglar Bill ” on the elevating influence
of N.S.W. gaol-life:—
Much has been written lately on the reforming influ-
ence of our modern gaol treatment, but old-time warders
will tell you that the prisoners under the old system,
although rough as regards methods of expressing dis-
satisfaction with their treatment, were withal manly.
A “smooger,” ready to carry information to the
governor re fellow-prisoners or warders, was of
yore an exception; now he is the rule. The
new system encourages the “ pimp,” the higher
officials of the gaol encourage him ; under the
old system a spy would receive as his reward “ seven days
dark ” (many of the old-fashioned gaol governors would
give it to him now), but the new governors under the
new system protect him and he becomes a model show
prisoner of the Uriah Heep order. As instancing the
lack of manliness in prisoners, I have seen a certain high
official, during “ lock-up” search, place half-a-crown in the
hands of three different prisoners, giving them instruc-
tions to hold the coin between their fingers during the
search. In two cases out of three the unfortunate
warders omitted to search the outstretched hands and
were promptly reprimanded, while the prisoner, after this
lesson in manliness, had the half-crown taken from him
and was locked up for the night. Surely self-respect has
entirely left men who sing to a lady visitor selections
from hymns specially picked for them ! The “ smooger”
rules ; the rebel, 100 per cent, abetter man, is persecuted
because he cannot bend himself to the cringing methods
that stamp the “model prisoner.” Certainly everyone
must admit, that the gaols of N.S.W. were never in a
better state as regards order than now, but the elevating
influence had better be spoken of in a whisper; it can
hardly- bear a strong breath.

SOCIEIY
THE FORGIVENESS OF JONES.
[For Tiib Bulletin.]
Now, ’tis the holy Christmas morn
When Earth draws nigh to Heaven ;
Yet sadly here I walk in fear
That I may be Forgiven.
Last spring I owned a noble friend ;
Our hearts did close entwine.
Our bite and sup was from one cup—
That cup was always Mine.
A soul of haughtiness and pride
Was Jones’s lifelong curse.
To mitigate this fault so great
He vowed to share my purse.
He borrowed with a prinoely mien
(As one that played a part);
But when no more I’d yield my store
He thrust me from his heart.
Tis three months since my comrade wept
His high ideal fled ;
My pay-day’s due—l whisper you—
This merry morn I dread l
I fear the softening mood of Jones
May urge “ ’Tis Christmas Time,
When wandering sheep and villains deep
Are whitened of their crime 1
"This is the season sinful maids
Are pardoned by their sires 1
To show a mind as broad and kind
My soul, though proud, aspires !
“ No more this erring man I’ll shun ”
(His voice will falter here).
" I’ll seek him out; I’ll let him shout;
And—yes—l’ll drink his beer 1 "
And so this morn of peace and Joy
My heart a tremor owns ;
I fear to meet in road or street
The pardoning smile of Jones.
O. Cltdh.
WITHOUT THE FRAME.
Auctioneer : “ What, gentlemen I fifteen shillings
only I am, offered ? Why, the picture a ' one
is worth that money.”
It is a solemn, sad, sorrowful fact that
the rain came to Australia at last just when no
sect was holding special services about it. For
years past there have been periodical days of
prayer and days of humiliation, and days of
special clamor, and the mocking heavens still
hung like a fried gallows over our heads, and the
earth was like a hob under our feet. The sects
seemed at last to give it up ; anyhow no one has
heard for quite a long time about special days of
prayer. Then the rain came like a damp, dis-
creditable slanderer in the night and a wet back-
biter at noon. It came as an aspersion on the
sects and the creeds, and as a mockery and an
insult. It fell on the bishop’s palace and the
vicarage and the presbytery and the manse like the
howl of a lost opportunity. No church had
done anything sufficiently special for months to
enable it to claim, with any fair prospect of be-
lief, that it brought about the downpour. The
church had been too busy looking into its own
diminished rain-barrel to do anything. And,
therefore, a chance such as comes but once in a
dozen ye ars is Gone, like the kitten that ate too
many locusts last year and gave up the ghost-
gone as completely as Premier John See’s chance
of borrowing £10,000,000 at 3 per cent.—gone like
Bent s manners and Pompey’s bones, and other
utterly lost-and-gone things that are too gone to
be even remembered.
* * » *
The Cardinal comes down on the bubble
and squeak of Dill-Mackyism like a heavy damper.
It is an unholy wave of agitation, says he,
? n P ass * Which is true enough; but
it will make a good many things wet and uncom-
fortable ere the passing is accomplished. And it
might never have arisen in its present shape and
face had the Cardinal at all times and seasons
kept a little closer to the exact truth ; or when he
found that a mistake had been made, evinced a
more civilised readiness of apology.

To The Bulletin's queries to Dr. Dill-
Macky:—
Should Catholics have a vote at all ? Should Catholics
be admitted to Parliament, to the Ministrv, and to the
Public Service?
An anonymous correspondent an Dill-Macky’s
paper, The Watchman, comes forward with a
reply which is a complete exposure of the true
nature of the so-called “Protestant Defence”
movement. The Watchman writer says :
To give Romans a vote is a dangerous absurdity. . . .
Cromwell was logical when he treated the Romanists as
enemies of the State. . . . The Roman Catholic in Aus-
tralia is really an alien. ... No R.G. should be allowed
in the Education Department.
That 8 quite straightforward. People can now
Bee what is aimed at—disfranchisement of R.C.’s,
and then, perhaps, the logical step of a wholesale
Cromwellian massacre of them.
* • • ■
The latest development of what may be
called sectarian nationalism in Melbourne is a
North of Ireland Association. Pretty well all
the well-fed inhabitants of Australian cities, who
don’t call themselves English, are members of
some national sect or other, and even the English-
man hreaks out into Cornish and Yorkshire and
Lancashire societies, though it is mostly the out-
side corners of Bull’s country which do that sort
of thing. By the way, when is the A.N.A. going
to break into bitter rival sections ?
• * • •
Lo, the poor parson in the thickest of the
hatter battle ! Canon Boyce, none other, full of
fear and trembling for the glorious Empire and
the blessed Church. If the hatters are blocked,
asks the Canon, how will bishops, priests, and
deacons fare ? Poor Canon ! He has never read
the Act, nor yet the facts of the case which has
arisen under it. Bishops do not labor with their
hands, albeit they wear aprons. Deans, priests,
and deacons know nothing of manual labor,
though they may sport shovel hats ; and the
truculent canon goes off in a strangely-drunken
fashion, though strong drink and he are and ever
have been strangers. He has some brethren on
the daily press similarly explosive just now.
* * * *
Admiral Harry Rawson has all the ad-
vantage of a man who has made up his mind on
everything. Not about fleets and squadrons and
Trinity House only, but in those higher matters
where philosophers ponder and divines wrangle, he
is calm, assured, positive. “Countries which do
not re3peet religion do not prosper.” That’s the
way to get the Doubt-Devil in a bow-line. And
yet, Admiral Harry, there are countries called
Spain and Austria, and—lreland, where religion
is respected up to the limits of possibility, and yet
the corresponding degree of prosperity is not very
plain. And there are others —France, whiehsofar
dissembles its respect as to kick the whole box of
tricks out of doors; and U.S. A., which makes the
Cathedral its easy-chair and the chapel its foot-
stool, and they seem to prosper beyond the
average.

A Bulletin correspondent :
Saw a beautiful example of Railway-Minister Bent’s
benign rule at a big Melbourne suburban station yester-
day. The staff has been sr curtailed that one porter sells
tickets, collects tickets at the gate, and sees trains safely
off the premises every 10 minutes. At lunch-time yester-
day, this pampered official had to quit his booking-office
with seven people still clamoring for tickets, and he hadn’t
time to shut the gate to rope-in the passengers who left
the train, so 20 or more thus escaped. In his rush to
prevent any more from getting through, he had to let the
train pass out with five of the carriage-doors swinging
open—a danger to people on the platform and passengers
inside. Thomas might be able to get some more of his
Brighton election-committee to report, at £1 a day, upon
the dastardly neglect of his duties by the suburban porter
m question.

organ of Pastor George Walters’ Australian
Church, Sydney, prints this :
Scene—The 1.0.0. P. Temple.
Time—Sunday evening Lantern Lecture.
Act 1: Enter two working men, one in soft shirt, and
with no collar.
Each one says “ Thank you ” for copy of Modern
Thought, and each contributes sixpence to the offertory
Act 2 : Enter Leading Citizen, accompanied by his wife
and two young ladies.
Each takes a copy of Modern Thought, but forgets, in
the excitement, to say “ Thank you 99 ; and while Leading
Citizen contributes threepence, the ladies enter upon the
strength of the same—an average of three farthings each.

Letter written to the local orphan asylum
committee by a casual widow of Ballarat:—
Dear Sirs,—l just write you a line in regard to marry-
ing. I have the offer of marriage, but the gentleman
that wants to marry me does not want to be held respon-
sible in keeping my children, but he will marry me if you
are willing to grant what he wants to do, and'that is he
will give the orphanage the sum of £3O not to be held
responsible after. So, dear sirs, I hope you will consider
the matter.—Yours truly, .
Talk about the beauty and durability of maternal
affection !

* • • •
Be the fate of churches (“Nardo,” 8.,
29/11/’O2). In the fashionable Melbourne
suburb of Hawthorn is a church which was
bought outright, a few years ago, by a local
pastry-cook, and is now used by him as his
bakehouse and storeroom. The church—a
large brick building—lias a memorial stone,
with the words “This stone was laid by
Hon. W. F. Walker, M.L.A., Minister of
Customs, 25th Oct., 1886,” inscribed in
large gilt letters. The church’s notice-
board is still standing at the side,
giving the times of service, weather-
stained, but still readable. The pastry-
cook has added a wooden building on to the back
of the church, whereupon the public is informed
(in large black letters) that “catering is a
speciality,” and that “ goods are lent on hire,” &c.

There is to be litigation over Sydney’s
garbage-destructor—and some interesting muni-

Some N.S.W. in-
sane figures. Causes of
madness in 1901 (out of
total of asylum admis-
sions and re-admissions,
771): Alcoholic intem-
perance, 74 ; worry and
overwork, 27 ; religious ex-
citement, 14 ; opium, 5 ; love,
7 ; vice (of various sex sorts)
23. These are the causes for
which the patient is, so to
speak, responsible, and leave
out of the question epilepsy,
brain-disease, etc. There
are 4272 persons in N.S.W.
asylums. Unmarried men
seem to be twice as liable
to insanity as married
men ; unmarried
women only exceed
married ones in the in-
sane asylums by 10 per
cent. Proportionately to
population, Roman Catholics
predominate in the N.S.W.
asylums. Nearly 50 per cent,
of the cases are other than
N.S.W. natives. As regards occupations, miners,
laborers, seamen and shepherds contribute 1570
to the total of insane persons, domestic servants
633, “no occupation ” 700, commercial men 223,
agriculturists and pastoralists 183, professional
men only 70.

they could get at such rubbish and pelt it with
old bottles and jam-tins.

A WOMAN’S WAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Waiting before the judgment stand
Two souls—the woman’s and the man’s—
The Recording Angel reads aloud :
“ A glimpse of happiness had she,
Years of neglect and misery.
The cause —the man before you bowed.”
The woman pleads : “ Oh God ! like me.
Blot out the years of misery ;
Remember only I owe to him
The little happiness I knew
On earth—his love—ah God 1 *tis true.”
They pass unseen ; God’s eyes are dim.
H. E.

Branch : 93 QUEEN STREET, BRISBANE.


Queensland spends £2O 16s. per head to
educate the superior youths of a certain grammar
school against an average of about £3 10s. on each
State school pupil throughout the State. And out
in the Q. bush there are hundreds of towzled,
unkempt kiddies running wild for lack of the
elements of education. “No money for fresh
provisional schools ” sounds queer to anybody
who knows that the Q. Government has spent no
less than £275,000 on secondary education.
• * • #
An indignant legislator was holding forth
on the wrongs of Queensland female teachers who
have to go into the heart of the lonely bush to
take charge of provisional schools. It was, how-
ever, urged in defence of the system that female
provisional school teaohers got married more
rapidly than any other class in the community ;
that tne more lonely the district the more likely
they were to get settled down, and that, from one
school on the Warrego, no less than eight female
teachers had got married in three years. That
settled all the talk about the alleged grievance.
* * * *
“Alba’* speaks quite decidedly on the
school-marm marrying question :
The wail of “ A.0.H.” (Bulletin, Oct. 30, ’O2) as to the
small percentage of female teachers who marry, with the
reason given—their lack of opportunity to display their
charms —is ludicrous. What class of women has more holi-
days wherein to find, if so disposed, opportunities to charm
man? Asa matter of fact, the average “school-marm’*
is too wary to give up a good salary and plenty of holi-
days to settle down to a wash-and-scrub, cook-and-clean,
mind-the-baby-in-between existence. She objects to
becoming a Mary Ann, without even the privileges of
that lady. When men learn to treat their wives as wives,
and not as domestic servants, there will be no reluctance
on the part of the “ school-marm ” or any other “ marm ”
to marry ; neither will there be any need for The Bulletin
to make its nose red weeping over a falling birth-rate.
* • * *
It would be interesting to know who are
the actual proprietors of the Public School
teachers’ paper, known as the N.S.W. Educa-
tional Gazette. It is supposed to be owned by
a committee, who are stated to be mostly officials
connected with the Education Department. There
is nothing in this, of course, but if the paper is
oibsidised by the Department, then there is reason
for inquiry.
ACCOMMODATION WANTED.
R[?]DE NEWSBOY “Say, Mister, lend us ycr
stum[?]mick for the day, I’m goin ’ to the noose-
boys' picnic.”
One of Melbourne’s alien population—a
Sicilian dealer in fruit —was sued by a compatriot
last week on account of sundry libellous utter-
ances in Sicilian dialect. Under cross-examina-
tion, defendant’s amanuensis admitted to having
written one letter in “verse” to the following
effect: —“You are nothing but a pure miserable.
You are like a stunted mullet. You ought to
dispose of yourself, having a face like a dead dog.
I don’t want to say any more, as I could filth you
too much. I do not prolong this poetry any
longer, as everybody knows you are a heap of
filth.” The bards of modern Italy appear to draw
their inspiration from decayed fruit and shell-
fish.
* • • •
k recent Melbourne sensation was the
sudden going mad of a young man of 26 while
receiving a music-lesson. He was described by
the lady who taught him as a most talented and
promising pupil, and everything went smoothly
till he tried to choke his teacher in the middle of
the lesson. Then he shattered her establishment
with an axe, raging from room to room and
3miting with a fury that in five minutes left hardly
a breakable unbroken in the wljole premises ; set
the establishment on fire; howled several times,
and finally fled on his bicycle. Music-teaching is
none too good a game at any time. When
demented pupils wreck the establishment it is no
game at all.
Writes “ Ban ” :
Maoriland means Prohibition. Recent Option polls
resulted in a wholesale victory for the Pump party.
Clutha, the one dry district in M.L., retains No-License
by 2100 to 1300; Ashburton, Port Chalmers, Mataura,
Bruce, and Newtown (suburb of Wellington) have refused
further license, and 11 other districts, including godly
Dunedin, have reduced the number of existing licenses.
The Prohibition vote in total exceeds that for Continu-
ance by 13,000 for the whole country, though the former
has been weakened by the two issues open—Reduction
and No-License. Three-fifths of the total number of
voters must vote for No-License to carry the proposal in
any district, but Reduction can be carried by a bare
majority. “ The Trade ” have only themselves to blame
for the results of this poll. Their continual and bare-
faced defiance of the laws regulating their business has
disgusted most of the decent people in M.L. Universal
Prohibition is confidently prophesied by Cold Water
enthusiasts at next poll. At present the Prohibition
vote is reckoned a very strong factor in M.L. politics.
In Christchurch the champion of beer, W. _W.
Collins, was beaten, although returned at previous
elections ; the Prohibition candidates were returned
first and second respectively. The R.C. party fight
for the trade tooth and nail, and say that under
Prohibition they won’t be able to get any wine for Mass,
or something of the sort. The R.C.’s plumped for
liquor-man Collins, although he is the Freethought
orator of Christchurch. In a case like this, beer is
thicker than religion. Probably the whole liquor diffi-
culty could be solved by nationalising the trade, and
thereby putting it into law-abiding and disinterested
hands.
To which latter clause The Bulletin saya
“ Amen ! ”
• • • •
A couple of years ago, when the military
microbe pervaded Australia, a returned cohen-
tingenter taking his seat in a theatre was a sight
for the gods. He generally strode slowly down
the aisle, chose his seat with much deliberation,
turned two or three times to look around him,
and then fell into profound abstraction —as
if gazing in imagination across the distant veldt.
Suddenly, then, he would wake up, and settle him-
self down, satisfied that he was the object of the
“house’s” admiration. But now!—if a swaddy
shows prominently in a theatre he is guved by
the gods and ignored by the rest of the audience.
Recognising this lamentable state of things, the
returned farm-burners of Ballarat intend shortly
holding a banquet, ostensibly to commemorate
some event in S’Africa, but really in order to get
the limelight upon themselves once again. And
if, at the shivoo, any of the heroes gets overcome
by his “ feelings,” he can retire to the square in
Sturt-st. and there, gazing on the foundation-
stone of the proposed soldiers’ memorial, can
meditate upon the grievous slump in militarism,
evidenced by the fact that, though said stone was
laid by little York tw,o years ago, the statue
is still unerected and likely to stay so.
• • • •
Present high price of meat in Australia
is largely a “ fake” ; a ring of butchers keeps up
the prices locally, whilst, in some cases, carcases
are being exported for sale abroad at much lower
rates. In Sydney, the faker is helped by Free-
trade papers for party political purposes, so that
dear meat can be identified with Federation.

Reported from Mackay that a Chinese
leper —Mackay always has on hand a tame
Chinese or Kanaka leper —was mysteriously
smuggled on board a coasting steamer on King’s
Birthday and sent to Townsville for transhipment
to the leper settlement at Friday Island. The
Queensland sup-press declares said leper to be a
myth, but carefully smothers the fact that in the
middle of November a vessel left Townsville for
Friday Island with five Kanaka lepers and one
Chinese. During three years over 100 Kanakas
as “ suspected of having leprosy ” have been de-
ported to the islands, and Townsville now boasts
a leper village at the mouth of Ross River, within
two miles of Flinders-street. Yet the Boodle-
wraith still barracks for its Polynesian pets.
* • * •
The results of female contempt for the
majesty of the Law are sometimes pathetic as
well as humorous. At Bairnsdale (Yic.), t’other
day, the 70-year-old widow of a grazier was taken
to the local lock-up on the sheriff’s warrant for
having disobeyed a Supreme Court order re
the production of certain documents. The lock-
up having no fitting accommodation for a decent
old woman, an attempt was made to shift the
prisoner to the gaol at Sale, but the gaol authori-
ties wouldn’t accept her So the weary ancient,
when last heard of, was shaking her poor old head
at the lock-up keeper, and protesting that she
didn’t know what the Law meant by it. No
power on earth could persuade any grazier’s
widow that she deserves imprisonment for doing
as she chooses with the papers of her deceased old
man. Woman is her own Supreme Court of
Appeal every time.
IRREVERENCE.
THE COUBTt (in a deep voice): “ Prisoner at the
bar, after long and patient investigation you
have been found guilty, by a jury of your
countrymen, of the heinous crime of murder.
Have you anything to say ? ”
Convicted Homicide: “ Yes, yer Honor; I just
want to express the ’ope that yer Honor won't
attend the hexecution in that wig.”
(His Honor, who is usually good for 75 minutes
of eloquent admonition before passing sentence,
is quite broken up, and merely orders prisoner to
be taken away and hanged as early as possible).
London Daily Chronicle, commenting
on the number of gilded shepherds who had the
refusal of the bishopric of Melbourne ere Canon
Lowther Clarke accepted the billet, suggests in
a satirical tone —“It can hardly be possible that
churchmen have turned their backs on Melbourne
because the stipend is small in comparison with
the chances of useful work.” The fact is, of course,
that poor old Field Flowers Goe was a notorious
failure as Bishop of Melbourne, and the prospect
of having to shake things up, commercially
speaking, for a wage which is not very fat
(considering the “calls” that will be made upon it)
frightened the man who said “ No” to the offer,
A good many dignified clergymen are capable of
working hard for a big salary, or working a little
for a small family, but the number of high-toned
shepherds who will toil without hope of increasing
their bank account, is nowadays a limited and
diminishing quantity. Rev. Lowther Clarke is
probably “ blessed with this world’s goods ” out-
side of his professional practice—like ex-Bishop
Goe. Perhaps he is a brainy man of action,
which Dr. Goe wasn’t. In that case, he has his
work cut out to raise capital for the Bishop of
Melbourne’s’fund, and relieve the pitiable necessi-
ties of obscure country parsons, who are expected
to save the souls of four scattered parishes and
support a wife, family, and horse on about 455. a
week.

London Chronicle :
The talk about a batch of Australian peerages seems to
have died out now that the Coronation has passed with-
out any creations. It is a grievance with a certain
section of Australians that this mark of distinction has
not been made in their direction, seeing that Canada has
been profusely honored in this way.
Who are the Australians who nurse a grievance
that they are not peers—beerman Tooth, ship-
master M’Eacharn, and drainsman Fitzgutter ?
» * * *
A woman correspondent to Sydney D.T.,
bewailing the filthy long skirt of her sex, suggests
a remedy:—
Could we not demand of the Health authorities that
they move to make it a punishable offence for anyone to
wear any garments that come into contact with the
ground ? We fine man for expectorating on the pave-
ment ; why should we not fine a woman for wiping up
the dirt with her dress and spreading it over our carpets
and hanging it up in the wardrobes when she returns
home? The more one considers the question, the more
is one amazed that any clean-minded, intelligent woman
can adopt a custom the consequences of which must fre-
quently be, not only unspeakable, but unthinkable.
In short, if woman is to be reformed, it will only
be by dragging her from her dirty habits with the
strong arm of the law. Her slavery to Fashion
will keep her insanely insanitary otherwise. It’s
an awkward confession to make whilst the first
rolls of female voters is being prepared.
• * * •
Illavvarra (N.S.W.) miners appear to be
tired of some recent legal enquiries into colliery
management and clamor for an investigation by
“ competent men ” — i.e., by men acquainted with
colliery work. Govt. Geologist Pittman, after
consultation with the Under-Sec. for Mines, de-
scribed as “ harassing the Dept.” the action taken
by Jonathan May, a skilled expert, for better
methods. “If,” retorted Mr. May,
the efforts which, at the Glebe inquiry, proved : (1)
The existence of the brattice trick ; (2), the unreported
burning of Mr. Weir ; (3), the partial suffocation of Mr.
Taft; (4), the dismissal of a deputy for reporting gas—be
harassing the Mines Department, there must be some-
thing seriously wrong either with that department’s ideas
of duty or my own. I should certainly, as a matter of
duty and honor, be compelled to repeat my every action
in the Glebe matter under similar conditions.
• • * •
Writes an evil-minded correspondent:
Removing “dead horses,” etc., is not a bad game at
present if one has the connection. The “ highest price
given” is usually only mentioned in the advt., the
removal being considered adequate payment. One
Sydney dead-horse yard took £7 10s. in a recent week
retailing horseflesh for dogs’-meat, customers all
calling at the yard. Another long drought might estab-
lish the “cats’-meat” man in Australian capitals.
Re the alleged use of horseflesh in cheap restaurants.
“ Dead horse removers ” also take away condemned cows,
bullocks, &c., and, although I have asked repeatedly for
beef instead of horseflesh in my daily dog’s supply, I am
always told by the dead-horse man that “ he can do
better than that with beef.’’ Horseflesh costs Id. a lb. ;
what better can he do with dead-cow meat ? Sausages, I
see, keep at about the same old price level.
That was a well-deserved three months’
hard that an Albury Bench recently handed out
to a resident for cruelty to a horse. The kindly
soul had bound the nag, with fencing-wire round
the neck and loins, side-on to a fence, and was
belting it savagely with a trace. Through the
wretched horse’s efforts to free itself its neck and
body were deeply cut with wire.
• • * *
Complained in N.S.W. Assembly the
other night that “the Attorney-General had
written to certain stipendiary magistrates, for-
bidding them to make any general observations
from the Bench.” But should not the action be
applauded, even if it were extended to Supreme
and District Court Judges? The law is fre-
quently brought to contempt by the “extra-
judicial” remarks from the Bench, the little
discursive essays on things which have
nothing to do with the case, that a Jus-
tice Madden or a Justice G. B. Simpson
indulge in. Usually a Judge knows a good deal
about law; he is chosen for that; very often he is
deplorably ignorant in some other respects,_ and
when he airs his views on his weak points,
the ears of Midas peep from under the judicial
wig and take from it all its reverence. Every
judicial person, from the J.P. up to the Chief
Justice, should, when on the bench, abstain from
“general observations.”

Ifc is part of the dignity of being a J.P.
that a constable must touch his hat to you. A
N.S.W. man of pronounced “ Green ” views lately
had himself J.P.-ed, avowedly so that the local
constable, who was “Orange,” and hated him,
should have to salute him. He spends most of his
spare time now in promenading before the miser-
able bobby and seeing that his hat is duly hoisted.
« * • •
Even the “honorarium” of Victoria has
his “good days.” A couple of them at Melb.
City Court lately awarded “12 hours” to an un-
unfortunate German caught stealing fowls, and
then handed over the dead poultry to his sick
wife and children, for whom he had stolen them.
It wasn’t law —it was the sort of good-natured
justice that so extensively prevailed in New York
in the times of the Knickerbockers.
• * •
There was in a recently-tabled N.S.W.
Parliamentary report a striking illustration of the
relative strength of the public demand for the
unskilled male laborer and the domestic Susan.
Of 5613 males registered in 12 months the Govt.
Labor Bureau found work for only 195 with pri-
vate employers; while in its first four months
666 employers went to the Govt, female registry
seeking “ generals,” and found only 354, of various
sorts, to choose from.
* * * *
A petition praying that the death sentence
passed on the Kenniffs be commuted to imprison-
ment is being largely signed in Brisbane and
elsewhere. Comparison with the case of Selina
Sangal—about whose guilt there was no doubt,
while the K.’s were condemned on circumstantial
evidence only—has caused a mild revulsion of
feeling up north.
THE GARDEN SCENE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Marguerite ! Just for a little while
I hold you pillowed in my arms, content
In a sweet semblance of abandonment;
Your lips half-parted in a loving smile,
And your dear eyes drawn-lidded, to beguile
My soul, wherein a life’s great longing’s pent
Is it not false—nay, more—irreverent,
To feign that all is but a singer’s wile ?
Swathed in the softness of your unbound hair,
I listen to your heart’s loud voluntary,
And fondly would imagine Love is there.
If I should dare so much as question thee
With tremulous kiss, let answering kisses rare
Thrill me with passion’s sweet sincerity.
MELB.
