A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 23, 1902.
Mr Dicar Arini—
Please take the old, old wish about Xmas and
New Year as wished. I have just got a post-card
with a spray of flowering Xmas-bush gummed to
the back. The P.O. officials must have fairly
nursed it, as the spray is undamaged. Christmas
night and the nights following will see the usual
wrestling with the heavenly maid at Sydney
Town Hall and various churches. It will be
mostly of a sacred character.
At Sydney Grammar School, after Gov. Rawson
had distributed the prizes, he said some words
that went straight to the boys’ hearts. When he
was a lad, at Marlborough College, he had a great
ambition —not to do any work—(cheers from
ambitious boys). Whatever success he had
was due to his being on the right spot
at the right time; “he happened to be there,”
as one at the Tivoli used to sing. But he, in
effect, urged the boys not to do as he had done,
but to do as he told them—to work. It’s all very
fine and modest of the old man-of-war to ascribe
to luck such success, for instance, as being
appointed Gov. of this rather dry State, with its
finely-situated and . hideously laid-out capital.
There were scores of others on the spot when the
hour struck, but S’Harry was picked. And not
for his bushy eyebrows, certainly.
If, as one of my Melbourne correspondents
wrote last week, “ George Egerton,” the writer of
“Keynotes,” “Discords,” and other undraped-
soul-and-naked-truth books, which even yet have
a huge audience, be really in the Victorian capi-
tal, it may interest her to know that some rela-
tives of one of her husbands —the Clairmonte one—
are ih Sydney and eager to see her. “George
Egerton’s ” maiden name, according to the
Literary Year Book, was Dunn (her father was
Capt. Dunn) and she was born in Melbourne at the
close of the Maori war. She divorced one hus-
band, another died, and the third, whom she
married about a year ago, is a Dr. Bright.
Talking of the inverted-eye type of fiction. It
was bad enough whilst its exponents were women,
but now men are writing it under feminine pen-
names. The bold things! If men don’t mind,
they’ll lose the reverential respect and esteem
with which women have pretended to regard them.
A few years ago, one of the most gifted men in
this State said to me, when under financial
stress, “I’ve a good mind to write a some-
what improper book, sign it with a woman’s
name, and so retrieve my fortunes. ” He joked
better than he knew, for, since then have
come “An Englishwoman’s Love Letters”
(written by a man) and “The Confessions of a
Wife” (the writer of this last has not yet proclaimed
himself— he signs “Mary Adams.”) It is not
written with a woman’s hand, but with a man’s
foot—bunions on it at that. Why didn’t he take
the pyjamas off his own soul and write about
that ? But, then, a man’s soul doesn’t seem to be
of any value on literary ’Change. The ' ‘ wife, ”in
the “Confessions,” has all the furniture for
misery (and a story)—a soul, a husband, a horrid
baby, and a nasty dog. She puts on all the airs
of having invented matrimony. She is always
begging her husband “ to try to understand her,”
or informing him that Marion (the baby) “ has one
of her bad throats.” She cries whole forenoons
locked in her bedroom, then writes her hus-
band long, soul-grilling letters, and sends
them to his office. He comes home at six
as usual, and kisses her as usual. She recollects
that his kiss is “not rapturous” (but merely
“as usual ”), and off she goes again analysing,
torturing, questioning her soul ana his. And all
the time the man is a nice, natural, well-
bred, manly man who loves her. In the
most agonising crises of her life she records
minutely -bow “Job,” her nasty little dog,
behaves. „ And so she does away with any air
of reality her “ martyrdom ” might have. In this
the man’s writing-foot is apparent. So also is it
in the way he mishandles the child. He makes
it quite unconsciously a most unlovable young-
ster. Also in the way he dresses the woman.
“ The ruby gown with velvet on it,” in which she
sits before the fire “to write things.” The hus-
band, by the way, takes to morphia as an antidote
to his wife’s too much and too sensitive soul.
But there’s another recently-published feed-on-
your-soul-and-be-poisoned book which its Ameri-
can publishers assure us is written by a 19-year
old Montana girl. “ The Story of Mary Maclane,”
is a diary of a lonely bush girl who tramps
over sand-wastes and scrubs floors. She is
soaked with Marie Bashkirtseff and Walt
Whitman, especially the latter, and in this
book she has “oozed” to the value of £4OOO
in royalties alone. She mentions some of her in-
most organs vaingloriously, as, for instance, “ My
sound, sensitive liver rests content in its thin
yellow bile; ” “my calm, beautiful stomach sings
a song,” etc.; “my red blood,” etc., all Whitman-
ish. She also remarks that her peer does not
exist, and that her egoism and vanity are beyond
parallel, and that she has nine cambric handker-
chiefs padding her blouse. The “ Kind Devil,”
a dream-creation of hers, who is always with
her, and who helps her to unveil her soul, and
has “steel-grey eyes,” which peer at her lovingly,
is only her ideal of the man who will by-and-bye
marry her and take her for frisks to Coney
Island. This “stQry”is very dull, very trashy,
and not new, but it was accepted the day after it
was received by the publishers, and—l cannot get
over that <£4ooo in royalties. It’s enough to set
us all a-diarying about our blouses, our digestions,
our little dogs, and our lacerated souls.
Hon. Dorothy Brand, youngest daughter of our
late Governor, Viscount Hampden, was married
last month to Percy Feilden, youngest son of a
lieut.-gen. of that name. The two Ferguson
boys, her nephews (eldest Miss Brand married
Capt. Ferguson in Sydney) were the bride’s
pages. Algiers was the selected honeymoon
ground.
Miss Mabel Carey, second daughter of Mr. J.
Randal Carey, president of Sydney D.T., and
Major of the Citizen’s Bushmen, was married last
Saturday to Mr. Claude Hill Reading, of
Woollahra, with all the pomp and circumstance
of what her pa’s paper, in an extra-special article,
carefully styled a “ fashionable wedding.” The
bride’s mother, a daughter of General Taylor, one
of the early M.L. settlers, is one of the hand-
somest of Sydney matrons.
When Melba was at “ Cranbrook,” they gave
her, on one occasion, astronomer Russell as dinner-
mate “ because she was a star.”
Inez Bensusan, who went hence to try her luck
on the Yankee stage, is writing some bright travel
notes in S.A. Critic. Negro porters and conduc-
tors make good copy; so does a lady in Paris
frocks who runs a wild-beast show. Inez laments
her own inability to remember faots and figures,
which the practical Yanks demand of her when
she discusses her own country. “ I cannot in-
crease my luggage with figures,” she says, “it
takes me all my time to look after my own figure.”
Recent fite in aid of N.S.W. Free Kindergarten
Unions realised £250, expenses totalling only £l6.
Mrs. Frank Davenport, the organising hon. sec.,
and her committee have reason “to step high”
over this achievement.
Have you seen “the fruit delicious” which is
now ripening in the Botanic Gardens ? It is of
West Indian habitat. There are a few plants also
in private gardens in Sydney. Mr. Julius Caro
has one. The “ fruit ” has a far-off resemblance
to the over-grown pistil of an arum-lily. It takes
a year to ripen, the final stage being accomplished
when it is out off and put away in a cupboard.
Flavor must be like what the magnolia flower would
be if it were a fruit. The after taste is very acid.
Mr. George Flannery, who went to London and
Coronation with Premier Barton, as private seo.,
and was sick and in hospital long after he returned
to Australia, is now quite well again.
A London correspondent tells me that Yert and
two other managers heard Mary Conley (of Mel-
bourne) sing just before the mail left, and had
pronounced her voice “magnificent.” “All the
invading Australian phalanx assure me,” says
this correspondent, “that they are deluged with
singing engagements, which is a pathetio, if big,
untruth.”
A correspondent tells me that this is how
Dolores gets her audience to join in her “ Laugh-
ing Song.” When she reaches a passage where
she has to accompany the music witli laughter she
keeps her eyes on some particular person in the
audience, smiles at him, laughs, and does eye-
play. If, after a couple of seconds, he doesn’t
break up she selects someone else, and at the next
laughing passage goes through the same perform-
ance. Once a leather-lunged man is convulsed
with laughter the chances are that the whole
audience will laugh in chorus.
A local doctor’s wife has a much-loved dog,
which has lately gone quite blind. It insists, all
the same, upon accompanying the lady on her
walks, and when it bumps its head against a lamp-
post or other obstruction, it sits down and cries.
What a horror is the average furnished cottage
in our Mountains ! Bedding such as you would
scarce give your collie without first disinfecting
it, mosquito nets with holes big enough to admit
native bears; insanitary-looking rags on the
windows, rags more holes than material covering
the toilet-tableß and chests-of-drawers; grimy
walls, and carpets that have not been lifted since
they were laid. Whilst you are trying to make
an estimate of the different kinds of microbes
with which you might share this cottage, the
landlady, who usually lives next door, says
acidly: “Mrs. lived here for a year at
two guineas a week.” She mentions the name
of a lady of undeniable status. “But,” you
murmur, “did she not bring her own bedding,
mosquito-fences and window-blinds ? ” “ She did
not; this is a. fully furnished cottage.” And then
if the lady doesn’t set her dog on to you it’s be-
cause you’ve already made friends with it.
The choice in toys of most of the boy children
this year has run to motor-cars, a toyseller tells
me—motor-cars, with a red chauffeur, his eyes
guarded with green goggles. One genius from a
kindergarten created a demand for “ fighting kan-
garoos, ” but there was no supply. Another, who
had heard Wells’ “First Men in the Moon” read
aloud, wanted a “Grand Lunar,” the awful being
with the gigantic brain and insect limbs.
The withholding of the names of some of the
parties with whom the respondent and petitioner in
the Varney Parkes divorce-suit charged each other
with misconduct, saved the case, so goes the story,
from becoming a real cause ciUbre. Had the
allegations been published in full, the case would
have been the talk of Sydney. As it was, peti-
tioner has dropped out of public life so com-
pletely that the affair excited comparatively little
interest.
Late Henry Parkes’ grave, in Faulconbridge
cemetery, has four of the sturdiest of hawthorn
bushes, one at each corner, almost shutting out
the monument in the centre. One side of the
obelisk sets forth that it was erected to the
memory of the statesman, another side says—“ In
memory of Thomas Parkes, aged 73, and his wife,
Martha Faulconbridge.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Miss Elmes, of the Argus, is the latest of the
craft to set her face northwards —she goes to the
Big Smoke for a six months’ holiday. Personally
she is a very charming little woman, with un-
limited perseverance—and, as for her literary
qualifications, let that fascinating series of ‘Love
Letters ’ which sjie wrote for The Bulletin a
year or two ago answer.
“At the Town Hall concert in aid of the
Mallee Drought Fund, on Wednesday night,
thunder provided organ-notes of portentous
volume. The elements waged war—real war—
with the singers, and one time, when a sweet
male serenader was running up the scale, a great
growl of thunder rolled over his head, and a
lightning flash glared for a lurid instant through
the fanlight. But he didn’t take the hint. Three
smartly-frocked debutantes tried their vocal
strength on the public. Miss Mabelle Morgan
was best, but her struggle to cope with the diffi-
culties of the ‘Jewel Song’ was too palpable.
Miss Pearl Green has some good deep notes.
Miss Agnes Arnold showed a dra wing-room range
of warblets.
“The display of the Ramsay paintings at
‘Myoora,’ Toorak, was mainly regarded by the
Philistines as a glorious opportunity to tread the
crimson carpet and to breathe the perfumed air
of Melba’s abode. The songstress not only lent
her temporary mansion as a gallery to the home-
coming Melbourne artist, but she also had invita-
tions circulated for a private view of the collection
on Tuesday. The next two days saw a muster of
the rank-and-file suburbanites, who passed meekly
under the tilted nose of the hall Jeames, and slid
into the seats of the impromptu gallery, or
knotted themselves into groups about the place.
The pictures were the least part of the show —the
consciousness of this being Melba’s house was the
main attraction. Genuine art lovers were lost to
sight among the general gapers.
“Artist Hugh Ramsay has come out for the
benefit of his lungs, not his pocket. Bad drainage
in the vicinity of his Paris studio was the cause of
a lung affection. Six months of our hot clime
was the medical prescription. The artist is mid-
way towards the completion of a life-size (still
another) Melba portrait. In the Big Smoke
Hugh Ramsay fell in with her tastes, and he did
some panel work at the Great One’s order.
“Of 38 of the Ramsay pictures shown, quite a
dozen are portraits or studies of the artist himself,
his Melbourne fiancee, and his sisters. The best
of this lot shows him at three-quarter length in a
white working coat; his lean strong hands on his
hips; his face set as if for the camera.
“The Governor Clarkes and their retinue, the
Waxcellency Maddens and more of the exalted,
filed into the seats of the mighty at the National
Gallery on Thursday night, at the presentation of
the travelling-scholarship trophy to Meyer Altson,
and the general review of the students’ handicraft.
These things were prefaced by much show and
laborious talk on the part of banker Gyles Turner,
and a more pointed speech from Gov. Clarke. The
little Ex., giving his collar a nervous hitch, admitted
that he himself daubed in an intermittent and casual
but not clever fashion. How many more weak-
nesses has he ? The ’cello is his dearest diversion,
the piano has its charms, philosophy its wiles, and
painting its attractions for the little gentleman.
Surely a many-sided man !
“ Meyer Altson was a bashful figure. He is a
narrow little Oriental chap—melancholy, slow.
When his brother daubers, in an effort to be Latin
Quartiery, hoisted him, enthroned on a chair
on their shoulders, his dark head hung dejectedly,
his arms ditto. But later he made a bold jump
from his peroh, and dashed through a near door
to temporary oblivion and peace. His picture,
‘Leisure Hours,’ shows a group of indolent
figures (all art fellows) fallen to talk or books,
while the dashing female model, nude to the
waist, gives herself up to repose. A good subject
and some good work—unevenly done.
“ Mrs. Norton Grimwade was the most swagger
frocker at that Art Conversazione. She wore a
stately black with top tiers of Japanese cherry-
blossom pink and black embroidered chiffon.
Lady Silverlocks was tall and rigidly straight in
her lilac turn-out, and diamond flickers. The
Waxcellency’B dame took the shine out of most
women of importance by her blue garb and her
wonderful creaseless skin and delicate color. The
lady’s complexion is a marvel.
“ We are accustomed to meet with Mrs. (Doctor)
Blank and Mrs. (Majah) Dash in the tea-and-
muffin periodicals, but it came as a bit of a
startler last week to read of Mrs. (Detective)
Stroke. What next ? It will probably lead on to
Mrs. (Butcher) Blue and Mrs. (Undertaker)
Black. Why not ?
“Not nice for Marshall Hall to recognise in
star performers at Melb. Conservatorium concert
(conducted by Prof. Peterson) former promising
pupils. At the time of the split these young
things, in most cases, were willing to follow
Marshall Hall for better or worse, but their
parents decreed that they must stand by the
’Varsity Conservatorium, owing to its more
valuable (because ‘ official ’) diplomas.
“The public begins to think of turning against
the Conservatorium entertainments. The pro-
moters ‘invite ’ victims wholesale (more than the
hall can possibly hold). They charge for reserving
a seat (2s. is the usual figure), but if you don’t rush
to the box-office at the very start you’re left.
Mobs of gushers jump all the seats in the begin-
ning, and no blame to them, if they’re so set on
school displays as all that. But what about the
mass of ‘ invited, ’ decoyed to a place when there
isn’t the remotest chance of them getting in ?
“ Maggie Stirling (Mrs. Riddell) has now her
girl and boy with her at ’Ome. So long as their
invalid father was alive, they remained with him
in Melbourne, the idea being that he might some
day regain enough strength to travel with them
to their mother in England. Now that her Aus-
tralian home is broken up, our chances of hearing
the mellow-voiced Maggie again seem uncertain.
“Circus parties have been the vogue. Last
week a noted politician went to gaze upon
his prototype, and he watched the gambols
of ‘Lizzie,’ with a cheerful and good-tem-
Eered air. On Tuesday Mr. Toby Barton
eld the V.R. box with his family and one
or two small Eton friends. He looked a very
simple-minded Toby, indeed ; no affairs of State
prevented him from giving absorbed attention in
the show. It was even with reluctance chat Toby,
when respectfully invited to a tete-a-tete with the
circus proprietor at an interval, left to see the dog.
. “ The students’ concert epidemic broke out
virulently a week or two back, and it is still
raging. Some of the concerts have been harrow-
ing because of ‘operatic’ efforts. Every scaly
person these days gives her or his pupils the prima
donna’s goal to aim at; hence many tears. Why
try to grow grapes on a gooseberry bush ?
“ A curious incident has caused the postpone-
ment of a marriage this month. The ’groom to
be was visiting the Quarantine Station in the
cause of duty, when tie was detained. Up to
date he was still required at the station, and the
charming bride-elect and her friends anxiously
await word that he is free.
“ There are three well known Melbourne girls
in mourning for deceased fiances. At the funeral
of one a dog-cart that had often been used by the
sweethearts was driven immediately behind the
hearse.
There was a good gathering at the marriage of
Barrister Mackey, M.L.A., and Miss Bella
Watson Bates at Dr. Bevan’s Church, last Thurs-
day. An academic tone was given to the scene
by the presence of so many University identities,
bookworms and art students. The evergreen ana
most cordial Judge Casey (the blandest that ever
sat on a Victorian bench) was among the guests—
his recent globe-trot having added to his solid
proportions and rubicund countenance. The mar-
riage was followed by a reception tea at the resi-
dence of Mrs. Bates, in Hawthorn.
“Another marriage oi interest was that of the
popular Miss Nellie Billings, of the Conserva-
torium, with Mr. Charles S. Heath.
“ Mrs. Andrews, wife of the secretary of Mel-
bourne hospital, gave further evidence to an
Athenaeum audience, t'other night, of her busy
mind. The lady, who talks engagingly, descanted
on the happy home and the hand which rooks the
cradle and rules the spouse. The men of the
audience gazed with sorrowful envy at the vener-
able head of Secretary Andrews, whose wife would
have all women serving with gentleness, tact, and
healthful care, their lords and masters.
“ Maie Saqui has fallen on prosperous times in
the Great Smoke. She owns a pretty motor-car,
and dresses superbly. These days, Maie is large
and languishing.
“The girls who have gone in for the large
* Mother’s Darling ’ frilled hat of the hot season
are addressed playfully as ‘ Baby.’ It sounded
all right when a picture beauty like dainty Miss
Anderson, of South Yarra, was referred to in this
fashion, and Mrs. Oswald Watt could also carry
off the chaffing term, saucily ; but when elderly
dames with faces the hue of kharki come along in
these hats, the term appears a trifle silly. 4 Sweet
seventeen ’ looks all right under the childish rim,
but matured matronhood, and the large class of
fashionables in urgent need of consulting a skin
specialist, had better refrain. A good heavy
gossamer and substantial head-gear are the safest
for them.
“Feeding-bottles have hitherto been bartished
from fashionable circles, but with the craze for
adopted infants, ‘bottles are in.’ Conversation
in a Toorak drawing-room these days often con-
sists of attack and defence of baby ‘comforters.’
A well-known dame, who has always preferred
her expensive Pomeranian poodles to babies, con-
fessed in a contrite spirit, the other day, at a
luncheon party, not far from the Myoora mansion,
that ‘she had no idea infants were so interesting. ’
This looks most promising.
“The most flourishing calling in these drought-
ridden parts is dentistry. All the molar-tuggers
along Collins-st. are raking in the shekels at the
rate that the Commonwealth gathers in its Customs
fines. During the last month, the engagements of
five flourishing tusk-dealers, all resident in
Collins-st., were reported.
“A well-known N.S.W. squatter, who has
suffered severely from the recent drought, has
discovered a treasure in one of his daughters ; she
is said to possess the faculty of discovering water
by means of the divining rod.
“The Mutual Store, Melbourne, has hit on a
good way to help the drought-stricken mallee
settlers. It undertakes to deliver to any railway
siding a truck of mallee-roots at the rate of 17s.
per ton, four or five tons going to one truck. The
whole amount is to be given to the mallee farmers,
without any deduction by the Store for trouble.
No fuel is cleaner or more pleasant than mallee
roots, and the settlers have a great supply avail-
able.
“The Federal posts are gradually being filled
by the best-looking Johnnies in the State ser-
vices. Oom Bill Lyne’s fatherly affection for the
ladies is known. Is that why he has an eye for a
straight nose, a head of hair, and a lithe
limb in filling public-service positions? The
Melbourne girls, at any rate, are chuckling
the tide in the Federal affairs that brings such
pretty fish to their Social basket.
“ Melba has heard one sermon since descending
on this drought-stricken State. Prof. Peterson
delivered it at the reception at the Conservatorium
to the songstress. In his best gown, his loftiest
expression and his most parsonic manner, the
musical dominie held forth on the matter of voice-
production, while Melba dropped her chin on her
elevated bosom and did a think. The Prof, droned
on till several looked strong disapproval. It seemed
a foolish thing to plant the great singer down and
preach to her concerning the sol-fa system. Prof.
Peterson’s talk to Melba was a huge dish of cold
porridge, just as Marshall Hall’s inflamed oration
to the same lady was an orgie of wine.
“ The very latest of Allan’s Australian Music
Books (No. 61) contains thirteen songs, selected
with due regard to this season of praise and
pudding. Nobody can ask for a better shillings-
worth of song classics, Gounod’s ‘ Nazareth ’ and
‘ Ave Maria,’ Handel’s * Where E’er You Walk,’
Macfarren’s ‘Beating of My Own Heart,’ Mozart’s
‘Holy Portals,’ and other respected old friends,
such as ‘ Will o’ the Wisp ’ and ‘ The Englishman,’
which latter will appeal to all furious admirers of
the six disputed hatters. The Melb. Musical
Magazine, also from Allan’s, is another lot of 13
songs—ditties that reach the heart of the Rickards
public and awaken guileless memories of pictures
on the screen. These range from the frisky
‘ Bismarck Grenadiers/ sung by Henrietta Byron,
to the sorrowful tale of a fair, young spinster who
has ‘fallen by the wayside’ in a bejewelled con-
dition.

Said by some that Senator O’Connor gets
nothing at all from his colleagues for doing
as much of the work of Federal Government as
any of them ; but it is hard to believe this.
An ex-Minister of the Crown was fined
last week in a Sydney police-court £2 or 14 days’
gaol for selling liquor on Sunday. The court
allowed him 24 hours to pay or “take it out.”
He paid.
Bishop Moorhouse, of Manchester, still
concerns himself with Australasia. His nomina-
tion of Bishop Nelligan, to succeed Primate
Cowie at Auckland, is his latest move, but he
manages to have an effective say in the appoint-
ment of most of the C.E. Bishops imported by
Australia.
Recent letters raise a doubt as to the
return to N.S.W. of Chief-Justice Darley, who
has now the light to retire on a large pension.
The Boer commission, on which he has accepted
a seat, may be a step towards other Imperial work
which can be best discharged by residing within
cooee of London.
Arthur Robinson, who was member for
Dundas (Vic.) and was beaten at last election by
squatter Thompson, is a nephew of Prime Minister
Barton. Robinson is a fluent lawyer, and in
politics proved to be “ a young fellow in a hurry.”
In two years he changed parties twice.
A GREAT NEWSPAPER MAN: DAVID SYME,
PROPRIETOR OF THE “AGE."
Photo, by Johnstone, O’Shannessy & Co., Melb.
The recent pTioto. of David Syme flatters
the 75-year-old journalist-capitalist, insofar that it
gives him a stronger as well as a younger face
than he presents in profile. Confronting King
David and staring straight at him, one is dis-
inclined to believe that the student of spiritualism
and author of books on political economy can be
enslaved by a greed for power and siller. But to
catch him sideways is to somewhat change your
views of David Syme’s character. The name
otDavid is a cruel handicap through life, yet the
most thorough-paced David can begin to be
magnanimous to his enemies when he learns to
laugh at himself. Probably Syme of the Age,
brother of the late Ebenezer, has never even
smiled at himself.
The Gov.-General has sent £1 towards the
Xmas treat to the poor of Melb. Benevolent
Asylum. That won’t go far, but if everyone con-
tributed as much in proportion to his means the
inmates would have a good time.

while Acting-Governor, but tucks in afterwards.

Archdeacon Gunther, liberal churchman
and historiographer, is first in favor for the post
so long filled by the late Dean Cowper. Indeed,
in the history of Australia there has been but one
Dean of St. Andrew’s. The Dean has by office a
first claim on the apron when a vacancy occurs ;
but Dean Cowper never looked that way.
Senator Symon, K.C., has the unique
record of never having failed to secure the
acquittal of any prisoner for whom he has
appeared in Adelaide Criminal Court. Mr.
Symon got off Bertha Schippan, although the
newspaper evidence published prior to the tr;al
was (as in Martin’s ease) all the other way.

The’ dead Westralian Justice Moorhead
raced through his career faster than any other
judge figuring in Australia, In 11 years he was
admitted to W. A. Bar, entered Parliament, be-
came Minister of the Crown, ascended the
Supreme Court bench, and died.

There was a sort of minor skirmish in the
N.S.W. sectarian battle over the election of two
canons for St. Andrew’s Cathedral (Sydney) by
the Anglican clergy and laity. The extreme
Protestant party won, getting in their two
nominees, both strongly favored of the Orange
party, which has since publicly sent them its
congratulations.

G. H. Reid says that Kingston has been
put into the wrong box—be should have been
Czar of Russia. In a word, the Minister for
Customs, unlike Reid, is not a balancer, a fencer,
a smoolger, a Janus, a Yes-No, a Tory-Liberal a
Facing-both-Ways ;Jie is an honest, stalwart, in-
flexible, downright Radical, whose memory will
long live as that of one of the few great democrats
of Australasia.
T. R. Ashworth, a boresome
M.L.A., elected to Vic. Assembly
on the Reform wave, after years of
unsuccessful attempts as a Free-
trader, last summer encountered
George Reid at a seaside resort, and
at once pounced upon the leader of
the Federal Opposition to discuss a
recent speech of his in the Reps.
George, in his most innocent man-
ner, pretended not to know what
Ashworth was talking about, and,
after forcing him to make a literal
and categorical explanation, said,
“Oh!”—just as only George can
say it —“ I see what you are referring
to now; but, really, I am here on a
holiday.” Sticklers for truth always
spell politician T. R. Ashworth’s
name “ Trashworth.”
Someone has been speculating
in a Sydney paper on what the hang-
man says when he “takes charge”
of the condemned man. N.S.W.’s
Nosey Bob’s formula is (so the late
Chief Warder Lovett, of Darling-
hurst, told present writer): “My
poor man, I’m sorry for this, but ”
taking in two more holes of the arm-
straps with a vigorous jerk—“ it will
soon be over.” The late Mr. Gately,
when Victorian hangman, used to
say : “ Smith, your time’s come ! ”
Apropos, if you were going to be
hanged, which would horrify you the
more —a noseless hangman like him
of Botany Bay, or a masked brute,
like the Melbourne man-butcher ?

From Perth :
The Bulletin sets down Walter James, Premier of this
State, as a “ mediocrity.” Therein it is a bit astray. The
man is a K.C., and his reputation as a lawyer stands so
high here that public opinion indicates him as the next
Chief Justice ; personally, I think he is too high-minded
to appoint himself to the position. A Protectionist out-
and-out, in his ideas a Socialist—certainly restrained by
the House, but eager to go as far as practicable. He
introduced the Factories Bill here, and is starting a State
hotel at Gwalia, and has now a Bill before the House to
enable Kalgoorlie to erect a municipal theatre and hotel,
bonded warehouse, etc., etc. His Govt, is the first in
Australia to have the courage to say it will not borrow,
and, if compelled to do so, only locally, if it has to pay tf e
local capitalist a higher rate ; also, he openly avows h s
desire to see the abolition of the Upper House. An Aus-
tralian of the finest type ; high-minded, generous, and
just the nearest approach we have had to George Higin-
botham in State politics for years.

Apparently, Dr. Cockburn, that most
amiable theorist who set the S. Aus. fashion
in Liberalism before Kingston took up his later
work in earnest, has given up the idea of returning
from London and running for the House of Reps.
He has lately joined another bank board, and
seems to have settled down to a life of combined
guinea-pigging and philanthropy. It’s a curious
mixture ; but, then, so is the Doctor.
A lamentable defeat in the recent Maori-
land elections was that of Speaker Maurice
O’Rorke. He was probably the best Speaker in
Australia, and his decisions were hardly ever
questioned. IJo has sat in 43 sessions, and his
speakership (about 10 years) is a record for Aus-
tralasia, the nearest approach to it being George
Kingston's 18i years in South Australia. O’Rorke
was beaten by a Farmer’s Union candidate.
Died recently Colonel Angelo, ex-super,
at Rottnest (W.A.), a queer old gentleman who,
after closely studying the Australian aborigines,
concluded that our niggers were the “lineal
descendants of the ten lost tribes of Israel.” In a
word, the Colonel was soft enough to believe that
in 3000 years the lost tribes so degenerated that
they couldn’t at once perceive that a little golden
half-sovereign was more valuable than a big silver
half-crown.
Poor old Mulligan, the up-country S.A.
“cocky” farmer, who became famous by sitting
as coroner in the Towitta murder case, and more
famous still by suspending the proceedings in the
barn to oblige newspaper snap-shot fiends, died
a few days ago. In S.A., any J.P. is liable to be
called on to act as coroner ; and when Mulligan,
being handiest, had to take charge of the most
sensational case S. Australia has known for years,
it just broke him up.
“ H.H.” to The Bulletin on a Labor
matter :
What a land of mediocrities England must be, and
what a country of sycophants is Australia ! Our
Laborites, when one of their kidney happens to arrive
from the anointed Motherland, go wild with
enthusiasm. For instance, the long-haired poseur,
Tiilett, though he was obviously the inferior of several
of their own ranks in each State, and Tom Mann, now
being kowtowed to as though he were the veritable Pope
of Labor, while, on his merits, he merely stands in the
ruck of the Australian Labor members. All Labor Aus-
tralia shrieks over these two men simply because they
happen to come from London, yet what cool critic would
place either of them in the same street as Watson,
Hughes, Mahon, Griffith, Higgs, M‘Gregor, or Holman or
Dawson when he is in good form? The hard-up Melb.
Trades Hall Council is said to be trying to raise TGOO a
year to pay a salary to Mann as an organiser. The
money wouid be sufficient to secure the services of two
Australian organisers each the equal of Mann in every
sense.

The most exciting of the feats of Supfc.
Casey, now retiring from N.S-W. Police force,
i took place one night about 40 years ago when he
.was a constable at Bathurst. Prisoners broke gaol
and a melee ensued in which police and malefac-
tors fought at close quarters. Casey arrived on
the scene and blazed away with such effect that he
succeeded in turning the flight of the enemy,
driving thirteen of them back to their cells. Un-
fortunately one of his bullets found lodgment in a
policeman.
Although Premier Jenkins signed the
strenuous and sarcastic letters sent by S.A. Govt,
to Prime Minister Barton recently on the subject
of the transcontinental railway, the wheat duties,
and the Senate elections, they were all written
by Attorney-General Gordon, who is very handy
with the pen. Also, he is one of those genial,
happy-go-lucky persons who “don’t care nothing
for nobody,” and would sooner flay his best friend
—figuratively, of course—than miss the chance of
saying or writing something clever. ,
The impending return of ex-editor Ward
(now chief leader-writer of Melb. Akgus) to
Sydney, after many years’ absence, to his old
position as D.T. editor, is a piece of dramatic
justice not often seen in real life. Mr. Ward left
the D.T. as a matter of journalistic conscience
when he found the directors grabbing too big a
hold of the strings, and with him left his two
leader-writers, present editor Gullett and ex-
editor Brient. The latter returned immediately
to the control of the paper, and the result was a
feud of many years’ standing, which has not pre-
vented the final restoration of capable editor
Ward to his old chair.
One of the best-known members of Vie.
Leg. Council, who rose from very lowly beginnings,
had not the slightest aspirations towards politics
when a deputation waited upon him to ask his
consent to nomination. “ But,” he protested, “ I
dunno nothin’ about these here things.” >The
spokesman pointed out that such an objection
was no objection at all in the case of the Leg.
Council. “ But Pve took no interest in anythin’
of the sort,” said the capitalist, “ I wouldn’t know
what it was all about.” The spokesman was still
unsatisfied. “You can read,” he suggested.
“ Um—er—yes,” replied the prospective candi-
date, uncertainly. “You can write,” he con-
tinued. “Well ,” replied the other, with an
eloquent pause. “You can write cheques,” sug-
gested the spokesman. The face of the man of
money brightened that they should have discovered
bis metier. “Yes,” he firmly announced.
“Then,” said the deputation, “you’re the man
for us,” and it departed to arrange for the dud
expenditure on the campaign requirements.

BEWARE OF IMITATIONS.

Aboriglnalities.
To Contributors. —In the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
'many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
The so-called “ locusts ” being very plenti-
ful this year, the way of the schoolboy is hard.
He never tires of his captive insect till the legs
and wings are off, and the “ music-box” is broken.
Then the boy, with a feeling of great self-sacrifice,
puts the remnant into a tree. Experience never
teaches boys that it’s dangerous to take locusts
into school. They smuggle them in all right, but
the temptation to see if they’re quite well takes
the hand to the pocket. Then begins the skirling,
and the unfortunate naturalist has often to join-
in. Remarkable are the school names for locusts
—“bakers,” “floury-bakers,” “washerwomen,”
“yellow Mondays,” “drummers,” and “double-
drummers.”

Judge Noel recently decreed at Long-
reach (Q.), that “an owner must be able to
positively identify a beast, the brand and earmark
not being considered as prima facie evidence of
ownership.” Then what is the object of the
brand ? A grazier who has 10,000 head of cattle
scattered over a big run, and could “positively
identify” any one of them in a strange place,
minus brand or earmark, would be a phenomenon.
If such decision holds good, it removes practic-
ally the only safeguard the squatter has against
cattle-stealers.
A positively majestic sight was the dust-
bank driven ahead of the monsoon rains that
brought hope to the out-back in the first days of
December. In the distance it hung like a curtain
hundreds of feet high, apparently motionless;
then it moved on slowly from the west, rising
until it had blotted out the sun, turning and
twisting and rolling, in all shades of brown and
on to the deepest purple; then, as it overtook
one, for a few minutes —five, perhaps—dense
darkness, passing to the yellow of a flame ; then
the yellow died out and the gloom was wliitey-
brown ; and finally the rain drops, driven on a
raging wind, fought with the dust, and sometimes
won and some times lost.
As usual, the monsoon rains which fell out-back
at the beginning of Dec. spent themselves in
patches. One station was flooded ; its neighbor
had to hide itself from the fury of an appalling
duststorm. The coaches that crawled into Broken
Hill from the North were heavy with mud, and
floods had made them a day late ; the coaches that
crawled in from the south were heavy with dust,
and had travelled a track cursed with a 40-mile
dry stage. One station at least has some paddocks
wlii-ji were turned into bogs, and others which re-
mained dust-heaps.

Esley River cattle station, Northern
Territory, was sold last month (just before the
big rains) to a Westralian at the rate of 20s. per
head, or about £4OOO cash. The highest bid for
the property at auction in Melbourne was 16s.
per head, notwithstanding all the cattle were fat.
The drawback was evidently the vast stretch of
droughty country between that district and the
big markets.

“ Bilby ” : The most complete collection
of drunks I ever saw in one house was in a
pub, many
years ago, in
Condobo li n
district (N.S.
W.). I was
passing there
about midday,
and called in
with the idea
ofgettingsome
dinner. There
were six
drunks on the
verandah, all
incapable of
moving. The
proprietor
slept the sleep
of the drunk
on the sofa,
and, in re-
sponse to my
shakes, uttered
a guttural
note, which
sounded like
“Advanc h
Aushtralia,”
and went to
sleep again. The
cook was dead
drunk on the kitchen floor, with
both feet resting on the chair from
which he had fallen. When I tilted him
_ on to the ground with a flop he showed no
signs of life, A woman sitting in the bar was
far gone, and only smiled a vacant but
friendly smile when I spoke to her.
The dog wasn’t drunk, but someone
had left a long-sleever on the
verandah, and he was drinking.
The whole house was as silent as
a vault full of dead men. I have never
found out to this day what all these drunks
had been celebrating, but for some reason or
other there had been a heroic burst that day in the
Condobolin country. Possibly it was the dog’s
birthday. They get drunk on small provocation
in Condobolin.

“ Myhree ” : When going round the
rabbit-traps you can always tell whether a cat or
fox has been at bunny, by the different methods
pussy and Reynard adopt in satiating their appe-
tites. Reynard makes a neat puncture in bunny’?
heart and draws the blood, finishing up with the'
heart; while puss tears her victim till her appe-
tite is appeased.
“Boko”: “E.S.S.” (B. 20/9/’O2) gets
astray when he starts on his ‘ ‘ snake critique ”
business:
(1) All snakes do not take to the water on their own.
I’ve never seen any but blacks, greens, whips and carpets
in the water excepting in flood time.
(2) No snake was ever yet known to eat dead tack ; he
must catch it alive.
(3) Has he or any other contributor ever seen an eel
leave water and crawl around unless said water was a
vanishing quantity ?

“ C.G." : Saw, the other day, at Gympie
(Q.) a woman driving a team of four horses with a
load of timber for the mines. Her sisters cut and
load it, and she carts it into the town. She has
been doing this for a long time past. She always
says that she will never go to service “ while
there’s a stick of wood in the country.”

“L.F. ” : Underground here in
Blook 12 (Broken Hill) the 500 ft. level
swarms with mosquitoes, which breed
in the stables, and thence have spread
throughout the level. I have worked
mates with rats, mice, lizards, frogs,
ants, &c. Also had a cat and dog
underground for weeks. Puss always
got under a bucket at firing-time,
otherwise took to the life-well. But
the dog got fat, and one day failed to
clear a mullock pass he was jumping
over, and caused the trucker, who
was working at the shoot below, a
severe shock through 201 b. of dog-pulp
hitting him in the chest. And at a
Gippsland mine, when I was there,
another trucker ran a truck (empty)
off the rails, owing to a sft. goanna
which happened to be going in ahead
of him. The goanna was incensed,
and as Teddy hadn’t seen such beasts
before lie got a bad scaring, and would
never after travel out without a light.
But mosquitoes are the worst I’ve
struck yet—the fracteur-snmke makes
them more savage.
* * • •
“ H.J.M.” : All the small Ger-
man cane-growers around Marburg,
near Ipswich, Q., employ Kanakas,
at Bs. per week ; and sometimes they
are dear at that. Many of them
cleverly sham sickness, and grow daily
fatter and lazier. I knew one who
became “ insane ” regularly every
week-day immediately after breakfast,
got better during dinner-hour, and
went howling mad again until the
supper-bell, whereupon he instantly
recovered, ate well, and slept placidly
until next morning. His modus was
this : The moment cane-cutting began
he’d give a hideous yell and spring
into the atmosphere, flourishing his
cane - knife, and roaring “ Blood !
blood ! more blood ! ” Then he’d shy
his cane-knife at his German em-
ployer’s head—and sometimes he’d hit
it, too. Next, he’d roll over and
over on the earth, roaring like a bull
and squealing like a guinea-pig; then
he’d bound into the air again, and
hold himself up there with both
hands by the hair of his head. This was his
trump card, and always frightened Sheol out of
the boss, who’d yell “Mine Gott in Himmel!”
and make for the house, the other Kanakas
stampeding into the cane as if they were frightened
too —but only to have a quiet smoke.
AN OPTIMIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The chaps at Brolga oft gave vent
To gloomy fits of discontent —
Excepting William Henry Twist,
Who claimed to he an optimist.
He’d look upon the country round—
The seared, drought-stricken, parched-up ground—
And say, “ Well, boys, now don’t sling mud,
It might ha’ bin a blanky flood.”
A buzz-saw’s teeth, combined with booze,
Caused him one day his leg to lose.
And as they picked him up he said,
“ Thank Cord, it warn’t me bloomin’ head.”
At length his course of life was such,
Death laid on him his icy clutch.
He murmured as he lay expiring,
“A spell is just what I’m requirin’."
To nether regions sped his shade,
And calm good-day the keeper bade.
“ Just tell the bloke what runs the show
That Billy Twist’s arrived below.”
He sat upon a bank of flame
And cried as Satan to him came—
“ Say, boss, what luck 1 struck your camp 1
I never coaid stand cold or damp.”
JASON.

“ Bullfrog ” : I once heard an eloquent
nreacher hold forth on the dull misery of the
married women in India in a manner that drew
tears and shekels from the congregation, mid the
Zenana Mission grew richer On a Western
station an ignorant, brutish loutsof <
rider got married, and was sent, with 1 ’
one of the loneliest huts on the run. His jealousy
was something awful, and he tortured that woman
for vears with his narrow-minded idea that a
married woman who spoke to any man, even
most occasional swagman,
must be unchaste It gripped what little intellect
she had, and she thought constantly what a grand
thing it would be for all women to be dumb. 1
passed there last winter, and they _ were sending
the boundary-rider’s three daiighters to one of the
city asylums. They are dumb from birth, there
are four boys, but they are noisy enough.

who ran might read.

cotted Mark and his
pub., and when they
failed to starve him
out offered him a
good round sum to
pull down his boards.

rat papers refused to

T C Watson, leader of the Labor party in the
Federal Parliament, is an Australian by senti-
ment, a Maorilander by training, an Anglo-Scot
bv descent and a native of Valparaiso (Chili) by
birth. AH those facts point with much probability
to strength of character. English-Scotch is a
good mixture ; parents who stray from Britain to
South America in search of a career arell,^ Iyt^) 1 y t^)

land is a fine place for a youth to grow strong in ,

Australian by adoption, at any rate. Mr.
Watson doesn’t disappoint expectations. Luring
two years he has led what is practically the
dominant party in the Australian Legislature
wTth skill and discretion. He has also kept
his head level and unswollen under a stream
of mischievous praise from the Freetradepressof
Australia. It has seemed good to the Argus
particularly to represent Watson as tne hmperoi
o t Australia, to whom Barton is but as an oifice-
boy and Turner a clerk. This was partly an effort
to be nastv to the Federal Govt., but it was To a
greater degree an effort to be hostile to Watson;
Conservative knows what a keen weapon
flattery has proved against many l
But J C Watson, so far, seems to lack the spirit
of snobbery to which so many have fallen victims

COMPLIMENTS OF THE (DRY) SEASON; OR CHRISTMAS IN HARD TIMES.
1. A drought-stricken Christmas-tree. 2. Tommy Tucker: “Are yer goin’ to hang up yer stockin’ Christmas Eve?” Billy Buckles: “No, ’cause I hainfc
gob no stockings to hang up.” 3. qFather of large `family, who has just been “touched” for the usual Christmas-Box appropriation: “My dear, don’t you
thank the time has come to disillusionise our children’s minds regarding the Santa Claus myth ? It’s puerile, and it’s—it’s deuced expensive ! ” 4. A few home-
made toys, to suit the times. Every man his own Santa Claus. 5. A hint to absentee uncles. No good sending toy yachts out here just now. 6. This is the
vain-glorious citizen who, just before Christmas, always personally conducts a goose home in a bag, with its head ostentatiously sticking out of a hole in that
bag. This year, it will only run to a “Wile rabbit.” 7. There was silence in our boarding-house for half-an-hour when the landlady announced that, owing to
the long dry spell, she could only give us dry-hash on that festive occasion. 8. “What strange bird is that, grandpa?” “That, my child, was a kind of domes-
ticated water-fowl, once sacred to the Christmas feast. It is extinct now. Andrew Carnegie and Pierpont Morgan ate my last obtainable specimen.” 9. Box. :
“Lucky dog! where did you get it?’ “Oh, this is a last year’s one. I buried it last Christmas behind the stable, and, as I like to keep up these good old
customs, I dug it up to-day, and am gnawing it as a matter of form ! ”

“ Scribo ” : Witnessed in t>4 at Mold.
Gaol, the ’hanging of Mrs. Elizabeth Scott, the
first woman executed in Victoria ; also her two
paramours. The woman was a pleasant-lookmg,
trim body of about 30. She stepped out of the
condemned cell as unmoved as if receiving a call
from the milkman or baker. The nigger, a boy of
18 or 19, was sobbing bitterly. On the drop,
Mrs. Scott said to her white paramour: “Won t
you clear me now ? ” and received no answer.
She probably had hopes of a reprieve at the last
moment. There was no occasion to shoot the
husband, because he was already dying. Another
proof that the criminal is usually insane.
A good reform idea is that which Mr.
M‘Gregor Dey is agitating in Adelaide-for the
transplanting to Australia of the Scandinav
system of civil Courts of Conciliation. In case
of dispute citizens instead of going to law can go
to a plain home-spun Court of Concihation
which no lawyers are allowed) and haie tnm
differences adjusted at a cost of a few
If the Court of Conciliation doesn t makethe
neace the parties can still go to law. A 1 ■
meats made before the Court of C. are without
prejudice,” and can’t be used in evidence in a law
action.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm tas
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, 4 which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited.
Query. Why are Australian lawyers so
generally against free education ?

,ihich lays bricks, doing the work of seven nien,

having counted the cash carefully, lie pushed it
back to the official: “Allri’. Put ’im all back.
Pm all ri’.” Our brother Singh had evidently
been studying the financial policy of N.S.W.
Government, and wanted to know if it nad
“ blued his cash.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Be sure, now, never was it planned
That White Australia be Japanned.
A co. has begun making salt at Port
Douglas (N.Q.). The Commonwealth duty of
12s. 6d. per ton is the cause of this cruel outrage
on the feelings of importers.
In Maoriland, lawyers are debarred from
appearing in the Arbitration Court. A recent
oase, involving 700 men, was settled at a cost of
£l4 7s. These costs included railway-fares and
payment to witnesses of 10s. each.
M A law must be observed till it is re-
pealed,” cries the capitalistic Press whenever the
working classes think that a particular enactment
operates unfairly against them. That cry wasn’t
raised in the Orontes hatters’ case.
The very names of those behind the move-
ment to repeal the “contract labor” clause of the
Immigration Restriction Act were enough to
damn the thing in Victoria—Fallshaw, D. White,
Edgerton and the resurrected £ £ Smith (young-
man-don’t-you-feel-well) all opponents of the
minimum-wage laws.
Some humors from the first book of
“Rulings” in the Federal Parliament: —“It is
very improper for a Senator to sing while another
is speaking.” Unparliamentary expressions ate :
“Willing to be the catspaw of despotism,”
“ political criminal,” “robbers,” “vampires,”
“ghouls,” '“political sausage-skin.”
N.S. W. Parliament generally ends a session
with a champagne supper. Parkes and Dibbs
established the custom; Gr. H. Reid interrupted
it (his was “ a dry push,” as bluff Labor member
Edden lamented); but John See revived it. This
year, for the first time on record, the Opposition
somewhat churlishly refused to be present, and
held a little celebration “on its own.”
“ Vul. V.” : To those in favor of Jenkins’
continental railway I would recom-
mend the careful perusal of E rank Morris’
‘‘Octopus,” for an insight to the extor-
tion and iron-hearted villainy of land-
grab syndicates when once they gain
a monopoly of the railway traffic of
vast areas which have been handed
over to them as an inducement to
build. The novel, which is
based on actual fact, is a ter-
rible indictment of, and warning
against, the syndicate railway
and the horrors arising from that
dividend-hunting vampire.

the bogus democrat. The “ Reform ”-

Later on, however, lie “didn’t think it was

Tasmania is financially in a tight place,
but the holy Governor’s screw is still held sacred'.
He now receives £3500 —nearly twice as much as
the Governor of Ontario (Canada) which has
eleven times Tasmania’s population. A proposal
to cut down any future Gov. to £l5OO was
cast out a few days ago with so much shudder
that it might have been a decomposed reptile.
Victorian Treasurer Shiels puts out a pre-
liminary feeler re the abolition of old age pensions
and suggests that perhaps the State granted them
m a rash moment and “the money could have
been more wisely dispensed.” As the money is
evidently wanted to pay for the nine-year free
railway passes that Bent proposes granting to
suburban property-holders, anything may happen.

There was some doubt as to whether Bent’s
original idea of issuing free passes for as much as
nine years to builders of new residences in certain
specified suburbs would really “make the Vic-
f?"" 1 ™ llw ays P a y.” till the details came to
lignt, then the doubts vanished like a morning:
thirst before a long beer. Persons entitled to
second-class passes (builders of houses worth not
!? or j.l lan -£400) can travel first-class by paying
the difference, and people entitled to travel first-
class will be granted a rebate should they elect to
travel second-class. This will certainly make the
railways pay pay out hard cash to greedy well-
to-do builders of palatial piles. Patman lias
indeed reached his apotheosis in Victoria, where
it is seriously announced that the State will
actually pay him for the privilege of carrying him
free second-class!

.The Loan Bill for £3,070,472 was duly carried through
,® Assembly on Tuesday night, as, with the assistance
ot the Labor party, it was bound to be.
That’s the daily-press game—“ with the assistance
ot the L.P. If the L.P. doesn’t promptly break
from its support of the borrowing policy it
will be asked to carry the whole burden of the
blame when the crash comes.
After long and anxious search, in which
they were helped by the officers of the Lands
i>ept., the N.S.W. Labor Commissioners cannot
find, within 100 miles of Sydney, as much as 2000
acres of Crown land, in one block, of good enough
quality and well enough watered to suit the pur-
poses of the “industrial farm settlement,” for
winch, some years ago, Parliament voted £5OOO
Al> i j°’ , thou^h such an institution is badly
needed, the money remains unspent—or has been
spent on something else.
One of the most indignant accusations
levelled at the Australian Government by George
-Keid in his Maitland speech concerned its
attempt to pass important legislation at the fag-
end of the session. That fag-end argument always
comes in when there is nothing else available,
lhe fixed impression that all work should be
done at the beginning of the session, while the
end of it is devoted to hard drinking and running
after strange girls, seems to be an incurable
mental affliction with some people.
Canon Boyce, of Sydney, thinks that the
hatter ” episode may be made to reach clergy-
men imported from ’Ome, and wonders if the
law will interfere with the landing of Bishop
Clarke at Melbourne. And why not, anyhow ’
Can t Austraha produce “laborers in the vine-
yard without bringing them from abroad under
contract? lhe United States has before now
blocked the entry of a parson imported under
contract, and if the Land of Washington and
J! reedom does that, why shouldn’t we ?
While the Six Hatters were howling at the
Cornstalks’ gate, the N.S.W. Agent-General sent
along an anguished cablegram to the effect that
ttie trouble was seriously affecting N.S.W’s
financial prospects in London, In other words it
was casting a shadow over the next Big Loan. This
is one of the special joys of the big loan policy.
Almost everything that Australia thinks or does
or contemplates is influenced by the fear lest Bull
won t like it, and that it may hinder the next big
loan or the hope that Bull will like it and it may
help the next big loan. y
Joe Cook, M.H.H., has assured his Lith-
gow constituents that he wants to see the iron
ln< * u ?* r y started, but is dead against bonuses
and won t tax the community to contribute to
put up this blast furnace.’’ And if JoS,p|, “tS
also set his face against taxing the community to
contribute to the support of the 100 or 200 or 300
or more men who are out of work for want of that
blast furnaca he would be logical. This country
has had a long-spell of relief-works now; also of
paying bonuses to the farming industry in the
shape of reduced rents, and hopelessly unprofitable
railways, roads, bridges, and so on.
As a consequence of a long and inex-
pressiMy wearymg session of N.S.W. Assembly
both the Speaker and the Chairman of Committees
are HI with nerves.” It is not the long hours of
sitting only, it is the mental agony to a man of
even moderate intelligence, of having to sit still
without cursing aloud whilst the average N.S.W
-Parliamentary debate unfolds its dreary length
of fatuous insults, boorish recriminations, asinine
scenes and unutterable stupidity. A sensible
speech is a rarity; insolence and folly are almost
constantly displayed. The onlooker for any
length of time feels inclined to go out for a
blunderbuss or to throw the furniture about or
to shriek his disapproval. But the Speaker,
the helpless Speaker, chained down by his office
must calmly, courteously listen to all. No sane
insurance-office would cover a N.S.W. Speaker’s
life without heavy “ loading.”

Federal postal
department has
called tenders for a
daily steamer service to carry mails between
Tasmania and Vic oria ; this is a point
on which Senator Keating has persistently agi-
tated. A request that the service shall be -
exclusively a Commonwealth one has been made;
if agreed to it would bar out the Union S.S. Co.
of Maoriland, which holds the trade just now.
The Westralian Upper House was grimly
hostile to Premier James’s Factories Bill from the
very beginning. The Bill in its original state
included factories, workshops, and shops, and also
offices, and in that respect it was about the most
advanced apd liberal measure Australia has yet
seen. The Council first “amended” it to make
it apply to boot and clothing factories and nothing,
else. Then it restricted it to boot and clothing
factories where at least 20 persons were employed.
Finally, the House of Fossils decided that even
this was too liberal, and kicked out the remnant
of the measure.
It is now stated, as an aggravation of the
grievance of the -Six Hatters, that “ the labor
societies knew of their coming as far back as.
February last.” Possibly—but where is the
point ? If the labor societies had known before
the deluge that would make no difference in the
law, or in the fact that boss hatter Anderson had
to comply with the law. The labor societies
didn’t hinder the Six Hatters landing, and they
had no locus standi to apply for admission on
their behalf. Even if the labor societies had
known last February that Christmas was coming
as well as the hatters, they couldn’t prevent it
coming.
Treasurer Shiels continues to rise to the
occasion, like a long weak rag balanced on end, in
Victoria. He now estimates the deficit, to be met
by new taxation and “savings” at £957,58(5, and
he proposes to meet it thus£33B,ooo by fakes
(£lOO,OOO taken from sinking fund ; £lOO,OOO from
the Mallee land fund which Turner set apart for-
the reduction of the public debt; £60,000 from the
fund provided to pay compensation for railway
accidents; £50,000 from railway stores fund ; and
£28,000 from other loan-redemption funds);
£250,000 is to be raised by new taxes (i.c., by
levying income-tax on people with small incomes,
£180,000; and by levying probate duty on small
estates, £70,000). The rest is to be made good by
cutting down wages, charities vote, and municipal
subsidy—the last being almost the only decent
item in the programme. As for the general land-,
tax—well, there isn’t to be any general land-tax*-
only free railway passes for landlords.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Carruthers an-
nounced his political programme last Friday (the
“Liberal platform” it was called in the" daily
papers), and behold, it was just the platform of
See and O’Sullivan, also Reid, Parkes, M'Millan,
Bruce Smith, Dibbs, old Jack Robertson, anti
everybody else. Same old planks to a chip ; same
old platitudes to a hair. This is it:—
Maintenance of the principles of Responsible Govern-
ment.
General legislation to be passed upon sound Liberal
and humane principles tending to the common good, and
not upon class interests.
Ministerial administration to be just, firm and im-
partial.
Constitoosh’nal reform.
Economy in State expenditure.
Local Government reform.
Progressive, but prudent, public works policy.
The writer knows where that policy was borrowed
from ; years and years ago he owned a ramshackle
old ten-head mining battery that used to say
exactly the same thing. For days at a stretch it
would dully pound out the words “Ec-o-no-my in.
State ex-pen-di-ture ” as it hit the quartz—the
fourth stamper was especially cronk and
spoke in italics. Then when it was work-
ing more smoothly, or the stone was easier
to crush, it would change its tune and talk
about “Con-s<i<-oosh-nal re- form” for days at
a stretch. There was a hurried shambling 15-head
mill further down the creek which always said
that the “ principlesofresponsiblegovernment-
mustbemaintained, ” but it lost almost all the gold
and was a generally useless and despised machine.

Mr. S. A. PALMER, 9th April, 1900.

medicine, VITADATIO, has worked on me. About 12

others to try VITADATIO.

Recently experimented with, in Sydney,
from a seventh floor in a Wynyard-Square build-
ing, the Zimmerman patent fire-escape. The
apparatus consists of a strong cane basket, bound
with iron, provided with rope of any length re-
quired, and fitted with two guys for steadying
purposes, which have first to be flung down and
attached to an iron ring sunk in the pavement.
The rope is made fast to an iron bar fixed to the
inside wall of the room, and the occupant of the
basket can perfectly regulate the rate of descent.
Victorian Leg. Council, at the instance of
Mr. Balfour, has tacked on to an administrative
Amending Education Bill, a provision which, if
assented to, will strike the first blow at the Vic.
system of secular education. At present, there is
no religious instruction in Vic. State schools, but
clergymen are permitted to use school buildings
after school hours to impart facts about Zedekiah
and Jonah to those children who chose to stay.
The Balfour proposes to change this system by
keeping in all the youngsters until the religious-
minded ones have finished. While religious in-
struction was being given, they were to be kept at
toil on vulgar fractions, or something of that sort.

John Forrest, in passing through Ade-
laide, attended at the University to. receive an
honorary LL.D. The undergraduates sang at
him a doxology :
Praise John, through whom the water* pour
At Hannan’s Town to each back door.
God made Fremantle rather warm,
But John has made it safe from storm.
A skeleton slowly descended upon him from the
roof as he took his degree, but what (if of any-
thing) this was a symbol was not indicated.
A Vic. railway retrenchment chicken came
home to roost the other day in the shape of a ver-
dict for £llOO and costs for the killing of a carter.
This was the result of the cheeseparing policy that
puts women in charge of gates that men should
look after, and allows an electric warning signal to
remain out of order because there is no one
available to fix it up. That verdict will take a
good many days’ pay of gangers to make up.

the reasons for Mr. Chamberlain’s trip to S’ Africa
was that:
he might be absent from Parliament during the debates
on the Education Bill, as the pro-clerical policy of his
party must necessarily be distasteful to him if he retains
any shred of his Non-conformist convictions.
But that consideration surely would not have
weighed with so experienced a Judas.

The KTEJ'W SHORTHAND (Rgd).
COUNTRY PUPILS.
Distance is no object, as the New Shorthand is wonderfully easy, and may
be taught by post. A Lesson Book accompanies each lesson, and postage
must be paid by pupil both ways. Letters of inquiry will not be answered unless the postage be prepaid.
NEW SHORTHAND
is the simplest yet invented, and the great beauty of it is its legibility
to be read when written. No matter whether smart or dull, you learn
in 21 lessons. The Dull pays no more to learn than the Smart. Take as long as you choose between lessons.
Master one, and then come (or send) for the next.

newspaper tipsters’ selections for the Saturday races—
before they are run. A few hours later that card, as a
rule, makes melancholy reading for the punters who
looked to it for guidance. The tipping record for five
days’ racing, from Nov. 24th to Dec. 2nd, was as follows :
