A grand total of 353 newspaper selections—some of them
“odds-on" chances—yielded 17 successful tips. The
prophets, on an average, picked a winner once in every
21 guesses I
• * • •
There’s lamentation in Israel over Sydney pro-
prietary race clubs’ raising the betting fees to a guinea a
day. Extra expense of stipendiary stewards' pay is the
club’s excuse, but Ikey will yield considerably over and
above that outlay. Recently W.A.T.O. approved of
following fees being exacted from each of that section’s
raucous-voiced : Enclosure, 70 guineas ; flat, 35 guineas.
Eastern Ikey has much to be thankful for still.
• * • ■
Winner (Cherson) of A. J.C. Nursery, Saturday,
succeeded in handsome fashion. He was bought at foot
of brood mare Nada by present owners (Messrs. H. E. and
A. V. White) at St. Albans stud dispersal—both going for
450 guineas, with the fact that Nada had been served
again by Oherson’s sire—Bill of Portland (imp.) thrown
in.
* • • •
“ Shop ” tote and double business of Adelaide,
which for long brought heaps of joy and coin to
those in it, is probably a thing of the past now. Pro-
vision in the Act, that gives police power to prosecute all
found in the “ shops,” did the trick effectively. Falling
off was so great at one big place that, where 3000 half-
crowns were previously invested in one afternoon, less
than 300 passed over the counter latterly. Last shop put
up its shutters t’other day, mainly through police parad-
ing outside, and thereby scaring business off. Meanwhile,
“perambulating totes” (in the persons of individuals
with capacious pockets and a “ gun ” appearance gene-
rally) and straight-out betting goes on in the streets as
merrily as ever.
* * • •
Big Sydney Tatt.’s dislike of new National
Sporting Club is explainable. Tatt.’s offer no induce-
ment but facilities to bet and wet; the National
Sporting Club has attractive gymnasium, baths,
etc. So Tatt.’s has decreed that none of its
members shall belong to a “sporting club.” About
200 members are concerned. Sydney Commercial
Travellers’ Club, Amateur Sports Club, and other organ-
isations all come under the head of sporting; ’twill be
interesting to watch if the big Ikey concern will bar
them. All Sydney Tatt.’s legislation is for the protection
and fattening of Ikey Mo—nothing else.
• * * »
Q.T.O.’s three days’ meet disbursed £3130 prize
money. Mosman, M.L.0., owner of Balfour, gathered
£830; M. Callaghan, £305; and M. F. Ramsay, £2lO. The
first loss for many years is reported, £6OO odd, of which
£563 was due to that tote blunder ; but machine receipts
were £3764 less than last year ; the land is beginning to
feel the effeets of both Philp and the drought.
• * • »
The chairman of Melb. Hospital committee
remarks —“ It seems somewhat peculiar that strenuous
and unceasing opposition to the totalisator for even such
a laudable object as charity is the one and only subject
upon which churches and bookmakers are “cordially
agreed.” And it is still more peculiar that the parsons,
who profess to oppose the tote because they think it a
new incentive to gambling, never propose to reduce the
ranks of the bookmakers by imposing a big charity tax
on those protected parasites. Why not a hospital tax of
£lOO a head on the registered Ikev, anyhow ?
* * * •
Bowler Hugh Trumble announces his “ definite
retirement.” ’Tis a pity, from a cricket point of view.
He’s unquestionably the ablest trundler Victoria has
produced. Fifteen years ago he appeared for Vic. against
N.S.W., and has missed but two matches out of the 30—
the Vic. match in ’B9 and the Sydney first fixture last
season. His record is 135 wickets against N.S.W., the
inter-State record. Turner is next with 125, and
Evans third with 107. In England, last season, an
injured hand kept him out of the first matches of the
tour, but he averaged 948.2 overs, 305 maidens, 1988 runs,
140 wickets ; average, 14.20.
• * • •
George Giffen announces that he will not be
able to play for S.A. this season, except in the matches
at Adelaide. He has a good position in the G.P.0., and
finds increasing difficulty in getting away.
* * • •
N.S.W.-S.A. cricket-match started Friday in
Adelaide, Cornstalks at the wickets. Attendance poor,
about 700. Duff and Trumper opened. Jones’ first ball
to Trumper should have had him but Waters dropped it,
but in the next over he held one off Travers, and the
champion was out for 10. Noble followed, and by good
play gilded over with much sterling luck, put up 100 in
2hrs. 20mins. Another eight saw him caught; his score
included a fiver off Giffen and 10 fourers; it was his eighth
“ hundred ” in Sheffield Shield matches four each
opposed to Victoria and S.A. This is the Cornstalk
record. Duff made 94, and then Kirkwood clean bowled
him with a slow full pitch to leg. Stumps drawn—6 down
for 227. Rain made pitch damp and slow, and, bar Pye
(25 n. 0.), there were no more stands and innings closed
for 285, Jones securing bowling honors. S.A. commenced
disastrously; Howell bowled Reedman first ball, and
bagged Hill for 14. Stonewaller Hack batted 2|hrs. for
48 ; Pye caught Geo. Giffen (27) and bowled Kirkwood (0)
in one over. Crow-eaters seemed afraid to hit out, and
the day ended all out for 140. Kelly, as wicket-keeper,
never let one slip him ; bowling honors were Pye’s—4 for
31, then Noble 3 for 34, and Howell 3 for 51.
* * * *
Latest N.S.W batting colt, J. R. Mackay (37
and 57 XI. v. XIII. match), is 22 years old, and full of
sand. He came from Uralla, with 618 n.o. to his credit,
and, since starting cricket between five and six years ago’
has made 27 centuries. There are possibilities about
Mackay.
• • • *
Discarding of Marsh by N.S.W. in S.A. was
Justified by his poor shaping with N.S.W. XIII. against
the XI. Noble is captain, and ’twas he who kicked
against the aboriginal a year ago. Noble says he is unre-
liable, and he is.
• • • *
Within probability that Lord Hawke’s team
may jet meet a N.S.W. eleven in Sydney. At present there
is some slight misunderstanding, but as the N.S.W.
authorities leave the matter in the visiting skipper’s
hands, it is hoped the Englishmen will play one match in
Sydney en route for Brisbane, where they finish up and
leave for Vancouver, March 26.
Cabled that, as a result of the five English tour
tests, Australians got £4248.
• • • •
“Gate” in connection with recent Australian
XI. v. N.S.W. XIII. match panned out £7oo—half goes to
the XI.
* • * •
N.S.W. XIII. v. Australian XI. match ended
as reported in last Bui<lktin. Rain prevented further
play. With N.S.W. 227 and 214 and Australians 191 and
7 for 185 (wanting 65 to escape defeat) contest was a
pretty even draw. Strangely enough, this Australian XI.
began and ended its existence in the rain. It engaged
in 46 matches all told and lost only two.
• * * •
In the N.S.W.-Vic. cricket tussle, Friday, the
Gumsuckers may be short of some good men.
Trumble threatens retirement and close attention to
business ; Armstrong and Saunders are more than doubt-
ful, owing, it is whispered, to a prominent Victorian
player having written to an English umpire that ’twas the
chance of a lifetime for him to “ no-ball" Saunders and
Noble.
■ • * *
R. N. Hickson, who compiled 68 (n. 0.) for
N.S.W. against the Australians, is only 19, and was the
youngest of the team. Last year, for Syd. C.E. Grammar
School, he topped the Great Public Schools’ performances.
He is a finely-built youth of most brilliant promise.
• • • •
N.S.W.-Queensland cricket fixture this week
gives five new men an opportunity. Team is good, but
N.S.W. has such a prolific crop of cricketers that still
another XI., equally good, could have been picked.
• * • •
Whilst touring S’Africa, several members of
the Australian XI. received very flattering offers to
remain, simply to play cricket. However, as no definite
agreements were signed, the wily Australians agreed that
the terms offered were excellent, and then carefully
packed their grip sacks for the return voyage.
• ’* * «
“B.B.” : The idiotic choosing of N.S.W.
cricket-team to play against Vic. and S.A. before the
playing of the XI. v. XIII. match shows how far
a selection committee can go. In the light of recent
events at least three of the dozen should not have
been picked. Marsh trundled horribly, and is a wretched
bat as well. Returned Australs. Howell, suffering from
rheumatism, and Hopkins, who played much below his
English form, would have been better eliminated for
Mackay, Hickson and Howard.
* * * a
Lord Hawke’s team opened Maoriland tour
against an Auckland team last Friday, and won the toss.
Failing light and rain stopped play at 7 for 243. Heavy
downpour during night made things bad for batting,
but 321 were accumulated. Auckland went out
for 120, and at stumps draw, Saturday, the follow-on had
6 down for 31. EngliBhbattingisparticularlystrong.no
tail being visible. Captain Warner’s play is finished and
stylish, and he hits round the wicket and drives clean
and weightily. Top-scorer Fane’s (82) forte is cutting.
Burnup (43) got his mostly with fine behind-wicket shots.
Dawson is brilliant, especially at driving, and Taylor’s
skill is greater than his smiting power. English bowling
stuck Aucklanders up frequently, Burnup being very
deadly with medium-paced deliveries swerving to the off.
He got 4 for 27, and Bosanquet 5 for 44. English fielding,
as with Maclaren’s team, was excellent.
* • * *
The Adelaide “college match,” won easily last
week by Prince Alfred’s, was only notable for a fine
inniegs by “ Solly ” Hill, Clem’s youngest brother, and
the last of that long family to play for the school. Hill
had the bad luck to be run out at 99. At one time there
was a useful Hill-Darling partnership, the latter youngster
being Joseph D.’s nephew. The end of the game was
spoilt by rain, but immediately afterwards 40 sheets of
iron were put down to preserve the wicket for the N.S.W.
match, two days later.
• • * •
There was a free fight at the big college match
in Adelaide on Dec. 13. The boys from St. Peter’s and
Prince Alfred invariably occupy adjoining sections of the
grand-stand, which always ebb and flow with yell or
groan or cheer, as the game fluctuates. On this particular
day, a “ Saint ” began waving a blue flag over the nearest
row of reds. The irritation was endured awhile with
nothing worse than muttered expostulations, but at last
a P.A. seized and tore the bunting. Then arose trouble,
and, ere calm was restored, two policemen had been
tumbled over by the excited boys and several scrimmages
had taken place. The masters discreetly allowed the
tumult to subside without intervention. On the Monday
all the trouble had been forgotten, and Saints and Princes
were mutually cheering each other.
* • * *
Four of the Hill brothers (sons of John Hill,
head of the well-known Adelaide coaching firm) were out
cricketing on Saturday (Dec. 13). Clem., in Sydney,
made 28 ; H. Hill and F. Hill, playing for E. Torrens Chib
in Adelaide, made 56 and 23, not out, respectively ; Stan.
Hill, batting for P.A. College in the big match on the
Oval, compiled 57, notout, in fine style. Surely in first-
class cricket this must be something like a record,
although long ago three Gregorys often took the field
together.
• * • •
When the English cricket-team reached Maori-
land, the usual young man with the fountain pen waited
on sub-captain Warner, Taylor and Burnup, the three
crack batsmen of the team, and asked their opinion on the
proposed increase in the width of the wicket. The
English sports simply said that as they were blowed if
they knew how wide the wicket is at present they
couldn’t say anything about it. Query: How many
cricketers really know the width of a wicket ?
• •• • •
Bananaland’s lathy aboriginal bowler Henry
will figure in Sydney this week. The continent doesn’t
own, just now, a man who can send them in like Henry,
nor does it possess a bowler who so literally lets bowling
run away with him. Authorities credit him with a
speedier delivery (as long as he lasts) than ever Jones
boasted, which means that the black will be a revelation
to Sydney.
* • • *
First-grade rounds in Sydney cricket won’t be
resumed till the return of N.S.W. team from Vic. early
coming month. Other grade competitions, Saturday,
supplied sensational results. Burwood v. ’Varsity saw
former’s fourth wicket go for 101, but not another fell,
and runs totalled 393. Chinn (143) and Baker (125), both
not out, piled the heap up. Second St. George team
hunted leather while five North Sydney men accumu-
lated 548—Sayers (206) and Clarke (143), each still going
strong, contributed the major part of the score. St.
George thirds also came in for a decidedly warm experi-
ence ; Leichhardt-Balmain, compiling 523 for 7 wickets,
had their opponents chasing all the time. Principal
scores were Howe 147 (not out), Walsh 98, and Carrie 93.
Record against St. George for the day was 12 for 1071—a
staggerer. Sundries were much in evidence. Those in
Burwood-’Varsity match reached 61, and Leichhardt-
Balmain v. St. George 86.
* • • *
T’other day, at Bendigo, Harvey Sutton wiped
out in one swoop whilom Sydney Harrier J. F. Dalrvmple’s
Victorian (2.02-5) and Australasian (1.59 1-5) half-mile
records, which had remained unaffected for 10 years.
Two watches showed Sutton’s speed 1.57 4-5, and a third
1.58. There was nothing hair-raising about Dalrymple’s
figures, particularly the Victorian best, and ’tis surpris-
ing, indeed, that no one succeeded in shattering them
both long ago. Last Australasian premiership games in
M.L. saw Queenslander D’Arcy Wentworth equal the con-
tinent’s best to the fraction. Sutton’s feat is rendered
more brilliant by the fact that he did it on a grass track,
which was, upon re-measurement, found 2£yds. in excess
of the 880. Sutton is only 21, has just been through five
years of medical study, aad is prominent at cricket, foot-
ball, and lacrosse.
• • • •
The star performer at the Public Schools sports
in Melbourne this year was N. W. Barratt, of the Scotch
College, an 18-year-old boy, whose success is the more
remarkable, seeing that he hasn’t the use of his hands.
In the Scotch College competitions Barratt won the
100yds., 220yds., and quarter-mile, 120yds. hurdle race
from scratch. Also, he took first prizes for the high
jump (sft. 3in.) and long jump (20ft. 3in.). In the Public
Schools championships he was to the fore in the mile
and J-mile races. After which the name of N. W. Barratt
cropped up again as winner of an Ormond College resi-
dent scholarship, so his brains can’t be all in his feet.
* • • *
World’s premier cyclist “ Majah ” Taylor came
to Sydney by the ’Murkan mail boat Ventura Saturday.
His appearance is youthful, with a frame sturdily put
together ; if he gets into anything like shape he ought to
stagger Australian cyclists. Most of the best riders in
the world, including present champion Ellegaard, have
been beaten by Taylor, who tells that he has ridden
a mile privately in 1.17, and doesn’t see why—when fit —
he shouldn’t break the existing world’s record of
1.15 2-6. If he strikes form here he’ll try.
Maoriland Wheel Race(£7s) went to Geo. Suther-
land, who won from scratch, covering the 2m. in 4.40
and beat 32 competitors. N.S.W. scratch riders would
have won that coin in 20 seconds less than Sutherland
did.
* * * «
Melbourne Bicycle Club conducted its Austral
meeting (spread over four Saturdays through rain) in a
modified odor of sanctity and respectability, though there
was a liberal cohort of flossiedom in the best seats and
transparent yokes.
Attendance was
very moderate—
mostly “paper."
Competitors were
carefully railed off
from the public by
double fences, 6ft.
apart, lest they
should be openly
corrupted by I key
Mo. This precau-
tion was needless,
as the few Ikeys
who attended mere-
ly insinuated their
desire to “ take
two to one ’’ in a
subdued whisper,
and the raucous
roar that used to
he was absent.
Plugger Bill got
tender treatment
from the officials,
who evidently
wanted him to win
at least one race.
To make up for the
absent Don Walker
the club provided
a 5-mile canter for
motor-cars, which
suggested tremend-
ous possibilities for
that form of sport.
Five started, but
only two had much pace ; these covered the 5 u.j
in 9.22 on a grass course. Fastest mile took 148 &&
Winning machine was a 2J horse-power affair, built
by Beauchamp, of Prahran (Vic.) Cutting the sharp
curve round the press-box the winner struck an angle of
65°. On an asphalt saucer track a race between fast
machines would be a hair-raising spectacle.
* • • •
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen should carefully
gag the Yankee wheeler, Major Taylor. At Auckland the
dusky flier was “ doubtful as to his success owing to
changed climatic conditions and being entirely out of
form.” Probably the Major’s idea is to talk himself down
and pose as a monument of modesty and then scoop the
pool in a brilliant burst of excellence ; but if the great
exacting Australian public gets the idea that the Major
is off color as well as black it won’t roll up and leave a
pile of shekels at the gate. Australians generally dislike
boasters, but have an utter contempt and indifference for
the fellow who doesn't boast for all he is worth. They
roll up to see the first fellow taken down, but they don’t
trouble about the latter at all.
» » « •
The rifle-shooting record of the Echuca cadets
won’t be easy to beat. They have won the Sargood
Shield (4 times), the Gumming Shield, and the South-
street Shield—7l consecutive wins against other schools,
and 70 individual prizes.
• • • •
N.S.W. J-mile swimming supremacy went to
Dick Cavill, at Bondi, Saturday, after a tremendous
tussle with George Read—not the Dry Dog. They were
locked till 40yds. from finish, and the crowd made
the welkin (whatever the welkin may be) ring
as Cavill landed the title by a little better than a yard.
Time, 5.49 2-5, is 12sec. slower than the Australasian
best fOavill’s), and 17 2-5 behind the world’s (Billington’s).
Cavill was not at his best, and Read was still apparently
stiff from shipboard life. But he should be back to his
wonderful form of last year during present season.
• * • •
Question as to who won the English “ hundred”
supremacy is now raging in Sydney. Lane, Cavill, and
Englishman Derbyshire were declared first, second and
third respectively. Lane’s yarn is that he touched,
and immediately afterwards heard another “smack
on the wooden boom,” which he knew to be
Cavill’s smack, then, turning, he saw Derbyshire
strike the boom with his head. Cynics want to
know how Lane heard, let alone recognised, Cavill’s
smack, with the cheering of hundreds lifting the
bath-building roof and the tumbling, worked-up water in
his ears. The men in the water would be hardly able to
hear the howling din, let alone a solitary smack on a
wooden boom.
* * • •
Second round of Sydney bowling clubs’ supre-
macy on Saturday was thick with sets-back : Gladstone
Park, Redfern, Randwick. Ashfield and Mosman dropped
out. Balmain, expecting to romp over Ohatswood, went
down by 20. Strathfield-Annandale match, antici-
pated a soft thing for former, resulted other
way about. Redfern, contrary to expectations,
went under to St. Leonards. St. George beat Ash-
field by 39. Stiff westerly affected all greens and
rendered play flukey. Looks now as if City and Victoria
Park will have final to themselves ; but the way of the
bowling prophet is precarious.
• • * «
Alf Thompson-“ Monty” Andrews scrap at
Sydney National Sporting Club was well-attended and
full of ginger. Thirteen rounds of heavy slugging saw
Thompson on top all the time. The second round was
terrific, and the air was so thick with whirling fists the
light couldn’t get through. But for the fact that Boz.
gloves were used the thing would have ended imme-
diately in sudden death, but though Thompson
hit like a steam hammer, Andrews “ smothered ”
like a four-poster mattress. In the eleventh round
Monty’s nose and mouth stopped Thompson’s left
absolutely, and then his body did the same office
for a swing, and a number of new stars so dazzled his
vision that thereafter he couldn’t fight, and the referee
mercifully ended it.
• * • •
Sydney dailies are as bitter against boxing as
against Federation. Tom Mitchell, after his defeat by
Johns, Monday night, broke up completely at beingbeaten.
He thought the fight a good thing, and the shades of his
friends’ lost dollars sat heavily on his soul. Morning after
he was taken to Sydney Hospital he informed friends and
reporters that there was nothing wrong with him, and
that he wished to leave, but sensational cross-headings
and pars, kept on appearing. Even’ Nooze of Thursday
(Mitchell was going to be discharged next day)
published that he had partially recovered but was
still seriously situated. On Saturday night the alleged
dying man sat on his alleged coffin and seconded the
winner Lynch at the Golden Gale fight 1
• • • •
Snowy Sturgeon and Jack Thompson fight for
a £2OO purse and £lOO a side at Sydney National Sporting
Club Tuesday night. Snowy has been vainly seeking
stoush for two years, and ’tis doubtful if he’ll shape as
well as of yore. Thompson hasn’t the skill that Snowy-
still evidences, but he’s a rising tide and has swamped
everything met so far. That low guard arm and his
failure to eat up old, played-out Bill Jennings in quicker
time than he did, seem to indicate that Thompson will
go under.
• • • •
The cyclonic bruiser Lynch, of Wngga, N.S W.
took on Sam Maber (brother of one-time famous
“ Shadow ” Billy) at Sydney Golden Gate A. 0., Saturday
night. Things were lively, though somewhat one-sided,
as Lynch had too much knowledge and condition. Maber
seemed to have lost the vigorous sting that won the
novice tourney and made difficulties for Alf. Thompson
at Sydney Gaiety A.O. Thompson probably punched it
all out, for theirs was a desperate battle. Bruisers are like
fighting dogs sometimes, and a bitter battle takes all the
zest for punishment out of them—as witness 'Murkan
sailor Sharkey, once considered on the world’s champion-
ship plane, but Jeffries, Fitzsimmons and Ruhlin cut him
up so that he’ll have none of the game now. Maher’s
corner gave in at the close of the fifth round, and Lynch
got the decision. Maber will probably drop out of the
business.
The value of preserved and frozen meat
exported from Queensland during the first nine
months of 1901 was £947,944, while for same
period of 1902 it reached £1,390,872 ! To out-
siders these figures would imply a flourishing
state of affairs in the north; but to most
Australians they have another significance.
Stock from hundreds of Q. stations were simply
rushed to the preserving-houses to save them
from dying of starvation, and this, with drought
losses, has so depleted the Q. runs that the next
two or three years will witness a tremendous
slump. Another aspect of the case is that, while
this meat is being retailed abroad at 44d. a lb.,
people in Sydney, Brisbane, and elsewhere are
paying from Bd. to 14d. per lb; and there are
scores who have not tasted meat for months, while
others again are paying 4d. to fid. for wallaby.
A Bulletin correspondent:—
Trenwith thus far has not justified his allegation that
David Syme “ tried to induce ” him to repeal the Vic.
Railway Lands Acquisition Act. Syme declares that he
has never spoken to Trenwith, or had any communication
with him upon any subject in his life. If Trenwith can
produce any evidence of pressure being brought to bear
upon him by the Age proprietor, why doesn’t he produce
it ? As matters stand, there is a very palpable personal
motive for Trenwith’s attacks on the Age and its boss,
but not a sufficient reason for accepting Trenny’s state-
ments whole. Ten years ago the enemies of Syme (whose
collective name is Fatman for the most part) charged
their bUe noir with pure malice in his persistent denun-
ciations of poor Dick Speight’s railway management.
Syme’s newspaper undoubtedly hastened the expulsion of
Speight and the inevitable cutting-down of railway expen-
diture. The imputation of malice in that case looks
absurd to-day. Speight had to go. The Age was attack-
ing a bad railway regime when it established a precedent
for Trenwith’s easy cry of “ Malice 1 ” which, by the way,
is the cry of every sort of public performer who feels hurt
by adverse newspaper criticism. Isn’t it about time that
sombody looked for paltry envy, hatred and malice in the
frothy maligners of David Syme, the wealthy, plain-living,
intellectual hard-grafter?
Astounding item from the American mail-
summary of Australia’s holiest and most proper
daily, S.M. Herald :
FANCY HOSIERY PREVENTS WEDDING.
A telegram from New York on November 18, published
in San Francisco Chronicle, states :—“ The marriage of
Miss Margaret M‘Dermott,of Elizabeth, N, J.,and Terence
Sheehan, of Newark, N.J.,did not take place as scheduled
last evening, in consequence of the discovery by Sheehan
that Miss M'Dermott wore open-work hosiery. The
couple went to the office of a Justice of the Peace in
Jersey City, accompanied by two friends, and said that
they had come to get married. Just before the ceremony
was about to begin, the tying of the bride’s loosened
shoe-lace disclosed the fact to the bridegroom that
she wore open-work stockings. The bridegroom
remarked that he agreed with the views of
the Brooklyn clergyman who recently lost his
pastorate by denouncing open-work hosiery. In the
course of the ensuing dispute between the loving couple,
the groom said he would not marry any girl who wore
such flimsy stockings, and the girl said she would wear
whatever she liked. It all ended by the intended bride
and bridegroom leaving the Justice’s office in angry mood
without the marriage taking place.
Poor Mr. James ! He is too innocent to know that
whenever a woman wears open-work silk stockings
her shoe-laces are eternally becoming untied.

If the fact that Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills

waiting to testify, justice would have no use for

witness to the blood purifying power of Dr.

reliable, and responsible people, whose testimony
would carry weight in any court of law in the

who believe “that all men are created of one

John Smith testifies to a cure, little notice is
taken of the fact; if Senator somebody is cured

blood is the same in King and Kaiser, Prince and
Peasant, Senator and trainp. It is made in'the
same way, of digested food ; it is circulated
the same mechanism. If an Emperor’s blood xa
impure he will have boils or some other eruption,
like the commonest of his subjects. The medicine
that will purify the blood of somebody, will purify
the blood of anybody. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills purify the blood, anybody’s blood—every-
body’s blood, Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
are a positive and permanent cure for biliousness,
constipation, indigestion, headaches, sallow com-
plexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples,
boils and blotches, and for female ailments.*

University Commemoration at Adelaide
caused the usual trouble with the undergrads.
The Chancellor (Judge Way) is not popular, and
the presence of the Tennvsons (who are) effected
no restraint. The boys have a printed programme
of highly personal songs, a verse for each
degree-taker, and a pause is always made
for them to sing; but they don’t keep to the im-
plied bargain, interrupting all through with yelis
and particularly exasperating electric bells. 1 his
year Judge Way tried giving them all an enter-
tainment the evening before, but it made no dif-
ference when the time came. The Register
claims that they should either be excluded alto-
gether or kept in order by the police.
Melbourne Hospital has been reduced to
the necessity of supplying rabbits to the patients
in place of poultry. The finances of the hospital
are going to eternal smash. The Government,
led by Iceberg Irvine and Shiels the raven, cut
down the subsidy, and the general policy of the
same two dismal, shortsighted politicians, in
bearing down wages all round, has had the effect
of interfering with the flow of private donations.
Treasurer Shiels suggested to a deputation
recently that the churches should support the
totalisator and raise a charity revenue in that
way. Against this the goody-goodies have kicked
sorely. Perhaps it would be more in accordance
with the wishes of these people if a special tax
was placed on church land values for the benefit
of the charities.
Melbourne Argus says :
There is no proposal in the Reform Bill to deprive the
public servants of their franchise.
Quite true; who said there was ? It is simply
claimed that in many places the votes of two or
three dozen public servants may turn the scale at
an election, therefore by giving the civil servants
two members of their own and shutting them out
of the general body of electors their influence is to
be diminished. And if other classes were treated
similarly the civil servants would have nothing to
complain of. Two or three dozen teetotallers, or
landlords, or Orangemen, or Presbyterians, or
farmers, or Roman Catholics, or pick-and-shovel
men, or shopkeepers, also turn the scale at many
elections, and each of these classes should similarly
be shut out of the general body of electors and
have members of its own. In fact, any class
found turning the scale at any election should be
similarly treated.

"Bead That Again!
The man and woman stood at the altar and the
minister was engaged in the interesting\task of
uniting them in the bonds of holy matrimony.
The ceremony proceeded without interruption
until the minister inquired of the bride, “ Will you
love, honor, and obey this man ? ” when, without
allowing the bride time to respond with the usual
“I will,” the bridegroom exclaimed : “Read that
again please; read it dead slow. I want the lady
to get the inward and spiritual meaning of those
beautiful words. Pve been married before.”
That bridegroom was a knowing man. Not
only does much interesting matter entirely escape
being read, but much that is read receives so little
attention that the readers profit nothing. Some
people think it a waste of time to read advertise-
ments ; but what of the numerous genuine cases
of men who have starved in this country while
they were being ineffectively invited by advertise-
ment to step forward and claim fortunes to which
they were entitled in England ? What also of the
numerous sufferers from indigestion who daily
ignore announcements of a world-renowned
remedy entirely effectual for the removal of their
trouble? Mr. Joseph Cooper, the well-known
and respected artistic wood carver, of Currie-St.,
Adelaide, S.A., is not one of these On the 22nd
March, 1902, Mr. Cooper stated : “For two years
I was a great suffer from indigestion and sluggish
liver. I had no appetite, and the little food 1 ate
lay on my chest like a leaden weight. I frequently
had pains in the chest and between the shoulder
blades, the latter sometimes of extreme severity.
I used to dread to go to bed, because of a sense of
suffocation which I there experienced and of my
inability to sleep. After a restless night I would
rise with a furred tongue, a bad taste in the
mouth, feeling weak, nervous, and exhausted, and
in a very poor condition to begin work. I tried
many remedies, but though a few of them afforded
me slight temporary relief none of them, did me
any permanent good. About twelve months ago
I read an advertisement which induced me to try
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup. By the time I
had taken the first bottle my health was much
improved, and three bottles made me almost well
again. However, I continued to take the Syrup
until I had used seven bottles in all, by which
time I was thoroughly cured, not having suffered
in the least from indigestion or liver troubles
since. Thanks to Mother Seigel’s Curative SyrUp
I can now eat heartily any food I have a liking
for, can sleep well, and enjoy life generally. I
recommend it to one and all with the'utmost con-
fidence.”*

In N.S.W. Assembly, the other night.
Kelly, M.L.A., moved that the minimum wage
for railway and tramway men and policemen
should be 7s. a day. Fitzpatrick proposed that it
should be Bs. a day. Sleath suggested 10s. a day,
but just then the House adjourned, and an
interesting subject dropped.
Promotion goes by seniority in the Vic-
torian Public Service. Recently a clerk was
wanted for the Attorney-General’s office, and by
the seniority rule a lighthouse-keeper had first
claim on the billet. Premier Irvine, who tells
this story, doesn’t say whether the lighthouse-
keeper got it or not; but even if lie had, he
wouldn’t have been a much greater absurdity than
market-gardener Bent as Minister of Railways.
Sydney D.T. waxed most virtuously in-
dignant during the Melba concert season at the
overcrowding of Sydney Town Hall, dilating on
the prospect of a panic in such a crowd ; so the
gaudy Mayor directed his orderly, Earl (or so)
Martin Carrick, to reside permanently on the
premises, to keep the unseemly crowds in their
right place. The D.T. greeted this arrangement
with great pleasure, and the very next day came
out with a trumpet-yell of satisfaction at G. H.
Reid’s big meeting—“ packed to the door; aisles,
gangways and passages crammed.” This audi-
ence, the biggest the D.T. ever saw, was presided
over by his Illuminosity the gaud Mayor himself,
his orderly, Baron (or Duke) Martin Carrick,
lending his moral support.
S. Australia’s best-known locally-grown
cartoonist, J. H. Chinner, has descended to a
suburban mayoralty. The only extenuating cir-
cumstance is £lOO a year.

Scepticism Thwarted.
Such Daily and Hourly Proof Must

The Case of tyr. JANILS WINGFIELD.
(BY A SYDNEY SCRIBE.)
What with the seven years’drought, a re-
urreuce of the plague in our midst, and
shucks of earthquake in various parts of the
country,it is high time that a definite policy
for purposes of personal defence should be
dopted, and altli ugh there are some
elements of disaster which cannot be re-
sisted, it is no reason why we should not
make the most of the opportunities at our
disposal for overcoming some of them.
Effective aid in the shape of advice is given
below by Mr. James VN ingfield, of Ko. 14
Garden Avenue, Glebe, who says
“When I came from the country seven years
ago I was down for eighteen months with
inflammation of the lungs and pneumonia.
My constitution was fairly broken up, as for
nearly three years I was not able to do a
stroke of work, and, of course, that meant
everything going out and rot ring coming
in. The consequence was that as soon as I
could get about at all I took the first job
available. The work was heavv —much too
heavy for me, in fact —so that what little
strength I had was soon exhausted ; but it
was not until I severely strained my back
that I realised the great risk I had been
Following closely upon that mis-
hap, I caught a terrible cold in the kidneys,
which, in turn, was attended by an awful
attack of influenza, thus showing how one
misfortune follows another. I managed to
jog along in extreme difficulties until the in-
fluenza came, but that proved too much for
me altogether, as I went right under as
soon as it seized me.”
“ Was it necessary to take to your bed? ”
asked the p: essman.
“ Rather.® The pains fn my head were
so intense that I could not hold it up, and
my temperature rose to an alarming degree.
My back ached worse than ever, while my
limbs were so much affected in the same
way that I could not get any ease no matter
in what position I lay. Supervening upon
those torture j came a burning pain in my
eyes, over which I had two bandages placed,
and also one around iny forehead, to see if
my agony could be reduced. I must have
looked as if Iliad been at the Boer war,
but I can tell you it was no laughing matter,
for 1 could neither eat nor sleep, and I
deeply regretted to find that the cold was
getting on my chest, which became clogged
up during the night, and in the morning, as
soon as daylight came, I started to cough,
and a very heavy feeling oppressed me till
I got my chest cleared again. However, I
have to be very thankful that at the critical
moment, when there was every likelihood of
a prolonged and calamitous illnes* being
again experienced, I resorted to a medicine
which had previously enlisted my implicit
confidence, as, by its timely use, the im-
pending destruction was averted. To be
explict, I must tell you that after my ill-
ness with pneumonia I took to using
Clements Tonic for the purpose of recuperat-
ing my vitality, and I met with so much
success through the revivifying powers of
that medicine that I naturally looked to it
to strengthen me again. But Clements
Tonic had a chance of doing more this time,
and it was quite equal to the occasion. You
see, when I was lying prostrate with in-
luenza I did not waste much time before
,etting Clements Tonic, and thus that
readful complaint was held in check. My
•ruciating head pains were very shortly
lieved, and the agonising manner in which
v eyes were affected was greatly minimised,
•i wonderful relief I then felt through
agency of Clements Tonic helped me
,ig considerably, and as my strength w'as
dually improving under the same
;edy, so was there more vital power in
system to fight against my adversities.”
‘You mean,” said the reporter, “that
ur disease made no further headway.”
“ Exactly,” was the enthusiastic reply.
“It was like a pitched battle between two
gladiators, and there is no doubt that
Clements Tonic won easily, as it prevented
the cold from getting a further hold upon
my chest, and the heaviness and difficulty
in breathing were entirely removed. I
used to think that I had lost control of my
nerves completely, because they trembled
so whenever anything happened to excite
me, and, besides, I got-irritable at the least
thing. If I chanced to hear anybody
whistling or singing I felt annoyed, for it
seemed that everybody could be
happy but myself, and that is a very
melancholy frame of mind to be in, believe
me. But the Clements Tonic I was taking
quickly held the upper hand -over these
troubles, for my nervous system was re-
stored to order, and the general sense of
ease and comfort which I was gaining had
the effect of making my mind more peaceful
and contented. The awful complication of
complaints from which I had suffered had
made such a wreck of me that I could not
get quite strong for some time, as, in the
meantime, I felt languid and drowsy all
day, and with my limbs for ever aching ;
but it was a grand thing that Clements
Tonic had gone so far as to make me well
enough to go back to my woik, although for
a while I felt very exhausted when I came
home at nights and was always glad when
Sundays came round.”
“ Were you resting better then than
before ? ”
“ Oh, yes. I found that Clements Tonic
was a fine medicine for making me sleep,
and I was pleased to feci refreshed and
active when I woke up because I had pre-
viously been put to such distress on getting
out of bed that 1 had to straighten myself
gradually on account of tli* pains in my
back, which at last yielded to the curative
properties of Clements Tonic, like the rest
of the miseries which I had suffered from.
After a certain period my shattered con-
stitution was properly welded together
again, and whenever I have been asked how
it was done my friends have always rec i ved
the same reply- through using Clements
Tonic, and so long as I* live it will be a
pleasure for me to advise all sufferers to
take the same medicine for curing tin ir
complaints. Food had to be very tempt-
ing, and even then I could not always touch it
befor e taking to Clements Tonic, but after-
wards I felt so hungry that I could take a fair
quam-ity of any kind of food, and thoroughly
enjoy it. Moreover, my digestive organs
were so benefi ed by the use of Clements
Tonic, that the assimilation of my food
followed as a matter of course, and
I suppose that is one of the princi-
pal reasons why flatulence, headaches,
body pains, giddiness, and other physical
defects do not trouble me now.”
“ Have you any objection to this state-
ment being published ? ”
r ‘ Not the slightest. You can publish
these words of gratitude to Clements
Tonic in any way you may prefer.”

At Poverty Point.
Harry Rickards’# application for an injunction to
restrain Barney Fagan and wife, Henrietta Byron, from
appearing in the panto, at Her Majesty’s, Melb., disposes
finally of the rumor that there is any sort of business
partnership or arrangement between Rickards and
Williamson, or Musgrove. W. and M. are partners for
the present as regards the Melbourne panto., so the
Rickards action is directed against both of them.
The case of Rickards v. Barney and Mrs. Fagan (other-
wise Fagan and Byron) is an interesting lesson in the
ways of the theatrical world. Rickards engaged Fagan
and Byron for a period of six months, at a salary of £65 a
week, with the ordinary business proviso that they were
not to enter into any eng igement with a rival Austral-
asian manager within six months of the termination of
the Rickards’ contract. Having served their term
with Rickards, who offered them a re - engagement
which they didn’t accept, Fagan and Byr'. i sold
their services to Williamson and Musgrove in defiance
of the proviso aforesaid. Rickards, last week, ob-
tained an injunction to restrain F. and B. from
fulfilling their new engagement, but he practically con-
sented to cancel the restraining clause in their contract
if they pay £250 compensation. On behalf of the Fagans,
who had no legal leg to stand upon, it was pleaded that,
although they received £1570 salary from Rickards for the
six months previous to Oct. 18 of this year, they are now
in an unfinancial condition and must either work for
Williamson and Musgrove or “sit here and starve.”
Actors and their money arc soon parted, seemingly.
“T.D.” : By the sudden lunatic outbreak of Cornelius
Osmond whilst having a singing lesson with a Melbourne
teacher, that city loses a voice which was bound to have
been heard of as soon as Kirby and one or two others had
taken their share of public esteem. The voice was much
the same in quality, and a l rifle more robust and reliable
on the higher notes than Kirby’s. Also, Osmond was a
worker, and went into his music thoroughly.
Alice Crawford, the bonnie actress from Bendigo,
writes during her first week in London “ Mrs. Maes-
more Morris had a small part in * Chance, the Idol.’ She
looked lovely, which was practically all that was required
of her. Titheradge played the father of the heroine—a
small part, unsuited to him. But Titheradge is always
artistic. Went to see all the musical comedies. I like
• The Toreador ’ the best; Maie Saqui was amongst the
show girls. Louie Freear, who plays the slavey
in “Chinese Honeymoon’’ is too grotesque and
funny for words. If Nance O’Neil had opened
in something new, instead of courting compari-
son with Bernhardt, Duse, Mrs. Pat. Campbell,
and others, I believe she would have had a successful
season. Everybody thought her clever, though not clever
enough to follow the greatest artists in powerful plays.
. . . It’s hard to get a good engagement over here. The
market is overstocked, and wealthy amateurs are pre-
pared to pay for positions. You would be surprised at
the salaries really clever actors are glad to accept; a
leading lady in a touring co. in England does not get as
good a salary as I got in Australia when I had only been
on the stage a few months, and m.y pay, as you know, has
never soared as high as the dreams of avarice. The Aus-
tralians I have met over here seem to be doing very little.
... I got a Bulletin wherein I was told I was 24. Tell
my friend the libeller that a woman never forgives a
cruelty of that sort. I am 22. If Tub Bulletin doesn’t
believe me, it can look up the Bendigo register.” •
Every day for the last eight or ten weeks Melb. Bijou
has been advertised “To let, on long lease or short
seasons.” The lessee, J. A. Wilson, who has paid for the
insertions of this Jin. announcement immediately under-
neath the business advt. of Hawtrey, his tenant, seems
to harbor the delusion that theatrical managers wander
around casually with the company looking for suitable
premises to act in.
Two enterprising Melbourne girls—Miss Maud Gran-
tham and Lucy Barton—duly brought off a concert
at Hobart the other night, and were asked to give
another, so they might do worse than pitch their tent
and their talent in Tassy for a while, before going West.
Several of the Marshall Hall Conservatorium damsels
have migrated to Germany, but Miss Grantham and
Premier Barton’s niece are the first of the Prof.’s ex-
pupils to launch their own little show upon Australian
waters, so to speak.
About some people known to Australians who are now
in America :—Oscar Asche has made a great hit as
Maldonada in “Iris” at N.Y. Criterion. His very
brutality in the part wins its way into the feminine heart
and the box-office is ever in a state of feminine siege’
Herbert Ross (recently identified with the “ Message from
Mars” in Australia) is likewise in the cast; also Hilda
Spong as the friend of the wilful Iris. She shortly joins
William Faversham, another of Frohman’s stars, in “ Im-
prudence,” a new play by H. V. Esmond, author of “ One
Summer’s Day.” William Elton has been playing in
serious drama again. He supported Brandon Tvnan
m “Robert Emmett,” but fell so seriously ill 'that
he had to retire to hospital for an operation. He
is now seeking recovery among the Catskill Mountains
Maud Hobson brightens up the tedium of “ The Night of
the Party” which Weedon Grossmith, author, actor and
manager, is presenting to New Yorkers at the Princess
Theatre. Maud Hobson’s face, figure and gowns are all
in the key of G.—which is a Yankee’s most eloquent
remark of appreciation. The beauteous lady (who, by the
way, is Toorakian by birth),is seriously threatening matri-
mony and retirement to domestic solitude in S’Africa.
Harry R. Roberts, recently married to Maggie Moore is
doing distinctive work in Belaseo’s “Du Barry.” He’ is
under a three years' contract to David Belasco. Kyrle
Bellew has been bobbing on the outskirts of New York in
Stanley Weyman’s “ Gentleman of France." He threatens
a Shakespearian revival shortly.
J. C. Williamson is understood to have in view a
comedy co. for playing two or three of Brough’s pieces.
“The Second in Command ’’is mentioned.
“Kookoo”: That injury to Adelaide Porter’s hand
(mentioned B. 5/10/’O2) was the result of a playful blow
from her fellow-student, the now famous Clara Butt.
Clara’s play, like her person and voioe, is evidently of the
massive order. London specialists pronounced the injury
irremediable, but, some years after the sufferer’s return
to S.A., Prof. Watson, of Adelaide University, diagnosed
a “ tangle” of tendons in the wrist or forearm, and an
operation restored the use of the fingers. Miss P. had
meanwhile learnt to play pieces written for the left hand
and kept up the accomplishment. She married, a few
years ago, a popular Adelaide lawyer, Jack Fox, who died
only a few days before your par. appeared.
A. P. Sykes, of Melbourne, is offering to an enlightened
world a new lot of “ red-label ” gramophone records at
the grand operatic price of ten shillings each. Madame
Suzanne Adams and other costly singers have contributed
to the red-labet series, hence the extra charge for these
records. Some of them are wonderful reproductions of
fine voices, and in every case the first-class art of
the vocalist is distinctly marked. It is still an
open question as to whether the wax record used
for the “phonograph” gives a keener reproduction
than the vulcanite gramophone record, which is much more
durable ; but, anyhow, the fact remains that the greatest
singers in the world are now prepared to warble into the
" Gram.” receiver for a thumping big fee. Two or three
years ago one sort of voice was much the same sort as
another for gramophonic purposes, but under the im-
proved system of registering it is good business for the
manufacturers to pay star singers 50 guineas a time, and
more, for one song.

In Hoy a Gully.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
vV henever the monotony of the manage at Wir-
rowah became no longer bearable, Donald King
would take his horse and ride over to the Hoya
Gully—a fern-clad rift cut deep in the bosom of
rolling plains—which made a natural divide
between the Wirrowah run and Beattie’s selec-
tion. He sought it, in the first instance, because
the artistic side of his nature delighted in sharp
contrasts, and to compare the Hoya Gully with
Wirrowah Homestead was an object lesson in
opposites. The gully was not a mile away from
the homestead—a collection of aggressively new
and disreputably old buildings set in an enclosure
called by courtesy a garden. King had to m ike
a bee-line westward over the coarsely-grassed,
treeless plains to reach it, and, once there, he could
forget the utter ugliness of the place in
which he had taken up his abode for
the last two months—of the house, which
was never free from the shouts and loud
laughter of Jack Broughton’s hoyden sister, as
she raced up and down the verandahs with her
troop of dogs, a veritable imp of darkness with
attendant devils, in King’s estimation; of the
dreadful garden, with its heaps of shavings and
rotting slabs of bark and growth of nettle weeds,
which was divided from the endless yellow plain
by a wire fence only.
Those plains, with their weird mirages and
shadeless distances, were depressing enough in
themselves ; but there was shade and to spare in
the Hoya Gully—that sweet caprice of Nature—
for it was rock-lined in a land where one might
travel leagues and never see so much as a pebble.
There were shrubs bearing brilliant brown and
yellow blossoms, and billows on billows of pale-
green maidenhair fringing the cold spring pools
at the bottom, and from the trees which were
rooted in the rich soil between the rock ledges
hung trails on trails of the pink waxen flower,
threaded on slender brown strings, from which
the place derived its name.
And it was to gather the hoya that the girl
from Beattie’s selection made her way there one
morning when Donald had taken possession, and
after that he had another reason for seeking the
seclusion of the gully on every possible occasion.
He was basking, lizard-like, on a flat, sun-
flecked rock when the girl first saw him, and
being a good looking young fellow, with an olive
skin and dreamful blue eyes and a figure as lithe
and straigiit as a blood wood tree, small wonder
that Miry Beattie compared him inwardly with
the man Fate had allotted to her—a red-bearded,
pale-eyed teamster, whose figure was built more
for use than for beauty.
And then Donald looked up and saw her and
thought at once howkissablea mouth she had, and
how pretty she was in her brown frock amongst
the yellow flowers, and her evident shyness and
desire to run away bred in him the desire to make
her stay.
He had qufte a fight to break down the barrier
of her reserve ; but it made matters all the more
interesting, and it was quite a long time before
he grew tired of riding every day to Hoya Gully
and seeing the girls’cheeks flush redly as he came.
Even after he was tired of it, he continued to kiss
her from force of habit, and because the affair
made an artistic and pretty interlude in his long
visit to the station of his old school chum, Jack
Broughton.
Mary Beattie was a dull person for all her good
looks, and was quite incapable of making fine
distinctions. She thought Donald was in love
with her because he had made love to her, and so
put an end to the idyll abruptly one day when she
sprung upon him the fact that she intended to give
her teamster fiance the go-by and was quite
prepared and willing to stick to King for the
term of his natural life !
It was a shock certainly, but King, still in the
early twenties, was a man of some experience.
He had a silver tongue, even in those days, and
he set to work to argue the girl out of her foolish-
ness, without raising her anger or making matters
needlessly uncomfortable. He proved to her
that his kisses and attentions had been but the
ordinary advance-guards of friendship, he showed
her how impossible any other sentiment could be
between them, and he scouted the idea of dis-
honorably stealing another man’s promised bride.
Mary was a docile creature, and, after a few
tears, was dumbly acquiescent. Certainly she
asked one question, which Donald took as a proof
of her innate stupidity and answered as toler-
antly as he could, though it necessitated the going
over again of all his arguments. “If you'd no use
for mq, #iy didn’t you let me alone? I was
willin’ tb be let alone at first,” she said. How-
ever, lie kissed her quite affectionately when she
finally departed, and he felt tenderly towards her
for being so sensible ; and his heart glowed with
virtuous satisfaction when he considered how very
much worse his conduct might have been !
He was le iving Wirro wah next day, en route for
tie city again, and would think twice before he spent
another vacation in the baekbloeks, even if it
was in a district which knew his name for two
generations and which was part and parcel of
certain political plans of the future. There wou'd
only be one more evening on the verandah, listen-
ing to Jack’s uninteresting “sheep” talk—only
one more morning's sleep broken by the clatter of
the impish sister’s strong leather boots on the flat
tin roof of his room ! . . . He turned swiftly in
the saddle—for he had just mounted his horse—to
see the cause of the noise behind him. Stones
were falling freely in Hoya Gully, and through
the fringe of tussocky grass above it he saw the
head and shoulders of Broughton’s sister appear-
ing- . i
There was a swirl of short skirts and a flash of
long legs, and before he could collect his thoughts
the creature had covered the distance between
them, and had sprung to the horse’s back, grasp-
ing its rider in sinewy, vyce-like arms, as she bent
her head sideways, so that she could peer into his
face.
“ I saw it all,” she said. “ I heard every word,”
and she grinned delightedly.
“Get off—let go—or I’ll shove you off 1 ” said
King, irately.
“I’lltell Jack if you do, and he’ll set the whole
place against you when you come electioneering
some day,” said Miss Broughton, calmly. “ Tell
me something I want to know,” she added,
insinuatingly, “ and I’ll let go and never breathe
a word to a soul, s’elp me ! ”
“Well,” said King, “what is it?” He knew
the Imp too well not to capitulate.
She wriggled sideways until her thick dark hair
fell over his arm and her gimlet eyes looked
straight into his angry blue ones, and she
repeated Mary Beattie’s question of a few
moments back.
“ Why didn’t you leave ner alone? ” she said.
“I want to knew the real, truly honest reason
Why you kissed her so at first—l’d like to know
aw’fly. You don’t love her, do you ?”
“ No, I dbn’t.”
“ Well, what did you kiss her for first ? ”
■ ‘ Jiecause I wanted to,” he said ; “I hadn’t
any other reason. I just wanted her then ; I
don’t now ; and I don’t want you either, so scoot
—do you hear me, scoot ! ” „ 1 „„„
And, true to her promise, the Imp of Darkness
slid to the ground and “scooted.” She had got
the truth and knew it, and Donald King swore
softly to himself as he watched her ungainly night
over the grass tussocks. , „ , ,
“ Little devil ! ” he muttered, “it s the hrst and
last time you'll ever score off me! ” And a sudden
breeze sweeping the Hoya Gully behind him
brought an echo of far-off exultant laughter, ne
shivered a little, and put his horse at the gallop
until he drew rein in sight of the unsightly huts
at Wirrowah.
11.
Many tilings may happen in ten years, and
Donald King realised that a great many things
had happened when ho saw Wirrowah again.
For one thing, the pepperina trees in the garden
were no longer tiny plants, but made a bower of
green and a brave show of scarlet berries, through
which the verandah of Wirrowah homestead
showed invitingly. It was draped with flowering
creepers also, and the array of squatter chairs
was supplemented by a white wickerwork one,
before which stood a table spread with a dainty
tea-service, presided over by a goddess in
brown grasscloth, who was none other than
the Imp, “knocked into shape,” as Jack
expressed it, by some years at a good
Sydney school. She was a very handsome! girl
indeed, and King wondered how lie could ever
have thought those eyes “gimleting” as they
gazed admiringly at him through a fringe of the
longest black eyelashes he had ever seen. I say
admiringly, because from the very first Miss
Francie Broughton made nodisguiseof her feelings.
King was going to stand for the district at the
coining elections, and was taking a trip round to
see how the land lay, and the girl was a useful
coadjutor when she volunteered to ride over to the
different selectors’ places with him. She was so
fine a specimen of womanhood that he forgot,
after a time, the feelings of mingled amusement
and scorn with which he first regarded her
devotion to himself. He felt distinctly jealous
once when she spent an evening walking up and
down thegarden with along limbed squatter called
S.nith —instead of sitting, metaphorically speaking,
at his feet drinking in his words. He took sbme
trouble to wean her from theaforesaid Smith’s com-
pany, and of course succeeded. There was a seat
under one of the pepper-trees, and the arching
branches screened them from the sight of Jack
and Smith on the verandah, and he was distinctly
flattered when the girl allowed him to kiss her
there —in fact she unequivocally encouraged him
to do so. Then Donald King, who had passed un-
scathed through far more dangerous ambuscades
than this one, fell promptly and very completely
in love, and gloried in the fact. Miss Broughton
had decidedly thrown herself at his head. A
Man is always credited with rejecting the fruit
which falls at his feet—but o.i the whole one may
doubt this, given that the fruit be the best of its
kind on the tree, some men are contented and
sensible enough to appreciate their good fortune.
At any rate Donald King did not cavil at his, and
all night long he dreamed of the Hoya Gully, to
which the girl had suggested a ride next day, and
of how he would confess his love to her there, for
Jack and the long-limbed Smith had rather
interrupted proceedings under the pepper-tree by
strolling past and inviting him for a moonligh'
swim.
But there was no one to interrupt them as they
rode over the grass tussocks next morning to
where the tops of the trees in Hoya Gully made a
fringe of green against the sun-dried downs. On
their way they passed a bush grave roughly
fenced, so that the Wirrowah cattle should not
trample it level, and Francie paused a moment to
fling a po3y of blue-bells she had gathered on to it.
“ Who lies there ? ” said King, his eyes on the
grand lines of the girl's figure as she stooped to
push the posy into place with her whip.
“ Oh, a friend of mine,” she said rather
evasively .; “the greatest one I ever had, I think.
She was very pretty and very foolish—just died
because she couldn’t get what she wanted; but
her eyes were just like those blue-bells. Come
on, Mr, King, one gets a good view of the gully
from here.”
They paced their horses a few yards further,
and the beautiful rift spread before them, un-
changed. and lonely as of old. The girl slipped to
the ground, and he hitched the horses to the
fence round the grave, and together they
descended into the green depths, led by the
music of the falling waters. The man’s heart
throbbed with excitement, for he was going to
kiss her again in a moment, and, stretching his
arms, he drew her to him. “Frances,” he whis-
pered, “Frances, darling .”
“Frances, darling,” took the caress with equa-
nimity, and lisrened attentively to her lover’s tale;
then she slipped from his arms into the billowy
sweep of maiden-hair, and, leaning over the pool,
gazed at the reflection of her face in the water.
“ You are so beautiful, sweetheart,” he said.
“ Yes,” she answered, gravely. “ I really think
I’m as good-looking for a girl as you ivere as a
man, for you are not quite so handsome now as
you were in the days when you made love to Mary
Beattie here—do you remember?”
“No,” he said, boldly, “I don’t want to remem-
ber that boyish foolishness. I’m a man now,
Frances, and I have a man’s love to give you.”
“ She was only seventeen then, but she }iad a
woman’s love to give you,” said the girl quietly.
“It lasted all her life long,” she added.
“Nonsense,” he laughed; “she has married
her teamster and has forgotten long ago—if she
ever gave it a second thought.”
“She married her teamster,” assented the girl,
“ but she always remembered.”
King smiled a little consciously. Even this
statement wasn’t altogether unpalatable to his
vanity. “Come,” he said, holding out his arms
to her, “I love you, and you love me, sweetheart;
don’t waste time with these old memories of
things that left no more impression on my heart
than your lovely face has left on those clear
waters.” And he bent to kiss her again, but the
girl sprang to her feet and, gathering up her
habit, said:
“No, no; I’ve had enough kissing. I really
don’t think I’d like to marry you at all.” And in
a moment she was half-way up the natural rock
steps which led to the Gully, laughing mis-
chievously as if she were the Imp of old. “ Come
on,” she called, “I’m going home.”
“Francie,” he said, “do you mean to tell me
that you have had no love for me at all—that you
have been playing with me ? ”
“Yes; just playing with you—just as you
played v. ith Mary Beattie, my girl friend of years
ago. She was older than I was, but she told me
all her hopes and fears, and I came that day to
hear her reward your devotion by telling you she
would give up the other man for your sake. But
I’m more honest with you than you were to her,
because I don’t carry on the game any longer than
I can help—l stop when I’m tired, I mean.”
“But you showed so plainly,” King began,
anger coming to the help of his wounded feelings,
“ Well, I suppose I did ; and, if it is a.iy con
solation to you to know it, I rather enjoyed the
experiment. I wanted you to care for me just
because I wanted you, do you understand ? It
wasn’t altogether out of revenge for what you did
to Mary, but (frankly) I’m tired, really and truly
horribly tired and bored with it all.” And the
Imp’s hard grey eyes looked out at him through
the softening fringe of lashes, their mischievous
gleam revealing far more plainly than did her
words the truth of her assertion. She was tired
of him. And as she stood there, on the brink of
the rift, he realised how much he was losing, for
apart from her beauty, the girl owned the won-
derful physical charm which dowers not even one
woman in every hundred, and which is stronger
than iron bands to hold the desire of man. _ She
saw the longing in his eyes, and laughed again as
she mounted her horse, using a gigantic grass
tussock as a block.
“ Don’t dare to come near me ! ” she said, as he
moved towards her mechanically. “You can’t
blame me; I’ve done no more to you than you
did to tier,” pointing to the grave on which the
blue-bells were already wilting, “ten years ago in
Hoya Gully.” And the echo of her laughter came
back to him as he watched her ride away. Then
he turned to look at the nameless grave in the
grass before him, and a strange thought came to
him, born of the memory of a sweet, tearful face
he had kissed at parting, in Hoya Gully, on
that bygone Spring morning.
“ She had forgiven me long ago,” he whispered.
“ She will only pity now.” And no doubt he was
right in his surmise, for most women forgive
easily—and have much to forgive, as a rule,
in this world.

The Light of Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Turn down the stars and hide the moon—Love’s
Light shines over all;
And tree-tops bend and softly croon beneath the
glad ray-fall!
When night is starred with girls' eyes bright, no
need for candle-glow—
The world is lit with Love’s true light, turn lamp
and burner low.
The sweet Love-light effulgent gleams, and earth
is lit enough
When from soft eyes there gently streams the
shining Light of Love !
The dead man’s deep and dreamy tomb is robbed
of dark and shade
By eyes—that haunt the narrow room—of some
remembered maid.
From pole to pole the earth is starred all night
with passion-pyres,
That burn with all the warmth of sard and opals
and sapphires 1
No corner where the golden bars of Love-light do
not fall—
Turn down the moon and hide the stars, Love’s
Lamp shines over all.

The Dog—as a Sportsman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sheep-dog and the cattle-dog are the work-
men of the animal kingdom ; sporting and fight-
ing dogs are the professionals and artists.
A house-dog or a working-dog will only work
for his own master ; but a professional or artistic
dog will work for anybody, so long as he is treated
like an artist. A man going away for a week’s
shooting can borrow a dog, and the dog will work
for him loyally, just as a good musician will do
his best, though the conductor is strange to him,
and the other members of the band are not up to
the mark. The musician’s art is sacred to him,
and that is the case with the dog—Art before
everything.
It is a grand sight to see a really good setter
or pointer working up to a bird, occasionally
glancing over his shoulder to see that the man
with the gun has not lost himself. How he
throws his whole soul into his work, questing
carefully over the cold scent, and feathering
eagerly where the bird is close, and at last draw-
ing up like a statue. Not Paganini himself ever
more thoroughly lost himself in his art than does
the humble Spot or Ponto. He is rapt, ecstasied,
carried away. It is not amusement and not a
mere duty to him; it is a sacred gift, which he is
bound to exercise. A pointer in need of amuse-
ment will play with another dog—the pair pre-
tending to fight, and so on, but when there is work
to be done, then the dog is lost in the artist. How
crestfallen he looks if by any chance he blunders
on to a bird without pointing it 1 A fiddler who
has played a wrong note in a solo is the only crea-
ture who can look as discomfited. Humanity,
instead of going to the ant of the parable for
wisdom, should certainly go to the dog.
• • • •
Sporting dogs are like other artists, in that
they are apt to get careless of everything
except their vocation. No sporting dog is
ever reliable in his affections—nor is any human
artist, either, for that matter. They are not good
watch dogs, and take little interest in chasing
cats. They look on a little dog that catches rats
much as a great musician looks on a cricketer—it’s
clever, but it isn’t Art.
Hunting and fighting dogs are the gladiators of
the animal world. A fox-hound or a kangaroo-
dog is always of the same opinion as Mr.
Jorrocks:—“ All time is wasted what isn’t SDent
in ’untin’.” A greyhound will start out in* the
morning with three lame legs, but as soon as he
sees a hare start he must go. He utterly forgets
his sorrows in the excitement, just as a rowing-
man, all over boils and blisters, will pull a
desperate race without feeling any pain. Such
dogs are not easily excited by anything but a
chase, and a burglar might come and rob the
house and murder the inmates without arousing
any excitement among these athletic canines.
Guarding a house is ‘ ‘ not their pidgin’, ” as the
Chinese say ; that is one great reason for the
success of the dog at whatever branch of his
tribe’s work he goes in for—he is so very thorough.
Dogs who are forced to combine half-a-dozen pro-
fessions never make a success at anything. One
dog one billet is their motto.
* • * •
The most earnest and thorough of all the dog
tribe is the fighting dog. His intense self-respect,
his horror of brawling, his cool determination,
make him a pattern to humanity. The bulldog
or bull-terrier is generally the most friendly and
best-tempered dog in the world ; but when he is
put down in the ring he fights till he drops, fights
in grim silence though his feet are bitten through
and through, his ears are in rags, and his neck a
hideous mass of wounds. In a well-conducted
dog-fight each dog in turn has to attack the other
dog, and one can see the fierce earnestness blazing
in the eye of the attacker as he hurls himself
on to the foe. What makes him fight like
that? It is not bloodthirstiness, because they
are neither savage nor quarrelsome dogs: a
bulldog will go all his life without a fight,
unless put into a ring. It is simply their
strong self-respect, their stubborn pride which
will not let them give in. The greyhound snaps
once at his opponent and then runs for his life
but the fighting dog stands to it till death. Just
occasionally one sees the same type of human
being—generally some quiet-spoken, good-tem-
pered man who has taken up glove-fighting for a
living, and who, perhaps, gets pitted against a
man a shade better than himself. After a few
rounds he knows h 6 1S over-matched, but there is
something at the back of his brain that will not
let him cave in. Round after round he stands
punishment, and round after round he grimly
comes up, till, possibly, his opponent loses heart,
or a fluke hit turns the scale in his favor These
men are to be found in every class of life—many
of the garaest of the game are mere gutter-bred
boys who will continue to fight long after they
have endured enough punishment to entitle them
to quit. You can see in their eyes the same hard
glitter that shows in the bulldog’s eyes as he
limps across the ring, or in the eyes of the race-
horse as he lies down to it when his opponent is
outpacing him. It is grit, pluck, vim, nerve force:
call it what you like, and there is no created thing
that has more of it than the dog.
There w another phase of dog that has never
teen quite understood—the occasional longing
that comes over dogs to get into mischief. Every
station-owner knows that sometimes the house-
dof?s ~ no °, f what breed—are liable to take
wiKl? f 6 ° f s £? e P- kill ing. Any kind of dog
i i*’ * r 9 m c °bies downwards. They are verv
artful about it, too. They lie round the house till
dark, and then slink off and have a wild night’s
blood-spree, running down the wretched sheep
and tearing their throats open, and then, before
dawn, they slink back again and lie down around
the house as before. Sometimes dogs from different
homesteads meet in the paddocks, having
apparently arranged the whole affair beforehand!
Even httle dogs, like fox-terriers and skye-terriers
will have an occasional fit of murder. Many and
many a sheep-owner has gone out with a gun and
sliot his neighbor’s dogs for killing his sheep,
which his own wicked, innocent-looking dogs had
slam, it by any chance one happens to meet
dogs while they are on these little excursions,
they show at once that they know the game is up.
I hoy sneak away like dingoes, and then go for
home as hard as they can run. Some dogs will
not kill sheep on their own run, but always visit
neighbors paddocks. In civilised parts, where
there are no sheep to kill, the dog sometimes
takes to fowl-killing, by way of diversion.
, j ni by experience, and the experience is
banded down from generation to generation. In
districts where dingoes are bad it is easy to poison
them at first; but, when poisoning has been going

* * * m
So much then for the dog whose courage, con-
centration and earnest attention to his business or
profession make him a pattern to humanity. No
man can equal a dog in any of these characteris-
tics. it will be seen that in this article there is
no reference to the dog with the tenor voice, who
howls in suburban back-yards, or the big swag-
genng dog, who lies fragrantly in the sun before
the doors of town houses. These poor unfor-
tunates have missed their vocation. The real dog
is a workman, and should be respected as such.
THE BANJO
‘ President Roosevelt has sent a watch and
chain and a medal to each of the chiefs of Tutuila,
the Samoan island belonging to America, who
ceded their rights of sovereignty to the United
States. (Gable Item.) What humor ! And how
like a king !
The mendacious Philp Government has
done its usual back-down over the deporting of
its beloved Kanaka. Owing to the fearful
reports of what would happen when Tanna and
co. returned to their native soil, the New Cale-
donian Governor offered to take any deported
Kanaka (who feared violence) on to the French
settlement, and the Philp Govt, has been com-
pelled to say that the offer is unnecessary. The
returned Kanaka, who was going to be eaten by
his loving family and old-time friends the instant
he landed, is a defunct myth.
At Terawhiti (M.L.) there still remain the
engine-house, machinery, and tramways of what
was once a busy gold-mine. More than 20 years
ago gold was *hund in the streams in that vicinity,
and, later, prospectors found the Albion reef,
which was considered to promise payable gold.
The whole of the material for the mine was
earned .by steamer to Qterhangi Bay, and
then carted in small parcels three miles
on a bullock-dray. There are no roads
within five miles of the mine, and the country is
rough. The principal reefs were near the top of
a range, and tramways ran along the summit and
down to the battery-house. But the mine was a
duffer. At least, there were no dividends; but
there appears to have been a good deal of reckless
expenditure indulged in. The machinery just
looks as though it had stopped for a few hours.
Everything is in place, but it is one mass of steel
“ frcize-up ” with rust. And instead of the crush-
ing roar of the stamps, one hears only the creak
of a loose sheet of iron and the melancholy “ flap,
flap ” of the whare door.
Writes a man who has just finished read-
ing his 17th book on the Boer War:
I have come to the conclusion that the war was a
cynical joke on the part of the administrator for things
terrestrial. A French officer who went out to teach the
Boers wrote again and again in his diary that the British
were too slow to catch tortoises. One day they
surrounded that smart Frenchmen, who, to his credit,
preferred to die fighting- and he did. Oronje said he’d
been fighting the British for 20 years and never knew them
to leave the railway line for fear they might have to do
without condensed milk ; still, the force that mopped him
up marched nearly 400 miles on its feet, and lived mostly
on a biscuit per day. When Cronje was trapped Roberts
slung lyddite at him for a week. The first shots killed
all Cronje’s animals, which, of course, could not be got
into dug-outs ; and Roberts might as well have saved all
the rest of his lyddite and used his 70 pieces of artillery
elsewhere. It did no harm after it had killed the trans-
port animals, and Cronje never dreamed of surrendering
till they began to hum. He was not bombarded out—
merely stunk into surrender. The stink was mightier
than the sword or the lyddite, a fact worth remembering
when our own trouble comes along. The most successful
general against the Boers was the one who attacked
them least. French always resolutely refused to attack
the Boers if he could help it. There was no beastly
pride about him, and he never hankered after glory.
Dead Boers were his game and he always swung round
behind the Boers where, if they wanted food or water
they had to come out and fight him on ground of his
own choosing. Result: whenever the Boers saw French
slithering away to the side they reached for their
carpet bags and left. Every Boer or continental
writer I have read speaks badly of the Canadian
and Australian troops, stigmatising them as cut-
throats and chicken-stealers. One snorting Hollander
described the approach of the British force so:—“ At
daybreak their big ship-guns flung shots at us from
seven miles away. They did no harm. We had just
finished breakfast when a mounted force appeared.
They were the farm-robbers under the Baron French. At
the first shot they scattered, swung to the east and
galloped to a line of kopjes which our force was too small
to occupy, and which overlooked the dorp (village).
These ruffians had not the slightest intention of attacking
our position as soldiers should; they merely waited
pasturing their horses in the long grass and training a
galloping gun on our water-fountain. A little later we
saw parties of them had entered the dorp, and were
chasing fowls, killing pigs and taking liberties with the
unfortunate women. The sight so maddened us that the
burghers compelled the commandant to lead them
against the kopjes. Alas ! their generous rage could not
prevail against the impregnable position of these brigands
from the ends of the earth, and after many brave patriots
were slam and wounded we retired to our kop unable to
prevent the looting of the dorp. These men who come
from the American and Australian colonies are strangely
favored. They never attack defended positions and if
the burgher forces will not retire to save having their
communications cut the colonial troops are drawn aside
and pasture their horses till the foot soldiers of England

1-ves of the foot soldiers of England are apparently of no
value as they are never spared.' The courage of these

A COMMON CASE OF PILES.
IT MAY LEAD TO SERIOUS RESULTS.
When people generally understand that all
such fatal diseases as fistula, ulcer of the
rectum, fissure, &c., almost invariably begin in
a simple case of Piles, they will learn the
wisdom of taking prompt treatment for the
first appearance of trouble in this quarter.
Doan’s Ointment will certainly cure every
form of piles—itching, bleeding, protruding,
and blind piles—and hundreds of lives ha,ve
been saved by using this cheap but effective
remedy right at the start, because at such
times a single pot will effect a cure, while in
the old deep-seated, chronic cases several pots
are sometimes necessary before a cure is
effected. Here is a casein point :
Mr. William Gilliver, of Gilliver and Curtis,
General Contractors, whose private address is
Avoca, Bankstown, writes us, 10th December,
1900:
“I wrote you February 14, 1899, advising
you that Doan’s Ointment had cured me of
itching piles, after twenty-two years’ affliction.
I had let six months go by before I wrote that
letter, as I wished to see whether the cure was
a permanent one. I had used many remedies
ia these twenty-two years’ trouble, and could
scarcely believe that only half a pot of oint-
ment could work such a cure. Since I wrote
that last letter I have had more lasting proof
of the durability of the good this preparation
has done me. I have not had occasion to use
the ointment since. It was a permanent cure.
Many people wrote to me about the value of
Doan’s Ointment, and I advised them to give
it a trial, and then let me know the results.
In consequence, I have received many letters
telling me of cures effected by Doan’s Oint-
ment.”
